The Syllogism
intermediate
12 min
Ibn Taymiyya's Refutation of the Two-Part Proof Restriction
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the logicians' restriction that any valid proof has exactly two premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the logicians' claim that any valid proof (dalil) must consist of exactly two premises.

Core Idea
A proof can be a single word or many; the two-part rule is an arbitrary restriction, not a logical necessity.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya attacks a foundational rule of Aristotelian logic: that every syllogism must have exactly two premises. Using examples from fiqh and Arabic grammar, he shows that a valid proof can be expressed with one word, two words, or many, depending on the context. The restriction to two is a groundless imposition—a 'taḥakkum' (arbitrariness). This lesson unpacks his argument and its implications for how we evaluate proofs in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
What exactly are the logicians saying about the number of parts in a proof? Find it in the text: they claim the required (al-matloob) is not more than two parts, meaning any valid proof consists of exactly two premises.
2
Follow Ibn Taymiyya's counterexample from fiqh
Consider the question 'Is nabidh haram?' answered with 'Yes'. This single word suffices as a proof, showing that a two-premise structure is not always needed.
The example uses the Islamic legal context of nabidh (date wine).
3
Examine his grammatical argument using inna/anna
Ibn Taymiyya uses the distinction between 'inna' (complete sentence) and 'anna' (singular masdar) to show that a proof can be expressed as a single grammatically singular expression, not necessarily a full proposition.
This demonstrates his use of Arabic grammar (nahw) to challenge logical rules.
4
Assess the charge of arbitrariness (taḥakkum)
Ibn Taymiyya claims that specifying two parts is 'pure arbitrariness'. He argues that one could just as easily specify one part as the foundation, since the logicians themselves admit a single proposition can be equivalent to multiple propositions.
He points out the inconsistency in the logicians' own framework.
5
Synthesise Ibn Taymiyya's alternative
His alternative: the required (matloob) and its proof can be one, two, or many, depending on the seeker and context. The word 'dalil' is singular and covers all forms, so no artificial limit should be imposed.
This aligns with the flexibility of Islamic legal reasoning.

Source Text

المقام الثالث - إبطال قولهم : ليس المطلوب أكثر من جزئين وأمثال ذلك من القيود التي يسميها النحاةُ الصفاتِ والعَطْفَ، والأحوال، وظرف المكان، وظرف الزمان، ونحو ذلك . فإذا كانت القضية مقيدة بقيود كثيرة لم تكن مؤلفة من لفظين، بل من ألفاظ متعددة ومعان متعددة . وإن أريد أن المطلوب ليس إلا معنيان - سواء عَبَّر عنهما بلفظين أو ألفاظ متعددة قيل وليس الأمر كذلك بل قد يكون المطلوب معنى واحداً، وقد يكون معنيان وقد يكون معاني متعددة. فإن المطلوب بحسب طلب الطالب، وهو الناظر المستدلّ، أو السائل المتعلم المناظر ، وكل منهما قد يطلب معنى واحداً، وقد يطلب معنيين، وقد يطلب معاني . والعبارة عن مطلوبه قد تكون بلفظ واحد، وقد تكون بلفظين، وقد تكون بأكثر . فإذا قال النبيذ حرام؟»، قيل له : «نعم»، كان هذا اللفظ وحده كافياً في جوابه، كما لو قيل له : هو حرام». فإن قالوا : القضية الواحدة قد تكون في تقدير قضايا كما ذكرتموه من التمثيل قولهم: بـ «الإنسان فإن هذه القضية الواحدة في تقدير خمس قضايا، وهي خمس مطالب والتقدير : هل هو جسم أم لا؟ وهل هو حساس أم لا؟ وهل هو نام أم لا؟ وهل هو متحرك بالإرادة أم لا ؟ وهل هو ناطق أم لا ؟ وكذلك فيما تقدم : هل النبيذ حرام فهل تحریمه بالنص أو بالقياس؟ فيقال: إذا رضيتم بمثل هذا وهو أن تجعلوا الواحد في تقدير عدد، فالمفرد قد يكون في معنى قضية. فإذا قال: «النبيذ المسكر حرام؟»، فقال المجيب: «نعم»، فلفظ «نعم» في تقدير قوله : «هو حرام» . وإذا قال: «ما الدليل عليه؟ ، فقال : الدليل عليه تحريم كل مسكر»، أو «أن كل مسكر حرام، أو قول النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم كل مسكر حرام)، ونحو ذلك من العبارات التي جَعَل الدليل فيها اسماً مفرداً، وهو جزء واحد، لم يجعله قضية مؤلفة من اسمين - مبتدأ وخبر. فإن قوله : تحريم كل مسكر اسم مضاف . وقوله «أن كل مسكر حرام" - بالفتح - مفرد أيضاً. فإن «أَنَّ» وما في حيزها في تقدير المصدر المفرد ) و (إن) المكسورة وما في حيزها جملة تامة. ولهذا قال النحاة قاطبة إِنَّ إِنَّ» تكسر إذا كانت في موضع الجملة ـ والجملة خبر وقضية ـ وتفتح في موضع المفرد الذي هو جزء القضية . ولهذا يكسرونها بعد القول»، لأنهم إنما يحكون بـ «القول» الجملة التامة . وكذلك إذا قلت : الدليل عليه قول النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم، أو الدليل عليه النص»، أو إجماع الصحابة، أو الدليل عليه الآية الفلانية»، أو الحديث الفلاني، أو الدليل عليه قيام المقتضي للتحريم، السالم عن المعارض المقاوم»، أو الدليل عليه أنه مشارك لخمر العنب فيما يستلزم التحريم»، وأمثال ذلك مما يُعبَّر فيه عن الدليل بـ اسم مفرد ، لا بـ «القضية) التي هي جملة تامة. ثم هذا الدليل الذي عُبّر عنه باسم مفرد هو إذا فصل عُبّر عنه بألفاظ متعدّدة. والمقصود أن قولكم : إن الدليل الذي هو القياس لا يكون إلا جزئين فقط»، إن أردتم «الفين فقط»، وأن ما زاد على لفظين فهو أدلة، لا دليل واحد، لأن ذلك اللفظ الموصوف بصفات تحتاج كل صفة إلى دليل قيل لكم وكذلك يمكن أن يقال في اللفظين هما دليلان لا دليل واحد، فإن كل مقدمة تحتاج إلى دليل . تخصيص عدد وحينئذ فتخصيص العدد باثنين دون ما زاد تحكّم لا معنى له. فإنه إذا كان المقصود قد يحصل بلفظ مفرد، وقد لا يحصل إلا بلفظين، وقد لا يحصل إلا بثلاثة، وأربعة وأكثر . فَجعلُ الجاعل اللفظين هما الأصل الواجب دون ما زاد وما نقص، تحكّم محض الزائد إن كان في المطلوب جُعل مطالب متعدّدة، وإن كان في الدليل تذكر مقدمات جعل ذلك في تقدير أقيسة متعدّدة : تحكّم محض ليس هو أولى من أن يقال : «بل الأصل في المطلوب أن يكون واحداً، ودليله جزء واحد. فإذا زاد المطلوب على ذلك جعل مطلوبين، أو ثلاثة أو أربعة بحسب زيادته ؛ وجُعل الدليل دليلين، أو ثلاثة، أو أربعة بحسب دلالته». وهذا إذا قيل فهو أحسن من قولهم لأن اسم «الدليل» مفرد، و «القياس» هو «الدليل».
The third station - Refutation of their saying: 'The required is not more than two parts.' And similar to that are the restrictions that grammarians call adjectives, conjunctions, circumstantial qualifiers, adverbs of place, adverbs of time, and the like. So if a proposition is restricted by many restrictions, it does not consist of two words, but of multiple words and multiple meanings. And if it is meant that the required is only two meanings - whether they are expressed by two words or many words - it is said: That is not the case; rather, the required may be one meaning, may be two meanings, and may be multiple meanings. For the required depends on the request of the seeker, who is the observing inferrer, or the questioning learner disputant; and each of them may request one meaning, may request two meanings, and may request multiple meanings. The expression of his required may be with one word, may be with two words, and may be with more. So if he asks, 'Is nabidh haram?' and he is told, 'Yes,' this single word is sufficient in his answer, as if he were told, 'It is haram.' So if they say: The single proposition may be equivalent to multiple propositions, as in the example you mentioned: 'insan', for this single proposition is equivalent to five propositions, which are five demands: Is it a body or not? Is it sensitive or not? Is it growing or not? Is it moving by will or not? Is it rational or not? And likewise in what preceded: Is nabidh haram? Then is its prohibition by text or by analogy? It is said: If you accept such - that you make the one equivalent to a number - then the singular may be in the meaning of a proposition. So if he says, 'Is intoxicating nabidh haram?' and the respondent says, 'Yes,' then the word 'yes' is equivalent to his saying, 'It is haram.' And if he says, 'What is the proof of it?' and he says, 'The proof of it is the prohibition of every intoxicant,' or 'That every intoxicant is haram,' or the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him, 'Every intoxicant is haram,' and the like of those expressions in which he made the proof a singular noun, which is one part, and did not make it a proposition composed of two nouns - subject and predicate. For his saying, 'prohibition of every intoxicant' is a genitive phrase. And his saying, 'That every intoxicant is haram' - with fatha - is also singular. For 'anna' and what is in its scope is equivalent to a singular masdar, while 'inna' with kasra and what is in its scope is a complete sentence. For this reason, all grammarians say that 'inna' is given kasra when it is in the position of a sentence - and the sentence is a predicate and a proposition - and it is given fatha in the position of a singular that is part of a proposition. And for this reason they give it kasra after 'qawl' because they only quote with 'qawl' a complete sentence. Likewise, if you say, 'The proof of it is the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him,' or 'the proof of it is the text,' or 'the consensus of the Companions,' or 'the proof of it is such-and-such verse,' or 'such-and-such hadith,' or 'the proof of it is the existence of the coefficient for prohibition, free from a resilient counterevidence,' or 'the proof of it is that it shares with grape wine in what necessitates prohibition,' and the like of that which is expressed for the proof by a singular noun, not by a proposition that is a complete sentence. Then this proof, which is expressed by a singular noun, when it is broken down, is expressed by multiple words. And the point is that your saying, 'The proof, which is the syllogism, consists only of two parts' - if you mean 'only two words,' and that what exceeds two words is multiple proofs, not one proof, because that word described by attributes, each attribute requires a proof - it is said to you: Likewise, it can be said of the two words that they are two proofs, not one proof, for each premise requires a proof. Then specifying the number as two without what exceeds is a groundless arbitrariness. For if the intended goal may be achieved by a singular word, may not be achieved except by two words, may not be achieved except by three, four, or more. So the one who makes the two words the necessary foundation without what exceeds or falls short, a pure arbitrariness: if the excess is in the required, he makes it multiple demands; and if it is in the proof, he mentions premises, making that equivalent to multiple syllogisms - a pure arbitrariness, not more appropriate than saying: 'Rather, the foundation is that the required should be one, and its proof one part. So if the required exceeds that, it is made two demands, or three, or four according to its increase; and the proof is made two proofs, or three, or four according to its implication.' And this if said is better than their saying because the word 'dalil' is singular, and 'qiyas' is the same as 'dalil'.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the logicians' claim that any valid proof (al-dalil) or syllogism (al-qiyas) must consist of exactly two parts (i.e., two premises). Ibn Taymiyya begins by noting that propositions can have many qualifiers, so they are not limited to two words. He then argues that the required (al-matloob) depends on the seeker and can be one meaning, two meanings, or more, and its expression can be with one word, two words, or many. He gives the example of someone asking 'Is nabidh haram?' and being answered with a single word 'Yes'—this is sufficient. The logicians counter that a single proposition like 'human' can be equivalent to multiple propositions (body? sensitive? etc.). Ibn Taymiyya turns this against them: if they allow one to be equivalent to many, then a single word like 'yes' can be equivalent to a full proposition. He then shows how proofs are often expressed with a single word (e.g., 'prohibition of every intoxicant'), which is a singular noun, not a complete sentence. He uses grammatical distinctions: 'inna' with kasra is a complete sentence, while 'anna' with fatha is a singular masdar equivalent. So the proof can be a single grammatically singular expression. He concludes that the restriction to two parts is arbitrary: if they say more than two words means multiple proofs, then two words could also be seen as two proofs since each premise needs proof. The better approach is to say that the required and its proof can be one, two, or many, and 'dalil' is a singular term that covers all. This passage demonstrates Ibn Taymiyya's method of attacking the foundational restrictions of Greek logic by showing their arbitrariness through concrete examples from Islamic legal reasoning and Arabic grammar.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what is the problem with the logicians' claim that a valid proof must have exactly two parts?
A) The proof must always have three parts because of the three terms in a syllogism.
B) The restriction to two parts is arbitrary because proofs can be expressed with one word or many, and the required can be one, two, or more meanings.
C) He agrees that two parts are necessary but insists that they must be explicit statements.
D) He argues that proofs always have four parts because of the Aristotelian categories.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya rejects the arbitrary limitation that proofs must have two premises, arguing that valid inference can be expressed with one word or many, depending on the context and the seeker's need.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Ibn Taymiyya says the required (matloob) can be one meaning, two, or many. Think of a question in fiqh (e.g., 'Is gambling haram?') and state how the required could be expressed as a single-word answer, a two-word proposition, or a multi-premise argument. Give one example for each.
?
Task 2: Using the grammatical argument (inna vs anna), identify whether the following expression is a complete proposition (qadiyya) or a singular proof (mufrad): 'That every intoxicant is haram' (أَنَّ كُلَّ مُسْكِرٍ حَرَامٌ). Explain why, based on the passage.

The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
Arbitrariness of the Two-Premise Limit
Critique of the two-premise limit as arbitrary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument against the logicians' two-premise requirement and identify why the specification is arbitrary.

Core Idea
Logicians' two-premise rule is arbitrary when a single term or multiple premises can serve as evidence.

Ibn Taymiyya launches a powerful critique against the logicians' claim that a valid syllogism must consist of exactly two premises. He argues that this restriction is not based on any rational necessity but is a mere convention. In this lesson, you will dissect his reasoning and see how he exposes the arbitrariness (tahakkum) of the two-premise limit, using examples from legal theory to illustrate his point.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
Locate where Ibn Taymiyya states the logicians' position: that evidence (dalil) which is the syllogism (qiyas) can only be two parts (i.e., two premises).
The logicians argue that any expression described by multiple attributes needs separate evidence for each attribute, so beyond two premises you have multiple evidences.
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's counter-argument
Find his response that even two premises can be considered two evidences, not one, since each premise itself requires evidence. This shows the specification is arbitrary.
He uses a tu quoque-style argument: if you require separate evidence for each attribute, then by the same logic, each premise is an evidence.
3
Analyze the variability of evidence
Notice his observation that the required meaning (al-maqsud) can be obtained by a single expression, two expressions, three, or more. Thus fixing on two is arbitrary.
He gives the example of a single term being sufficient sometimes, showing that evidence is not bound to a fixed number.
4
Examine his alternative proposal
He suggests that if we must define evidence by number, we should start from one: the required can be one, and its evidence one part. If the required is multiple, then evidence becomes multiple accordingly.
This alternative is more natural because the word 'dalil' is singular in form and meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمقصود أن قولكم: إن الدليل الذي هو القياس لا يكون إلا جزئين فقط، إن أردتم اثنين فقط، وأن ما زاد على لفظين فهو أدلة لا دليل واحد، لأن ذلك اللفظ الموصوف بصفات تحتاج كل صفة إلى دليل، قيل لكم: وكذلك يمكن أن يقال في اللفظين: هما دليلان لا دليل واحد، فإن كل مقدمة تحتاج إلى دليل. وحينئذ فتخصيص العدد باثنين دون ما زاد تحكّم لا معنى له. فإنه إذا كان المقصود قد يحصل بلفظ مفرد، وقد لا يحصل إلا بلفظين، وقد لا يحصل إلا بثلاثة وأربعة وأكثر. فجعل الجاعل اللفظين هما الأصل الواجب دون ما زاد وما نقص، وأن تحكم محض: الزائد إن كان في المطلوب جُعل مطالب متعدّدة، وإن كان في الدليل تذكر مقدمات جعل الأصل في ذلك في تقدير أقيسة متعدّدة: تحكّم محض ليس هو أولى من أن يقال: بل الأصل في المطلوب أن يكون واحداً، ودليله جزء واحد. فإذا زاد المطلوب على ذلك جعل مطلوبين، أو ثلاثة أو أربعة بحسب زيادته؛ وجُعل الدليل دليلين، أو ثلاثة، أو أربعة بحسب دلالته. وهذا إذا قيل فهو أحسن من قولهم لأن اسم الدليل مفرد، ومعناه مفرد ولفظ القياس يقتضي التقدير، كما يقال: قِسْت هذا بهذا. والتقدير يحصل أصل التقدير بواحد، كما يحصل باثنين وبثلاثة. فأصل التقدير بواحد، وإذا قُدّر باثنين أو ثلاثة بواحد يكون تقديرين وثلاثة لا تقديراً واحداً، فتكون تلك التقديرات أقيسة لا قياساً واحداً. فجعلهم ما زاد على الاثنتين من المقدمات في معنى أقيسة متعددة، وما نقص عن الاثنتين نصف قياس لا قياس تام، اصطلاح محض لا يرجع إلى معنى معقول، كما فرقوا بين الصفات الذاتية والعرضية اللازمة للماهية والوجود بمثل هذا التحكم. وحينئذ فيعلم أن القوم لم يرجعوا فيما سموه حداً وبرهانا إلى حقيقة موجودة، ولا إلى أمر معقول، بل إلى اصطلاح مجرّد، كتنازع الناس في العلة، هل هي اسم لما يستلزم المعلول بحيث لا يتخلف عنها بحال، فلا يقبل النقيض والتخصيص، أو هو اسم لما يكون مقتضياً للمعلول وقد يتخلف عنه المعلول لفوات شرط أو وجود مانع؟ وكاصطلاح بعض أهل النظر والجدل في تسمية أحدهم الدليل لما هو مستلزم للمدلول مطلقاً حتى يدخل في ذلك عدم المعارض، والآخر يسمى الدليل لما كان شأنه أن يستلزم المدلول، وإنما يتخلف استلزامه لفوات شرط أو وجود مانع؛ وتنازع أهل الجدل هل على المستدل أن يتعرض في ذكر الدليل لتبيين المعارض جملة أو تفصيلاً حيث يمكن التفصيل، أو لا يتعرض لتبيينه لا جملة ولا تفصيلاً، أو يتعرض لتبيينه جملة لا تفصيلاً؟ وهذه أمور وضعية اصطلاحية بمنزلة الألفاظ التي يصطلح عليها الناس للتعبير عما في أنفسهم، وبمنزلة ما يعتاده الناس في بعض الأفعال لكونهم رأوا ذلك أولى بهم من غيره، وإن كان غيره أولى بغيرهم منه؛ ليست حقائق ثابتة في أنفسها لأمور معقولة تتفق فيها الأمم، كما يدعيه هؤلاء في منطقهم. بل هؤلاء الذين يجعلون العلة والدليل يراد به هذا أو هذا أقرب إلى المعقول من جعل هؤلاء الدليل لا يكون إلا من مقدمتين. فإن هذا تخصيص لعدد دون عدد بلا
The intended meaning is that your saying: 'The evidence which is the syllogism cannot be except only two parts,' if you mean 'only two,' and that what exceeds two expressions is multiple evidences, not a single evidence, because that expression described by attributes each needing evidence, it is said to you: Likewise it can be said about the two expressions: they are two evidences, not one evidence, for each premise needs evidence. Then specifying the number to two without what exceeds is arbitrary with no meaning. For if the objective may be obtained by a single expression, and may not be obtained except by two expressions, and may not be obtained except by three, four, or more. Then the maker's making the two expressions the obligatory foundation without what exceeds and what falls short is sheer arbitrariness: the extra if it is in the required [conclusion] he makes multiple requirements, and if it is in the evidence he mentions premises, he makes the foundation in that as multiple syllogisms: sheer arbitrariness, not more valid than it being said: 'Rather the foundation is that the required be one, and its evidence one part. If the required exceeds that, it is made two requirements, or three, or four according to the increase; and the evidence is made two, or three, or four according to its indication.' If this is said, it is better than their saying because the noun 'evidence' is singular, its meaning is singular, and the word 'syllogism' implies measurement, as it is said: 'I measured this with this.' And measurement obtains the origin of measurement by one, just as it obtains by two and by three. So the origin of measurement is by one, and if it is measured by two or three by one, they become two measurements and three, not one measurement; so those measurements become syllogisms, not one syllogism. Their making what exceeds two of the premises as multiple syllogisms, and what falls short of two as half a syllogism not a complete syllogism, is pure convention not returning to a rational meaning, just as they differentiated between essential and accidental attributes necessary to essence and existence by such arbitrariness. Then it is known that the people [logicians] did not rely in what they called 'definition' and 'demonstration' on an existing reality, nor on a rational matter, but on pure convention, like people's dispute about 'cause': is it a name for what entails the effect such that it never lags behind it, not accepting negation and specification? Or is it a name for what is a requisite for the effect and the effect may lag behind it due to absence of a condition or presence of an obstacle? And like the convention of some people of theoreticians and debaters in one of them calling 'evidence' for what is absolutely entailing the indicated, so that the absence of a counter-argument enters into that, and the other calls 'evidence' for what is such that it entails the indicated, but its entailment lags only due to absence of a condition or presence of an obstacle; and the dispute of the debaters: is it upon the one adducing evidence to undertake in mentioning the evidence to explain the counter-argument summarily or in detail where detail is possible, or not to undertake to explain it either summarily or in detail, or to undertake to explain it summarily not in detail? These are postulatory conventional matters like the expressions that people agree upon for expressing what is in their souls, and like what people become accustomed to in some actions because they saw that more appropriate for them than others, even if others are more appropriate for others besides them; they are not fixed realities in themselves for rational matters on which nations agree, as these [logicians] claim in their logic. Rather, those who make 'cause' and 'evidence' intended as this or that are closer to reason than making these people [say] 'evidence is only from two premises.' For this is a specification of a number without number with no
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument by Ibn Taymiyya against the logicians' claim that a valid syllogism (qiyas) must consist of exactly two premises, and that anything beyond that constitutes multiple syllogisms. He begins by pointing out that if they argue that an expression described by multiple attributes needs separate evidence for each attribute, then even two premises would be two evidences, not one. Therefore, specifying two as the only acceptable number is arbitrary. He then illustrates that a single term can sometimes convey the required meaning, sometimes two terms are needed, and sometimes three or more. The logicians' insistence on two premises is a mere convention (istilah) without rational basis. He draws an analogy to their arbitrary distinction between essential and accidental attributes. He extends this critique to the terms 'cause' ('illa) and 'evidence' (dalil), noting that scholars disagree on whether they require absolute entailment or allow for impediments. These are also conventional, not fixed realities. He concludes that the logicians' system is based on convention, not on universal rational truths, and that other conventions (like allowing a single term as evidence) are equally or more reasonable. The passage uses examples from legal theory (e.g., dispute about the definition of 'cause' in usul al-fiqh) to illustrate the conventional nature of such terms.

The logicians' two-premise requirement for syllogisms is an arbitrary convention, not a rational necessity, as evidence can be a single term or multiple premises depending on the need.

According to the logicians' two-premise rule, it is one syllogism. Now add a third premise: 'Nabidh is khamr.' Using the same rule, the argument becomes two syllogisms. Conventional Nature of Logical Terms
Critique of logical terms as mere conventions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish Ibn Taymiyya's view that terms like 'cause' ('illa) and 'evidence' (dalil) are conventions (istilah) from the logicians' claim that they reflect universal rational realities.

Core Idea
Logical terms like 'cause' and 'evidence' are conventions, not fixed universals.

Ibn Taymiyya extends his critique beyond the two-premise limit to argue that the very terms used in logic—such as 'cause' ('illa), 'evidence' (dalil), and even 'definition' and 'demonstration'—are mere conventions (istilah). He compares them to linguistic expressions that people agree upon for communication. In this lesson, you will analyze how he uses examples from usul al-fiqh and jadal to show that the meanings of these terms are not universally fixed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key term 'istilah'
Find where Ibn Taymiyya states that the logicians' distinctions (e.g., between essential and accidental attributes) are 'pure convention' (istilah mahd) without rational meaning.
He uses the same phrase for the two-premise rule and for the differentiation of attributes.
2
Examine the example of 'cause' ('illa)
Locate his discussion of 'illa': people dispute whether it is a name for what entails the effect without exception, or for what is a requisite that may lag due to obstacles. This shows the term is conventional.
This dispute is well-known in usul al-fiqh: the 'illa in qiyas must be constant (mutṭarid) or can be overridden (muntasib).
3
Analyze the example of 'evidence' (dalil)
Notice that scholars differ on whether dalil must absolutely entail the indicated (even ruling out counter-arguments) or only be such that it would entail absent obstacles. This is another conventional variation.
He connects this to the jadal debate about whether the arguer must address counter-arguments in detail, summary, or not at all.
4
Understand the conclusion
Read his concluding statement that these are 'postulatory conventional matters' (umur wadi'iyya istilahiyya) like agreed-upon expressions, not 'fixed realities' (haqa'iq thabita) that nations agree on.
Ibn Taymiyya uses this to undermine the logicians' claim that their logic is universal and necessary.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of the Logicians' Definition of Qiyas
Ibn Taymiyya criticizes the logicians' arbitrary division of qiyas into one or two premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to articulate Ibn Taymiyya's linguistic critique of the logicians' requirement of exactly two premises for a qiyas and recognize arbitrary conventions in logical definitions.

Core Idea
The root meaning of qiyas (estimation) does not require exactly two premises; the logicians' definition is arbitrary.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya challenges a foundational assumption of Greek logic: that a valid syllogism (qiyas) must have exactly two premises. By examining the Arabic root of the word, he shows that estimation can be done with one, two, or three components. This opens the door to a more flexible understanding of reasoning that is grounded in language rather than convention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' definition under attack
The logicians define a complete qiyas as a syllogism consisting of exactly two premises. Find where this is implied in the passage.
2
Analyze Ibn Taymiyya's linguistic argument
He points out that 'qiyas' derives from 'taqdir' (estimation). Estimation can be performed by one, two, or three elements, not just two.
He cites the phrase 'qistu hadha bihadha' (I measured this by that) as evidence.
3
Trace the implication
If estimation by two or three yields two or three estimations respectively, then those are multiple qiyases, not one. So restricting to two premises is arbitrary.
4
Compare with the logicians' other arbitrary distinctions
Ibn Taymiyya likens this to their distinction between essential and accidental attributes, which he also considers baseless convention.
This reinforces his claim that the logicians rely on pure convention, not rational meaning.
5
Formulate Ibn Taymiyya's general principle
Logical definitions must be rooted in linguistic and rational meaning; otherwise they are arbitrary conventions.

ولفظ «القياس» يقتضي التقدير، كما يقال : قِسْت هذا بهذا». والتقدير يحصل أصل التقدير بواحد، كما يحصل باثنين وبثلاثة . فأصل التقدير بواحد وإذا قُدّر باثنين أو ثلاثة بواحد يكون تقديرين وثلاثة لا تقديراً واحداً فتكون تلك التقديرات أقيسة، لا قياساً واحداً .

،
فجعلهم ما زاد على الاثنتين من المقدمات في معنى أقيسة متعددة، وما نقص عن الاثنتين نصف قياس لا قياس تام اصطلاح محض لا يرجع إلى معنى معقول، كما : فرقوا بين الصفات الذاتية والعرضية اللازمة لـ «الماهية والوجود» بمثل هذا التحكم .
The third station: the expression of 'evidence' as a singular term. And the word 'qiyas' implies estimation, as it is said: 'I measured this by that.' And estimation originally occurs by one, just as it occurs by two and three. So the origin of estimation is by one, and if it is estimated by two or three by one, they become two estimations and three, not one estimation. So those estimations are multiple qiyases, not one qiyas. Then their making what exceeds two premises to be in the meaning of multiple qiyases, and what is less than two to be half a qiyas, not a complete qiyas, is a pure convention that has no rational meaning, just as they distinguished between essential and accidental attributes necessary to 'essence and existence' by such arbitrariness.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the logicians' technical definition of qiyas (syllogism). Ibn Taymiyya begins by examining the root meaning of the word qiyas, which implies estimation or comparison. He points out that estimation can be done with one, two, or three things, and when you estimate two or three things by one standard, you get multiple estimations, not one. Therefore, the logicians' claim that a complete qiyas must consist of exactly two premises is arbitrary. They treat more than two premises as multiple qiyases and less than two as a half qiyas, but this is pure convention (istilah mahd) with no rational basis. Ibn Taymiyya compares this to their equally arbitrary distinction between essential and accidental attributes.

Key Terms
قياس (qiyas): Here, Ibn Taymiyya critiques the logicians' technical definition of qiyas as a syllogism with two premises, arguing that the root meaning of estimation does not require exactly two. [Note: In classical logic, qiyas is a technical term for categorical syllogism; Ibn Taymiyya returns to its linguistic root to critique the technical definition.]
تقدير (taqdir): Estimation or measurement; the root meaning of qiyas according to Ibn Taymiyya.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya demonstrates that logical definitions must be grounded in linguistic meaning rather than arbitrary convention, and that the requirement of exactly two premises for a qiyas is not supported by the Arabic root of the term.

Refutation of the Two-Premise Rule
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the claim that proof requires exactly two premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that the minimum required premise in a proof is one, not two, and evaluate its logical force.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya turns the logicians' concession against them: if one premise can suffice, the two-premise rule is arbitrary.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya exposes an inconsistency in the logicians' doctrine. They admit that sometimes a single premise is enough for a proof, yet they insist that a valid syllogism must have exactly two premises. To resolve this, they reclassify any proof with more premises as a 'compound' syllogism. Ibn Taymiyya argues that their own logic should lead them to accept one premise as the essential minimum. Let's follow his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Summarize the logicians' position on premises
Understand the logicians' claim: a valid syllogism has exactly two premises. They admit that sometimes more are used, but they call that a 'compound' syllogism.
They also admit that sometimes one premise suffices, which is key.
2
Identify the logicians' concession
Ibn Taymiyya notes that they acknowledge 'the proof may need premises, and sometimes one premise suffices'. This admission is the foundation of his critique.
3
Follow Ibn Taymiyya's reversal
He argues: if you admit that one premise can be enough, then your claim that two are necessary is inconsistent. You should instead say the minimum is one, and any extra premises only clarify that one.
He uses their own classification of compound syllogisms to support this: extra premises are just separate syllogisms explaining the first premise.
4
Assess the conclusion
Ibn Taymiyya concludes that the essential logical unit is a single premise expressing the middle term. The logicians' two-premise rule is arbitrary and self-defeating.

ثم إنهم لما علموا أن «الدليل قد يحتاج إلى مقدمات، وقد يكفي فيه مقدمة واحدة، قالوا : إنه ربما أدرج في القياس قول زائد أي مقدمة ثالثة زائدة على مقدمتين، لغرض فاسد أو صحيح كبيان المقدمتين ويسمونه «المركب». قالوا: ومضمونه أقيسة متعددة سيقت لبيان مطلوب واحد إلا أن المطلوب منها بالذات ليس إلا واحداً. قالوا وربما حذفت إحدى المقدمات، إما للعلم بها، أو لغرض فاسد. وقسموا المركب إلى «مفصول» و «موصول» كما تقدم. فيقال : هذا اعتراف منكم بأن من المطالب ما يحتاج إلى مقدمات، ومنها ما يكفي الذي لا بد فيه مقدمة واحدة ثم قلتم : إن ذلك الذي يحتاج إلى مقدمات هو في معنى أقيسة متعددة. فيقال لكم: إذا ادّعيتم أن الذي لا بد منه إنما هو قياس واحد يشتمل على مقدمتين، وأن ما زاد على ذلك هو في معنى أقيسة، كل قياس لبيان مقدمة من المقدمات، فقولوا : إن الذي لا بد منه هو مقدمة واحدة وإن ما زاد على تلك المقدمة من المقدمات فإنما هو لبيان تلك المقدمة. وهذا أقرب إلى المعقول. فإنه إذا لم يُعلم ثبوت الصفة للموصوف ـ وهو ثبوت الحكم للمحكوم عليه، وهو ثبوت الخبر للمبتدأ، أو المحمول للموضوع ـ إلا بوسط بينهما هو الدليل، فالذي لا بد منه هو مقدمة واحدة، وما زاد على ذلك فقد
Then when they knew that 'the proof may need premises, and sometimes one premise suffices', they said: 'Indeed, possibly an extra statement is inserted into the syllogism, i.e., a third premise in addition to two premises, for a corrupt or sound purpose, such as clarifying the two premises, and they call it the 'compound''. They said: 'Its content is multiple syllogisms brought to clarify one sought conclusion, except that the sought conclusion in themselves is only one.' They said: 'And possibly one of the premises is omitted, either because it is known, or for a corrupt purpose.' And they divided the compound into 'disconnected' and 'connected' as previously. So it is said: 'This is an admission from you that among the demands are those that need premises, and among them are those for which one premise suffices as necessary, then you said that those which need premises are in the sense of multiple syllogisms.' So it is said to you: 'If you claim that what is necessary is only one syllogism comprising two premises, and that what exceeds that is in the sense of syllogisms, each syllogism to clarify one of the premises, then say that what is necessary is one premise, and what exceeds that premise of the premises is only to clarify that premise.' And this is closer to reason. For if the establishment of the attribute for the described—i.e., the establishment of the judgment for the judged, the establishment of the predicate for the subject, or the predicate for the subject—is not known except through a middle term which is the proof, then what is necessary is one premise, and what exceeds that has...
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the logicians' insistence that a valid proof (dalil) must consist of exactly two premises and no more or less. Ibn Taymiyya begins by summarizing the logicians' concession: they admit that sometimes a proof may need more premises or that one premise suffices. To preserve their doctrine, they reclassify situations with more than two premises as 'compound' (murakkab) syllogisms, which are really multiple syllogisms aimed at a single conclusion. They further divide the compound into 'disconnected' (mafsul) and 'connected' (mawsul). Ibn Taymiyya then turns their argument against them. He points out that if they admit that some demands require only one premise, then the essential minimum is one, not two. Their claim that everything beyond one premise is actually multiple syllogisms, each clarifying a premise, should logically lead them to say that the necessary minimum is one premise, and any additional premises are merely explanatory of that one. This, he says, is more reasonable. He then elaborates: to establish a predicate for a subject, we need a middle term (the proof). That middle term itself is expressed in a single premise. Therefore, the necessary minimum is one premise, not two. Any further premises go beyond the essential requirement.

The Flexible Number of Premises in Proof
Critique of the fixed two-premise requirement in syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the fixed two-premise requirement and identify when a single premise suffices in an Islamic legal proof.

Core Idea
The number of premises in a proof is determined by context, not by Greek logic.

The logicians insist that every proof must have exactly two premises, forming a categorical syllogism. But Ibn Taymiyya shows that this is not always the case: sometimes one premise is enough, sometimes three or more. Using the example of the prohibition of an intoxicating drink, he demonstrates how the sufficiency of premises depends on what the listener already accepts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' doctrine under attack
Pinpoint the exact claim Ibn Taymiyya challenges: that every syllogism (qiyas) must consist of exactly two premises.
This is the target of his critique.
2
Extract the counterexample from his text
Find the concrete example he provides: someone says 'This drink is forbidden because it is an intoxicating nabidh.' In some contexts, this single premise is sufficient.
Look for the exchange: 'Why?' – 'Because it is an intoxicating nabidh.'
3
Analyze the conditions for sufficiency
Determine when one premise suffices (the listener already knows all intoxicants are forbidden) and when more are needed (denial of intoxication or denial of universal rule).
Trace the reasoning: if listener concedes the premise, proof is complete; otherwise, more premises are required.
4
Observe the role of qiyas tamthil
Notice that Ibn Taymiyya uses analogical syllogism (qiyas tamthil) to establish the minor premise (the drink is intoxicating) via experiment or reliable report, and claims this yields certainty.
He groups sensory and experimental knowledge under analogical reasoning.

As for the claim of need for the syllogism which consists of two premises, for the need of that in some demands, it is like the claim of need in some of them for three premises, four, and five, for the need of that in some demands. And assessing a number is not more appropriate than another number. And what they mention of dropping one of the two premises due to its clarity or for misrepresentation, its like exists in the third and fourth. Whoever argues for an issue with a premise that alone does not suffice in clarifying the sought, or two or three premises that do not suffice, he is asked for completion that achieves sufficiency. And if the premises are mentioned, what is rejectable is rejected, and what is open to counter-argument is countered until the inference is completed. Like the proof for the prohibition of a specific drink when he said: this is forbidden, and it was said to him: 'why?' He said: 'because it is an intoxicating nabidh.' So this premise is sufficient if the listener is among those who know that every intoxicant is forbidden, if he concedes that premise. And if he denies it, saying: we do not concede that this is intoxicating, he needs to clarify it by the report of someone whose report is trusted, or by experiment in its like, and this is analogical syllogism (qiyas tamthil), and it yields certainty. For the drink by analogical syllogism: if some of it is tested and known to be intoxicating, then the rest of it is known to be intoxicating, because the ruling of some is like the ruling of others. And likewise all experimental propositions, like the knowledge that bread satiates, water quenches, and the like, their foundation is only analogical syllogism. Rather, all sensory things that are known to be universal are only through analogical syllogism. And if he is one who disputes that the intoxicating nabidh is forbidden, he needs two premises: to establish that this is intoxicating, and that every intoxicant is khamr. So he establishes the second by multiple proofs, like the saying of the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'Every intoxicant is khamr' and 'Every drink that intoxicates is forbidden', and because he was asked about a drink made from honey called 'al-Bath' and a drink made from grain called 'al-Mizr', and he was given comprehensive speech, so he said: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden'. These hadiths are in the Sahih. They and their multiples are well-known from the Prophet (peace be upon him) indicating that he forbade every intoxicating drink. Then if he says: I know it is khamr, but I do not concede that khamr is forbidden, or I do not concede that it is absolutely forbidden, he establishes this third premise, and so on.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the logicians' insistence that every syllogism (qiyas) must consist of exactly two premises. Ibn Taymiyya argues that the number of premises is not fixed; sometimes one suffices, sometimes three or more, depending on the context. He illustrates with the example of a wine prohibition argument. The one-premise case: if someone says 'This drink is forbidden because it is an intoxicating nabidh,' and the listener already knows that all intoxicants are forbidden, then one premise suffices. If the listener doubts the intoxication, then analogical syllogism (qiyas tamthil) is needed to establish intoxication through experimentation or reliable report. If the listener disputes that intoxicating nabidh is forbidden, then two premises are needed: (1) this is intoxicating, (2) every intoxicant is khamr (likely meaning forbidden as wine). The second premise is established by hadith evidence. Thus, the number of premises is flexible, not fixed at two. Ibn Taymiyya also emphasizes that analogical syllogism is the basis for experimental and sensory knowledge, providing certainty. All examples are from Islamic legal reasoning (fiqh) and hadith, firmly rooted in the Islamic tradition.

The Middle Term and Self-Evident Necessity
Author explains that a single premise suffices because it entails the judgment via a middle term, and critiques the distinction between essential and necessary attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how a single premise can suffice for proof by identifying the middle term as 'because' and distinguish between necessary attributes that require a middle term and those that are self-evident.

Core Idea
The middle term is not a physical link but a logical 'because'—and some necessities need no proof at all.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya deepens our understanding of proof by showing that a single premise can be sufficient when the entailment is clear. He uses the logical property of transitivity: if A entails B and B entails C, then A entails C. The key is the middle term (wasat), which is nothing other than the reason expressed by 'because'. He then critiques those who claim that all necessary attributes require a middle term, pointing out that many necessities are self-evident. This lesson will help you grasp the nature of evidence and the role of the middle term in Islamic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
The author states that a single premise may suffice to obtain the sought judgment. This is because the evidence (dalil) directly entails the judgment, and the entailment can be traced through a middle term.
2
Trace the transitive entailment
The logic is: if the first term (hadd awwal) entails the middle, and the middle entails the third, then the first entails the third. This is because 'the entailed of the entailed is entailed' and 'the necessary of the necessary is necessary'.
This is a logical property of transitivity applied to entailment.
3
Locate the middle term in your own words
The middle term is what is coupled with the word 'because' (li-anna). It is the reason that links the evidence to the judgment. For example, 'The world is created because it changes' – 'changes' is the middle term.
The middle term is the evidence (dalil) itself, not a physical intermediary.
4
Examine the critique of the essential/necessary distinction
Some thinkers distinguished between essential (dhati) and necessary (lazim) attributes, claiming necessary attributes always require a middle term. Ibn Taymiyya reports that logicians like Ibn Sina refuted this by noting that many necessary attributes are self-evident (bayyin al-luzum) and require no middle term.
This challenges the idea that all necessities need proof.
5
Correct the misunderstanding of 'middle'
The author clarifies that 'middle' is not a concept intermediate in reality between near and far necessary attributes. It is simply the logical evidence. This avoids confusion about the nature of the middle term.
The middle term is a logical relation, not a physical one.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثالث - مزيد البيان أن المقدمة الواحدة قد تكفي . ومما يبين لك أن المقدمة الواحدة قد تكفي في حصول المطلوب : أن الدليل هو ما يستلزم الحكم المدلول عليه كما تقدم بيانه . ولما كان الحد الأول مستلزماً للأوسط، والأوسط للثالث ثبت أن الأول مستلزم للثالث. فإن ملزوم الملزوم ملزوم، ولازم اللازم لازم فالحكم لازم من لوازم الدليل، لكن لم يُعرف لزومه إياه إلا بوسط بينهما، والوسط ما يقرن بقولك «لأنه» . وهذا مما ذكره المنطقيون ابن سينا وغيره . ذكروا الصفات اللازمة للموصوف وأن منها ما يكون بين اللزوم. وردّوا بذلك على من فرّق من أصحابهم بين «الذاتي» و «اللازم للماهية بأن «اللازم» ما افتقر إلى وسط» بخلاف الذاتي». فقالوا له : كثير من الصفات اللازمة لا تفتقر إلى وسط وهي البينة اللزوم. و «الوسط» عند هؤلاء هو «الدليل». وأما ما ظنّه بعض الناس أن «الوسط»: هو ما يكون متوسطاً في نفس الأمر بين اللازم القريب واللازم البعيد، فهذا خطأ . ومع هذا يتبين حصول المراد على التقديرين . فنقول :
The third station: additional clarification that a single premise may suffice. And what shows you that a single premise may suffice in obtaining the sought is that the evidence (dalil) is what entails the judgment indicated, as previously explained. Since the first term (hadd) entails the middle, and the middle entails the third, it is established that the first entails the third. For the entailed of the entailed is entailed, and the necessary of the necessary is necessary. So the judgment is among the necessary consequences of the evidence, but its entailment of it is not known except through a middle between them, and the middle is that which is coupled with your saying 'because'. This is something mentioned by the logicians Ibn Sina and others. They mentioned the necessary attributes of the described, and that among them are those that are evident in entailment. And they refuted by that those among their companions who distinguished between the essential (dhati) and the necessary (lazim) by saying that the necessary is what requires a middle, unlike the essential. So they said to him: many necessary attributes do not require a middle, and they are evident in entailment. And 'middle' according to these people is 'evidence'. And as for what some people thought, that the middle is what is intermediate in reality between the near necessary and the far necessary, this is a mistake. Nevertheless, the attainment of the desired becomes clear on both estimations. So we say:

Commentary
This passage clarifies how a single premise can be sufficient for proof. The author explains that the evidence (dalil) entails the judgment. Using terminology from categorical syllogisms, he states that if the first term (hadd awwal) entails the middle, and the middle entails the third, then the first entails the third. This is a transitive property: the entailed of the entailed is entailed, and the necessary of the necessary is necessary. The judgment is a necessary consequence of the evidence, but this entailment is only known through a middle term (wasat), which is expressed by the word 'because' (li-anna). The author then discusses a point from logicians like Ibn Sina: they spoke of necessary attributes (sifat lazimah) of a subject, some of which are self-evident (bayyin al-luzum) and do not require a middle term to be known. This is used to refute some thinkers who distinguished between essential (dhati) and necessary (lazim) attributes by claiming that necessary attributes always require a middle term. The logicians argue that many necessary attributes are obvious without a middle. The author then corrects a misunderstanding: the middle term is not a physical intermediary between near and far necessary attributes, but rather the logical evidence (dalil) itself. This explanation reinforces the idea that a single premise can suffice if the entailment is clear.

The Middle Term and Self-Evident Entailment
Author explains that the first term entails the middle, and the middle entails the third, so the first entails the third.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between entailments that require a middle term and self-evident necessary attributes, using Ibn Taymiyya's critique.

Core Idea
Not all necessary attributes need a middle term; some are evident by themselves.

Ibn Taymiyya explains that the middle term (al-wasat) is what connects evidence to ruling, expressed by 'because'. However, he argues against the logicians' claim that every necessary attribute requires a middle term to be known. Many necessary attributes are clear in their entailment (bayyina al-luzum) and need no proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the chain of entailment
Trace the chain: the first term entails the middle, the middle entails the third, so the first entails the third. This is the structure of a proof.
This mirrors syllogistic reasoning but is presented as a conceptual chain.
2
Recognize the middle term as the 'because'
Ibn Taymiyya explicitly says: 'الوسط ما يقرن بقولك لأنه' – the middle term is what is coupled with your saying 'because'. It is the evidence or reason.
In standard logic, the middle term connects premises; here it is the proof.
3
Distinguish essential from necessary attributes
Logicians like Ibn Sina distinguished between essential (dhati) attributes, which need no proof, and necessary (lazim) attributes, which require a middle term. Ibn Taymiyya challenges this distinction.
Essential attributes are intrinsic; necessary attributes are consequences.
4
Apply Ibn Taymiyya's critique
He argues that many necessary attributes are 'bayyina al-luzum' (clear in their entailment) and do not need a middle term. The necessity of a middle term depends on the clarity of the entailment, not a categorical rule.
This critique undermines the logicians' rigid dichotomy.

ومما مزيد البيان أن المقدمة الواحدة قد تكفي يبين لك أن المقدمة الواحدة قد تكفي في حصول المطلوب : أن الدليل هو ما يستلزم الحكم المدلول عليه كما تقدم بيانه ولما كان الحد الأول مستلزماً للأوسط، والأوسط للثالث ثبت أن الأول مستلزم للثالث. فإن ملزوم الملزوم ملزوم، ولازم اللازم لازم فالحكم لازم من لوازم الدليل، لكن لم يُعرف لزومه إياه إلا بوسط بينهما، والوسط ما يقرن بقولك «لأنه» .

وهذا مما ذكره المنطقيون ابن سينا وغيره . ذكروا الصفات اللازمة للموصوف وأن منها ما يكون بيّنة اللزوم. وردّوا بذلك على من فرّق من أصحابهم بين «الذاتي» و «اللازم للماهية بأن «اللازم» ما افتقر إلى وسط» بخلاف الذاتي». فقالوا له : كثير من الصفات اللازمة لا تفتقر إلى وسط وهي البيّنة اللزوم. و «الوسط» عند هؤلاء هو «الدليل».

وأما ما ظنّه بعض الناس أن «الوسط»: هو ما يكون متوسطاً في نفس الأمر بين اللازم القريب واللازم البعيد، فهذا خطأ . ومع هذا يتبين حصول المراد على التقديرين .
And from what clarifies to you that the single premise may suffice for obtaining the desired conclusion: that the evidence is that which necessitates the judged ruling, as was previously explained. And since the first term is necessitating of the middle, and the middle of the third, it is established that the first is necessitating of the third. For the necessitated of the necessitated is necessitated, and the necessary of the necessary is necessary. So the ruling is among the necessities of the evidence, but its being necessitated by it is only known through a middle term between them, and the middle term is what is coupled with your saying 'because'. And this is among what the logicians, Ibn Sina and others, mentioned: they mentioned the necessary attributes of the described, and that among them are those that are clear in their entailment. And they refuted thereby those among their fellows who distinguished between 'essential' and 'necessary to the essence' by saying that 'the necessary' is what requires a middle term, unlike 'the essential'. So they said to him: Many necessary attributes do not require a middle term, being clear in entailment. And 'the middle term' according to these is 'the evidence'. As for what some people think, that 'the middle term' is what mediates in reality between the near necessary and the far necessary, this is an error. Yet the attainment of the objective is clarified on both estimations.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage elaborates on the concept of the middle term (al-wasat). Ibn Taymiyya explains that the evidence (al-dalil) is what necessitates the ruling. The chain of entailment: the first term entails the middle, the middle entails the third, so the first entails the third. The middle term is what is expressed by the word 'because'. He references the logicians' discussion of necessary attributes (al-sifat al-lazima). Ibn Sina and others distinguished between attributes that are essential (dhati) and those that are necessary (lazim) for the essence, claiming that the necessary requires a middle term to prove its entailment. Ibn Taymiyya notes that many necessary attributes are clear in their entailment (bayyina al-luzum) and do not require a middle term. He also refutes the idea that the middle term is something that mediates in reality between a near necessary and a far necessary. This is an error, but the desired point is still achieved on both interpretations.

اللازم
الذاتي

Key Takeaway
The middle term is the evidence expressed by 'because', but not all necessary attributes require a middle term; some entailments are self-evident.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of the Two-Premise Sufficiency
Ibn Taymiyya critiques the claim that two premises suffice for knowledge of the conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that the number of needed premises is not fixed at two and explain why forgetfulness undermines the logicians' claim.

Core Idea
Forgetfulness proves that premises must be actually present; knowledge requires awareness, not just storage.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the foundational claim of Aristotelian syllogistic that just two premises always suffice for certain knowledge. He presents al-Razi's objection about infinite regress, but then goes deeper, showing that even having both premises in mind does not guarantee the conclusion if one is forgotten. The real issue is the psychological presence of knowledge, not the formal structure.

Guided Path
1
Identify al-Razi's Objection
Al-Razi argued that the subsumption of one premise under the other either requires a third premise (leading to infinite regress) or cannot be a condition because it is not distinct from the premises.
This is a logical dilemma: either infinite regress or no condition.
2
Understand Infinite Regress Problem
If the subsumption is a separate premise, then the same question applies to how it combines with the first two, leading to an infinite chain.
Ibn Taymiyya uses this to show the flaw in assuming exactly two premises.
3
Grasp Ibn Taymiyya's Alternative
He asserts that what is needed is whatever makes the conclusion known, whether one, two, three, or more premises. The number is not fixed.
This shifts focus from formal structure to epistemic sufficiency.
4
Analyze the Forgetfulness Example
The mule example: a person may have both premises but forget the key premise 'the mule does not give birth', so the conclusion is not known. Forgetting means the premise is not actually present in mind.
This psychological fact undermines the claim that two premises always suffice.
5
Conclude on the Nature of Evidence
Evidence (dalīl) is knowledge of the entailment between the sign and the signified. This entailment must be present and attended to; otherwise, it does not function as evidence.
Ibn Taymiyya emphasizes actual awareness over static possession of premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثالث - الخلاف في أن العلم بالمقدمتين كاف في العلم بالنتيجة أم لا؟ ونازعه الرازي وغيره وقالوا هذا ضعيف؛ لأن اندراج إحدى المقدمتين تحت الأخرى إن كان مغايراً للمقدمتين كان ذلك مقدمة أخرى لا بد فيها من الإنتاج، ويكون الكلام في كيفية التيامها مع الأوليين كالكلام في كيفية التيام الأوليين، ويفضي ذلك إلى اعتبار ما لا نهاية له من المقدمات. وإن لم يكن ذلك معلوماً مغايراً للمقدمتين استحال أن يكون شرطاً في الإنتاج، لأن الشرط مغاير للمشروط، وهنا لا مغايرة، فلا يكون شرطاً. وأما حديث البغلة فذلك إنما يمكن إذا كان الحاضِرُ في الذهن إحدى المقدمتين فقط، إما الصغرى وإما الكبرى. وأما عند اجتماعهما في الذهن فلا نسلّم أنه يمكن الشك أصلاً في النتيجة . قلت : وحقيقة الأمر أن هذا النزاع لزمهم في ظَنّهم الحاجة إلى مقدمتين فقط، وليس الأمر كذلك، بل المحتاج إليه هو ما به يُعْلَم المطلوب، سواء كانت مقدمة، أو ثنتين أو ثلاثاً. والمغفول عنه ليس بمعلوم حال الغفلة، فإذا تذكر صار معلوماً بالفعل. وهنا الدليل هو العلم بأن البغلة لا تحبل»، وهذه المقدمة كان ذاهلاً عنها، فلم يكن عالماً بها العلم الذي تحصل به الدلالة. فإن المغفول عنه لا يدل حين ما يكون مغفولاً عنه، بل إنما يدل حال كونه مذكوراً، إذ هو بذلك يكون معلوماً علماً حاضراً. والرب تعالى منزّه عن الغفلة والنسيان، لأن ذلك يناقض حقيقة العلم، كما أن تنزيه الرب منزه عن السِّنَة والنوم، لأن ذلك يناقض كمال الحياة والقيوميّة، فإن النوم أخو الموت . ولهذا كان أهل الجنة لا ينامون كما لا يموتون، وكانوا يُلْهَمُون التسبيحَ كما يُلهم أحدنا النَفَس. والمقصود هنا أن وجه الدليل العلم بلزوم المدلول له سواء سُمّى «استحضاراً» أو «تفطّناً» أو غير ذلك. فمتى استحضر في ذهنه لزوم المدلول له علم أنه دال عليه وهذا اللزوم إن كان بيناً له، وإلا فقد يحتاج في بيانه إلى مقدمة، أو ثنتين، أو ثلاثة، أو أكثر (۱). (۱) كذا بالأصل، ولعل الصواب : «أو ثلاث».
The third station - Dispute over whether knowledge of the two premises is sufficient for knowledge of the conclusion or not? Al-Razi and others disputed him and said this is weak; because the subsumption of one premise under the other: if it is different from the two premises, that would be another premise necessary for production, and the discussion about how it combines with the first two would be like the discussion about how the first two combine, leading to the consideration of an infinite number of premises. And if that subsumption is not known to be different from the two premises, it is impossible for it to be a condition for production, because the condition is different from the conditioned, and here there is no difference, so it would not be a condition. As for the story of the mule, that is only possible when only one of the two premises is present in the mind, either the minor or the major. But when both are present in the mind together, we do not concede that doubt can occur at all about the conclusion. I say: The reality is that this dispute arose from their assumption that only two premises are needed, which is not the case. Rather, what is needed is that by which the desired is known, whether it is one premise, two, three, or more. What is forgotten is not known during forgetfulness; when remembered, it becomes actually known. Here the evidence is the knowledge that the mule does not give birth, and this premise was absent from him, so he was not knowing it with the knowledge that yields indication. For what is forgotten does not indicate while it is forgotten, but only indicates when it is mentioned, as it then becomes known as present knowledge. God Almighty is free from forgetfulness and forgetfulness, because that contradicts the reality of knowledge, just as the Lord is free from drowsiness and sleep, because that contradicts the perfection of life and self-subsistence, for sleep is the brother of death. For this reason, the people of Paradise do not sleep just as they do not die, and they are inspired to glorify as one of us is inspired to breathe. The point here is that the aspect of evidence is knowledge of the entailment of the indicated, whether called 'presence' or 'mindfulness' or otherwise. So whenever he brings to mind the entailment of the indicated, he knows that it indicates it. This entailment, if evident to him, then fine; otherwise, he may need to clarify it with one, two, three, or more premises.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the logicians' claim that knowledge of just two premises is always sufficient for knowledge of the conclusion. Ibn Taymiyya presents al-Razi's objection: if the subsumption (indiraj) of one premise under the other is a separate matter, it becomes a third premise, leading to infinite regress; if not separate, it cannot be a condition because a condition must be distinct from the conditioned. Then Ibn Taymiyya responds that the real issue is not the number of premises but what actually makes the conclusion known, which may be one, two, three, or more premises depending on the situation. He uses the example of the mule: a person may have both premises in mind but still not realize the conclusion if the premise 'the mule does not give birth' is forgotten. This shows that actual knowledge requires the premises to be present and attended to. He analogizes forgetfulness to sleep, noting God is above both. The ultimate point is that 'evidence' (dalil) is the knowledge of the entailment (luzum) between the evidence and the inferred, and this entailment may itself need further premises to be clarified. Thus, the number of needed premises is not fixed at two.

المقدمتان
الغفلة
الدليل

The number of premises is not fixed; what matters is the actual presence of knowledge of entailment.

Ibn Taymiyya's Expansion of the Middle Term
Ibn Taymiyya redefines the middle term as variable evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's expansion of the middle term (al-wasaṭ) and distinguish between evidence (dalīl) and cause (ʿilla).

Core Idea
The middle term is not fixed but varies with people and contexts.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya challenges the rigid Aristotelian notion of the middle term, arguing that it is any evidence that mediates knowledge, not a fixed logical constant. He illustrates this with examples from transmission of the Qur'an and Sunnah, sensory knowledge, and rational guidance, showing how the same conclusion can be reached through different intermediaries. Understanding this expansion is crucial for appreciating how Islamic scholars across traditions validate knowledge through diverse chains of evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key definitions
Locate where Ibn Taymiyya defines al-wasaṭ (the middle) and al-dalīl (evidence). He states: 'the middle is the evidence, and it is the intermediary in knowledge between the entailing and the entailed.'
This redefines the middle term as any intermediary that leads to knowledge.
2
Recognize the variation of middle terms
Notice the examples: different reports for different people, chains of transmitters for the Qur'an and Sunnah, and rational or sensory guides. Ibn Taymiyya emphasizes that what serves as evidence for one may not serve as evidence for another, yet both can lead to the same conclusion.
This variation is rooted in God's guidance (هداية) and human circumstances.
3
Distinguish mental from external middle
Ibn Taymiyya criticizes the view that the middle must be a real ontological intermediary (ʿilla) in the external world. He asserts that the mental middle is more general than the external; every external middle can be a mental middle, but not vice versa.
This highlights the epistemic role of the middle term over its metaphysical status.
4
Differentiate evidence (dalīl) from cause (ʿilla)
He states: 'evidence is more general than the cause, for every cause can be used as evidence for the effect, but not every evidence is a cause in reality.' This means that evidence includes signs, reports, and indicators that are not necessarily causal.
This distinction is vital for evaluating arguments in Islamic jurisprudence and theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن الأوساط تتنوع بتنوع الناس. فليس ما كان وسطاً مستلزماً للحكم في حق هذا هُوَ الذي يجب أن يكون وسطاً في حق الآخر، بل قد يحصل له وسط آخر. فَـ «الوسط» هو الدليل، وهو الواسطة في العلم بين الملزوم واللازم، وهما المحكوم والمحكوم عليه فإن الحكم لازم للمحكوم عليه ما دام حكماً له . والأواسط - التي هي الأدلة - مما يتنوّع ويتعدّد بحسب ما يفتحه الله للناس من الهداية، كما إذا كان «الوسط» خبر صادق فقد يكون الخبر لهذا غير الخبر لهذا . وإذا رأى الناسُ الهلال وثبت عند دار السلطان وتفرّق الناس فأشاعوا ذلك في البلد، فكل قوم يحصل لهم العلم غير المخبرين الذين أخبروا غيرهم . والقرآن والسنة الذي يبلغه الناس عن الرسول بلغ كلَّ قوم بوسائط غير وسائط غيرهم، لا سيما في القرن الثاني والثالث. فهؤلاء لهم مقرئون ومعلمون، وهؤلاء لهم مقرئون ومعلمون. وهؤلاء كلهم وسائط ـ وهم الأواسط ـ بينهم وبين معرفة ما قاله الرسول وفعله، الذين دلوهم على ذلك بإخبارهم وتعليمهم . وكذلك المعلومات التي تُنال بالعقل أو الحس إذا نبه عليها مُنبّه، وأرشد إليها مرشد ، فذلك أيضاً مما يختلف ويتنوّع ؛ ونفسُ الوسائط العقلية تتنوع وتختلف . وأما من جعل «الوسط» في اللوازم هو وسطاً في نفس ثبوتها للموصوف، فهذا باطل من وجوه، كما قد بسط في موضعه . وبتقدير صحته، فالوسط الذهني أعم من الخارجي، كما أن الدليل أعم من العلة فكل علة يمكن الاستدلال بها على المعلول، وليس كل دليل يكون علة في نفس الأمر . وكذلك ما كان متوسطاً في نفس الأمر أمكن جَعْلُه متوسطاً في الذهن، فيكون دليلاً، ولا ينعكس، لأن الدليل هو ما كان مستلزماً للمدلول . فالعلة المستلزمة للمعلول يمكن الاستدلال بها والوسط الذي يلزم الملزوم، ويلزمه اللازم البعيد، هو مستلزم
For the middle terms vary according to the variation of people. So it is not the case that what is a middle term entailing the judgment for one person must necessarily be a middle term for another; rather, he may have another middle term. So the 'middle' is the evidence (dalīl), and it is the intermediary in knowledge between the entailing and the entailed, which are the subject and predicate. For the judgment is entailed by the subject as long as it is a judgment of it. And the middle terms - which are the evidences - are of a kind that vary and multiply according to what God opens for people of guidance, just as if the 'middle' is a true report, the report for this person may be different from the report for that person. And when people see the crescent and it is confirmed at the Sultan's palace and the people disperse and spread it in the city, each group obtains knowledge different from the informants who informed others. And the Qur'an and Sunnah which reach people from the Messenger reach each group through intermediaries other than the intermediaries of others, especially in the second and third centuries. These have their reciters and teachers, and those have their reciters and teachers. And all these are intermediaries - they are the middle terms - between them and knowledge of what the Messenger said and did, who guided them to that through their reporting and teaching. Likewise, information attained by reason or sense, if a noticer alerts to it and a guide guides to it, that also differs and varies; and the rational intermediaries themselves vary and differ. As for one who makes the 'middle' in necessary relations a middle in the very existence of those relations for the described, that is false in many respects, as has been expounded elsewhere. And if it were valid, the mental middle is more general than the external, just as the evidence is more general than the cause. For every cause can be used as evidence for the effect, but not every evidence is a cause in reality. Likewise, what is a middle in actual existence can be made a middle in the mind, becoming evidence, but not vice versa, because the evidence is what entails the indicated. So the cause that entails the effect can be used as evidence, and the middle that is entailed by the entailing, and entails the remote consequent, is entailing.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage expands on the nature of the middle term (al-wasaṭ) in logical argumentation. Ibn Taymiyya argues that middle terms are not fixed but vary according to people, knowledge, and context. He defines the middle as the evidence (dalīl) that mediates between the entailing (malzūm) and the entailed (lāzim). He gives examples: a true report that serves as evidence for one person may be different for another; the sighting of the crescent moon spreads through different informants; the transmission of Qur'an and Sunnah involves different chains of teachers and reciters. Even rational and sensory knowledge can vary when different people point to it. He criticizes the view that the middle term must be a real ontological intermediary; rather, the mental middle is broader than any external middle. Evidence is more general than cause: while every cause can serve as evidence, not every evidence is a cause. The key point is that the middle term is whatever leads to knowledge of the conclusion, and it is not confined to a single form or a fixed logical role.

Key Terms
الوسط
الدليل
العلة
الأواسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the mental middle is more general and includes many things that are not real causes.
B) Because necessary relations do not exist in reality.
C) Because the middle term is always a cause.
D) Because people disagree about what a middle term is.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya redefines the middle term as any evidence that leads to knowledge, emphasizing its variability and the distinction between evidence and cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Different scholars may use different evidence: one uses the direct Qur'anic prohibition, another uses a hadith, another uses analogy (qiyas) with other intoxicants.
