The Arbitrariness of the Two-Premise Syllogism
Ibn Taymiyya attacks the two-premise requirement as arbitrary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify arbitrary constraints in syllogistic rules and contrast them with the flexible reasoning found in Islamic scholarship.

Core Idea
The two-premise rule is an arbitrary imposition, not a natural necessity.

Ibn Taymiyya opens his critique by directly attacking the foundational claim that every inference must have exactly two premises. He argues that this is pure arbitrariness (تحكم محض) because reasoning naturally adapts to the number of premises needed. In this lesson, we will analyze his argument and see how it undermines the universal validity of the syllogistic form.

Guided Path
1
Identify the arbitrary rule
Find where Ibn Taymiyya states that only logicians commit to two premises and that this requirement is pure arbitrary imposition.
Look for the phrase 'تحكم محض' (pure arbitrariness).
2
Extract the rationale
He says 'sometimes one premise suffices, sometimes multiple are needed.' Why does this make the two-premise rule arbitrary? Because it imposes a fixed number regardless of the actual inferential need.
3
Contrast with Islamic practice
Note that Muslim theologians and other rational groups use as many premises as needed. This serves as counter-evidence to the logicians' claim.
Consider the fitra salima (sound natural disposition) as the alternative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثالث - لا يلتزم الاستدلال بمقدمتين فقط إلا أهل المنطق
فتبين أنه على كل تقدير يمكن الاستدلال على المطلوب بمقدمة واحدة إذا لم يُحْتَج إلى غيرها، وقد لا يمكن إلا بمقدمات فيحتاج إلى معرفتهن. وأن تخصيص الحاجة بمقدمتين دون ما زاد وما نقص تحكُّم محض .
ولهذا لا تجد في سائر طوائف العقلاء ومصنفي العلوم مَنْ يلتزم في استدلاله البيانَ بمقدمتين لا أكثر ولا أقل، ويجتهد في رد الزيادة إلى ثنتين وفي تكميل النقص بجعله مقدمتين، إلا أهلُ منطق اليونان ومن سَلَك سبيلهم ، دون من كان باقياً على فطرته السليمة، أو سلك مسلك غيرهم كالمهاجرين والأنصار والتابعين لهم بإحسان، وسائر أئمة المسلمين، وعلمائهم، ونظارهم، وسائر طوائف الملل.
وكذلك أهل النحو والطب والهندسة، لا يدخل في هذا الباب إلا من أتبع في ذلك هؤلاء المنطقيين، كما قلدوهم في الحدود المركبة من «الجنس» و «الفصل». وما استفادوا بما تلقوه عنهم علماً إلا علماً يستغني عن باطل كلامهم، أو ما يضر ولا ينفع لما فيه من الجهل أو التطويل الكثير .
ولهذا لما كان الاستدلالُ تارة يقف على مقدمة وتارة على مقدمتين، وتارة على مقدمات، كانت طريقةُ نُظّار المسلمين أن يذكروا من الأدلة على المقدمات ما يحتاجون إليه ؛ ولا يلتزمون في كل استدلال أن يذكروا مقدمتين كما يفعله من يسلك سبيل المنطقيين. بل كُتُب نظار المسلمين وخطابهم وسلوكهم في نظرهم لأنفسهم ومناظرتهم لغيرهم تعليماً، وإرشاداً ومجادلة على ما ذكرتُ. وكذلك سائر أصناف العقلاء من أهل الملل ، وغيرهم إلا من سلك طريق هؤلاء. وما زال نظار المسلمين يَعِيبون طريقة أهل المنطق، ويبينون ما فيها من العِيِّ، واللُّكْنة، وقصور العقل، وعجز النطق ويبينون أنها إلى إفساد المنطق العقلي واللساني أقرب منها إلى تقويم ذلك. ولا يرضون أن يسلكوها في نظرهم ومناظرتهم، لا مع من يوالونه ولا مع من يُعادونه
وإنما كثر استعمالها من زمن أبي حامد . فإنه أدخل مقدمة من المنطق في أول من أدخل المنطق في كتابه المستصفى، وزعم أنه لا يثق بعلمه إلا من عرف هذا المنطق .
وصنف فيه معيار العلم» و «محك النظر .
وصنف كتاباً سمّاه القسطاس ، ذكر فيه خمس موازين - الضروب الثلاثة الحمليات والشرطي المتصل والشرطي المنفصل ـ وغير عباراتها إلى أمثلة أخذها من كلام المسلمين وزعم أنه أخذ تلك الموازين من الأنبياء، وذكر أنه خاطبَ بذلك بعض أهل التعليم .
The third station: Only the logicians commit to inference from exactly two premises. So it is clear that in every estimation, one can infer the desired conclusion from a single premise if nothing else is needed, and sometimes it is not possible except from multiple premises, so one needs to know them. And specifying the need to two premises, without more or less, is pure arbitrary imposition. For this reason, you do not find among all other classes of rational people and authors of sciences anyone who commits in his inference to expressing with two premises, no more no less, and strives to reduce excess to two and to complete a deficiency by making it two, except the people of Greek logic and those who follow their way, not those who remain upon their sound natural disposition, or follow the way of others such as the Muhajirun, the Ansar, those who followed them in goodness, and all the Imams of the Muslims, their scholars, their theologians, and all the classes of religious communities. Similarly, the grammarians, physicians, and geometers do not enter into this matter except those who follow these logicians, just as they imitated them in the definitions composed of genus and differentia. And they did not benefit from what they learned from them any knowledge except knowledge that is independent of the falsehood of their speech, or that is harmful and not beneficial due to the ignorance or excessive prolixity in it. For this reason, since inference sometimes depends on one premise, sometimes on two, sometimes on multiple premises, the method of the Muslim theologians is to mention from the evidences the premises they need, and they do not commit in every inference to mentioning two premises as does the one who follows the way of the logicians. Rather, the books of the Muslim theologians, their speech, and their conduct in their own reasoning and in their debates with others, whether teaching, guiding, or disputing, are as I have mentioned. And similarly all other classes of rational people from the religious communities and others, except those who follow the way of these logicians. The Muslim theologians have never ceased to criticize the method of the logicians, and to clarify what is in it of stammering, halting, intellectual deficiency, and verbal incapacity, and to show that it is closer to corrupting intellectual and linguistic logic than to rectifying it. And they are not satisfied to adopt it in their reasoning and debates, neither with those they befriend nor with those they oppose. Its widespread use began only from the time of Abu Hamid al-Ghazali. For he was among the first to introduce logic into his book 'al-Mustasfa', and he claimed that he does not trust his knowledge except for one who knows this logic. And he authored on it 'Mi'yar al-'Ilm' and 'Mihakk al-Nazar'. And he authored a book called 'al-Qistas al-Mustaqim', in which he mentioned five scales: the three categorical forms, the conditional conjunctive, and the conditional disjunctive. He transformed their expressions into examples taken from the speech of the Muslims, and he claimed that he took those scales from the prophets, and he mentioned that he addressed with that some of the followers of the Batinites.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is a critical exposition by Ibn Taymiyya attacking the logicians' insistence on exactly two premises in every inference. He begins by stating the third station (maqam) of his critique: only the logicians commit to a strict two-premise requirement. He then explains that this requirement is arbitrary, since sometimes a single premise suffices, sometimes multiple are needed. He contrasts this with the practice of all other rational people—including the early Muslims (Muhajirun, Ansar, followers), scholars, theologians, grammarians, physicians, and geometers—who use whatever number of premises is naturally required without artificial constraint. He accuses the logicians of imitating the Greeks and criticizes their definitions composed of genus and differentia. Ibn Taymiyya then argues that Muslim theologians never bound themselves to two premises and always criticized the logicians' method for its deficiencies and corruption. Finally, he notes that the widespread use of logic in the Islamic world began with al-Ghazali, who introduced logic into usul al-fiqh in his 'al-Mustasfa', authored works on logic ('Mi'yar al-'Ilm', 'Mihakk al-Nazar'), and even wrote 'al-Qistas al-Mustaqim' which reframed syllogistic forms using Islamic examples from the Quran and hadith, claiming prophetic origin for the scales. This historical point serves to show that logic is a foreign innovation rather than an intrinsic part of Islamic sciences.

Key Terms
استدلال
مقدمة
تحكم
فطرة سليمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya in this passage, what makes the two-premise requirement arbitrary?
A) Because it is not derived from the Quran or Sunnah.
B) Because inference can be done with one premise or multiple premises, so a fixed number is unwarranted.
C) Because logicians cannot agree on the definition of a premise.
D) Because the Greeks themselves did not use only two premises.

Key Takeaway
The number of premises in inference should be determined by the reasoning need, not by an artificial rule.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In the debate on qiyas, some scholars require exactly four premises (original case, ruling, cause, new case). Identify whether this requirement is similar to the logicians' two-premise rule, and argue why it might be arbitrary or justified.

The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
Natural Reasoning vs. Greek Imitation
Ibn Taymiyya contrasts natural fitra with imitative Greek logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between natural reasoning (fitra) and the imitative adoption of Greek logical forms in Islamic scholarship.

Core Idea
Sound fitra, not Greek logic, guided the early Muslims and their scholars.

Ibn Taymiyya contrasts the logicians' two-premise commitment with the natural practice of all rational people, especially the early Muslims (Muhajirun, Ansar, followers) and scholars of various sciences. He accuses the logicians of blindly imitating the Greeks, even in their definitions. This lesson explores the concept of fitra salima as the authentic Islamic mode of reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the groups that follow fitra
List the groups Ibn Taymiyya mentions as not committing to two premises: the Muhajirun, Ansar, Imams, grammarians, physicians, geometers, etc.
Note that he includes all classes of rational people except the Greek logicians and their followers.
2
Analyze the critique of imitation
He says they 'imitated them in the definitions composed of genus and differentia.' What is the problem with this imitation? It imports an artificial method into Islamic sciences.
3
Evaluate the charge of harm
He claims this imitation yields 'ignorance or excessive prolixity.' How does the two-premise requirement lead to harm?
Consider the stress on reducing or expanding premises to fit a mold.

According to the passage, which groups do NOT use the two-premise method?
A) The Greek logicians only.
B) The Muhajirun, Ansar, and all Muslim scholars.

?

Al-Ghazali and the Spread of Greek Logic
Ibn Taymiyya criticizes al-Ghazali's introduction of Greek logic into Islamic sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace the historical introduction of Greek logic into Islamic sciences through al-Ghazali and evaluate Ibn Taymiyya's criticism of this development.

Core Idea
Al-Ghazali popularized Greek logic in Islam, but Ibn Taymiyya argues this was a corrupting innovation.

In the final part of the passage, Ibn Taymiyya turns to history, identifying Abu Hamid al-Ghazali as the key figure who introduced logic into mainstream Islamic scholarship. He recounts al-Ghazali's works and his claim that the logical scales are derived from the prophets. This lesson examines Ibn Taymiyya's historical critique and its implications for the authority of logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify al-Ghazali's role
Find where Ibn Taymiyya says 'its widespread use began from the time of Abu Hamid.' What works did al-Ghazali author?
Mentioned: al-Mustasfa, Mi'yar al-'Ilm, Mihakk al-Nazar, al-Qistas al-Mustaqim.
2
Analyze al-Ghazali's claim
He claimed the five scales (mawazin) are taken from the prophets. Why does Ibn Taymiyya find this problematic?
This implies the scales have prophetic origin, but Ibn Taymiyya sees them as Greek imports.
3
Evaluate the corruption charge
Earlier Muslim theologians criticized the logicians for 'stammering, halting, etc.' How does al-Ghazali's introduction contradict this tradition?
Al-Ghazali broke the consensus of criticism.

Similarly, the grammarians, physicians, and geometers do not enter into this matter except those who follow these logicians, just as they imitated them in the definitions composed of genus and differentia. And they did not benefit from what they learned from them any knowledge except knowledge that is independent of the falsehood of their speech, or that is harmful and not beneficial due to the ignorance or excessive prolixity in it. For this reason, since inference sometimes depends on one premise, sometimes on two, sometimes on multiple premises, the method of the Muslim theologians is to mention from the evidences the premises they need, and they do not commit in every inference to mentioning two premises as does the one who follows the way of the logicians. Rather, the books of the Muslim theologians, their speech, and their conduct in their own reasoning and in their debates with others, whether teaching, guiding, or disputing, are as I have mentioned. And similarly all other classes of rational people from the religious communities and others, except those who follow the way of these logicians. The Muslim theologians have never ceased to criticize the method of the logicians, and to clarify what is in it of stammering, halting, intellectual deficiency, and verbal incapacity, and to show that it is closer to corrupting intellectual and linguistic logic than to rectifying it. And they are not satisfied to adopt it in their reasoning and debates, neither with those they befriend nor with those they oppose. Its widespread use began only from the time of Abu Hamid al-Ghazali. For he was among the first to introduce logic into his book 'al-Mustasfa', and he claimed that he does not trust his knowledge except for one who knows this logic. And he authored on it 'Mi'yar al-'Ilm' and 'Mihakk al-Nazar'. And he authored a book called 'al-Qistas al-Mustaqim', in which he mentioned five scales: the three categorical forms, the conditional conjunctive, and the conditional disjunctive. He transformed their expressions into examples taken from the speech of the Muslims, and he claimed that he took those scales from the prophets, and he mentioned that he addressed with that some of the followers of the Batinites.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is a critical exposition by Ibn Taymiyya attacking the logicians' insistence on exactly two premises in every inference. He begins by stating the third station (maqam) of his critique: only the logicians commit to a strict two-premise requirement. He then explains that this requirement is arbitrary, since sometimes a single premise suffices, sometimes multiple are needed. He contrasts this with the practice of all other rational people—including the early Muslims (Muhajirun, Ansar, followers), scholars, theologians, grammarians, physicians, and geometers—who use whatever number of premises is naturally required without artificial constraint. He accuses the logicians of imitating the Greeks and criticizes their definitions composed of genus and differentia. Ibn Taymiyya then argues that Muslim theologians never bound themselves to two premises and always criticized the logicians' method for its deficiencies and corruption. Finally, he notes that the widespread use of logic in the Islamic world began with al-Ghazali, who introduced logic into usul al-fiqh in his 'al-Mustasfa', authored works on logic ('Mi'yar al-'Ilm', 'Mihakk al-Nazar'), and even wrote 'al-Qistas al-Mustaqim' which reframed syllogistic forms using Islamic examples from the Quran and hadith, claiming prophetic origin for the scales. This historical point serves to show that logic is a foreign innovation rather than an intrinsic part of Islamic sciences.

Refuting the Two-Premise Requirement
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the claim that every syllogism has exactly two premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's reductio ad absurdum against the logicians' claim that every syllogism needs exactly two premises.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya shows that the two-premise rule is arbitrary and leads to infinite regress.

Ibn Taymiyya directly challenges a foundational claim of Aristotelian logic: that every theoretical inquiry requires exactly two premises. In this passage, he uses a reductio ad absurdum to show that if one premise can be implicit, then any number can be, making the rule toothless. Let's walk through his argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
Find where the author states the claim that every theoretical inquiry depends on exactly two premises. This is the target of the refutation.
The claim is directly stated: 'ما يدعونه من توقف كل مطلوب نظرى على مقدمتين لا أكثر'
2
Understand qiyas al-damir
The logicians introduce the enthymeme (قياس الضمير) where one premise is omitted. Note the reasons for omission: known, error, or deception.
The author critiques this concept as an arbitrary exception.
3
Follow the reductio
Ibn Taymiyya argues: if one premise can be implicit because it is known, then two, three, or more can also be implicit. There is no principled limit.
This is a classic reductio: allowing one omission allows any number.
4
Recognize the lack of limit
The argument shows that the logicians cannot consistently allow one implicit premise without allowing infinite regress. Their rule collapses.
The author concludes: 'ليس لذلك حد' (there is no limit).
5
Contrast with eloquent speakers
The author points out that eloquent people (أهل البيان) produce certain proofs without needing such artificial restrictions.
This reinforces the argument that the rule is not necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمقصود هنا أن ما يدعونه من توقف كل مطلوب نظرى على مقدمتين لا أكثر ليس كذلك. دعواهم إضمار إحدى المقدمتين في «قياس الضمير» و هم يسمون القياس الذي حُذفت إحدى مقدمتيه «قياس الضمير»، ويقولون : إنها قد تُحذف إما للعلم بها، وإما غلطاً، أو تغليطاً. فيقال: إذا كانت معلومة كانت كغيرها من المقدمات المعلومة. وحينئذ فليس إضمار مقدمة بأولى من إضمار ثنتين وثلاثة وأربعة فإن جاز أن يدعى في الدليل الذي لا يحتاج إلا إلى مقدمة أن الأخرى مضمرة محذوفة جاز أن يدعى فيما يحتاج إلى ثنتين أن الثالثة محذوفة، وكذلك فيما يحتاج إلى ثلاث وليس لذلك حد. ومن تدبَّر هذا ووجد الأمر كذلك . وجود الرَّكَة والعِيّ في كلام أهل المنطق ولهذا لا يوجد في كلام البلغاء أهل البيان الذين يُقيمون البراهين والحُجَجَ اليقينية
And the intended point here is that what they claim—that every theoretical inquiry depends on two premises and no more—is not so. Their claim of concealing one of the two premises in the 'syllogism of implication'—and they call the syllogism in which one of its premises is omitted the 'syllogism of implication'—and they say that it may be omitted either because it is known, or due to error, or to deceive. So it is said: If it is known, then it is like other known premises. And then, the concealment of one premise is no more privileged than the concealment of two, three, or four. For if it is permissible to claim in a proof that needs only one premise that another is implicit and omitted, then it is permissible to claim in what needs two that the third is omitted, and likewise in what needs three, and there is no limit to that. And whoever contemplates this will find it to be so. The presence of stagnation and confusion in the speech of the logicians: and for this reason it is not found in the speech of eloquent people, the people of clear expression, who establish definitive proofs and arguments.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of the logicians' claim that every theoretical inquiry requires exactly two premises. The author begins by stating that this claim is false. He then discusses the concept of 'qiyas al-damir' (enthymeme), where one premise is omitted either because it is known, due to error, or to deceive. The author argues that if a premise can be omitted because it is known, then by the same logic, two or three premises could also be omitted, and there is no principled limit. The argument is a reductio ad absurdum: if you allow one implicit premise, you must allow an infinite regress, making the claim arbitrary. The author concludes by pointing to the 'stagnation and confusion' in logicians' speech, contrasting it with the clear speech of eloquent people who produce certain proofs without needing such technical distinctions. The passage demonstrates that the logicians' rules are not necessary for sound reasoning.

Ibn Taymiyya's reductio demonstrates that the logicians' two-premise rule is arbitrary and cannot be sustained.

Critique of the Enthymeme (Qiyas al-Damir)
Ibn Taymiyya critiques the logicians' use of the enthymeme to circumvent the two-premise requirement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's objection to the enthymeme and why it leads to an infinite regress.

Core Idea
The enthymeme arbitrarily hides premises; Ibn Taymiyya shows it can hide any number.

The logicians introduced the 'syllogism of implication' (qiyas al-damir) to handle arguments where one premise is omitted. Ibn Taymiyya seizes on this to expose the arbitrariness of their system. Let's dissect his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Define qiyas al-damir
According to the logicians, qiyas al-damir is a syllogism with one premise omitted. The omission may be due to knowledge, error, or deception.
This is the standard definition of an enthymeme in Aristotelian logic.
2
Analyze the reason for omission
If the premise is omitted because it is known, then it is just like any other known premise. Why should it be treated differently?
The author uses this to question the special status of the omitted premise.
3
Apply Ibn Taymiyya's reductio
If one premise can be hidden because it is known, then two, three, or any number can be hidden by the same logic. There is no limit.
The argument is: allowing one implicit premise allows any number, making the concept of qiyas al-damir arbitrary.
4
Conclude on the concept's viability
Ibn Taymiyya concludes that the concept of qiyas al-damir is not a principled logical category. It is an ad hoc device that cannot be consistently applied.
This undermines the logicians' attempt to salvage the two-premise requirement.

Source Text

And the intended point here is that what they claim—that every theoretical inquiry depends on two premises and no more—is not so. Their claim of concealing one of the two premises in the 'syllogism of implication'—and they call the syllogism in which one of its premises is omitted the 'syllogism of implication'—and they say that it may be omitted either because it is known, or due to error, or to deceive. And whoever contemplates this will find it to be so. The presence of stagnation and confusion in the speech of the logicians: and for this reason it is not found in the speech of eloquent people, the people of clear expression, who establish definitive proofs and arguments.

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of the logicians' claim that every theoretical inquiry requires exactly two premises. The author begins by stating that this claim is false. He then discusses the concept of 'qiyas al-damir' (enthymeme), where one premise is omitted either because it is known, due to error, or to deceive. The author argues that if a premise can be omitted because it is known, then by the same logic, two or three premises could also be omitted, and there is no principled limit. Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of the Two-Premise Rule
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the logicians' rule that every theoretical inquiry requires only two premises and attacks their concept of qiyas al-damir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument against the logicians' two-premise rule for theoretical proofs and explain why it is arbitrary.

Core Idea
If you can suppress one known premise, why not two or three?

The logicians claimed that every theoretical inquiry (matlub nazari) depends on exactly two premises. They used the concept of 'qiyas al-damir' — a syllogism with a suppressed premise — to explain cases that seem to have fewer. Ibn Taymiyya here attacks the arbitrariness of this rule: if one premise can be suppressed because it is known, then there is no principled limit to how many premises you can suppress. This undermines the entire claim. Let's walk through his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
State the logicians' claim
Identify the claim that every theoretical proof depends on exactly two premises. Note how they use qiyas al-damir to account for proofs that appear to have only one premise by claiming the second is implicit.
2
Understand qiyas al-damir
Qiyas al-damir is a syllogism where one premise is omitted because it is known, by mistake, or to deceive. The logicians use this to argue that any proof can be reduced to two explicit premises.
The omitted premise is considered 'implicit'.
3
Identify Ibn Taymiyya's objection
Ibn Taymiyya argues that if a known premise can be omitted, then there is no reason to stop at omitting just one. You could omit two, three, or four. Hence the number two is arbitrary.
He points out that the logicians have no principled limit.
4
Reflect on the implication
If there is no limit, then the claim that every proof needs exactly two premises is baseless. The rule collapses under its own logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمقصود هنا أن ما يدعونه من توقف كل مطلوب نظرى على مقدمتين لا أكثر ليس كذلك دعواهم إضمار إحدى المقدمتين في «قياس الضمير» و هم يسمون القياس الذي حُذفت إحدى مقدمتيه «قياس الضمير»، ويقولون : إنها قد تُحذف إما للعلم بها، وإما غلطاً، أو تغليطاً. فيقال: إذا كانت معلومة كانت كغيرها من المقدمات المعلومة. وحينئذ فليس إضمار مقدمة بأولى من إضمار ثنتين وثلاثة وأربعة فإن جاز أن يدعى في الدليل الذي لا يحتاج إلا إلى مقدمة أن الأخرى مضمرة محذوفة جاز أن يدعى فيما يحتاج إلى ثنتين أن الثالثة محذوفة، وكذلك فيما يحتاج إلى ثلاث وليس لذلك حد. ومن تدبَّر هذا وجد الأمر كذلك.
And the intended point here is that what they claim—that every theoretical inquiry depends on two premises, not more—is not so. Their claim is that one of the two premises is implicit in 'qiyas al-damir'. They call the syllogism from which one premise has been deleted 'qiyas al-damir', and they say: it may be deleted either because it is known, or by mistake, or to deceive. So it is said: if it is known, then it is like other known premises. Then omitting one premise is not more appropriate than omitting two, three, or four. For if it is permissible to claim in an argument that needs only one premise that the other is implicit and deleted, then it is permissible to claim in an argument that needs two that the third is deleted, and likewise for three, and there is no limit for that. And whoever reflects on this will find the matter to be so.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the logicians' claim that every theoretical inquiry (matlub nazari) depends on exactly two premises. Ibn Taymiyya targets their concept of 'qiyas al-damir'—the syllogism with a suppressed premise. He argues that if the suppression of a known premise is allowed, there is no principled reason to stop at two premises; one could just as easily suppress three or more. This makes their rule arbitrary and indefensible. The passage follows the author's own argumentative order: first stating the claim, then explaining the logicians' justification, then refuting it by showing the lack of a limit. The commentary does not introduce outside facts.

Key Terms
قياس الضمير (qiyas al-damir): A syllogism in which one premise is omitted (suppressed) either because it is known, or by mistake, or to deceive. The logicians use this to claim that every proof can be reduced to two explicit premises. [Note: In later logic, this term is less common; standard treatments focus on the explicit mood of the syllogism.]
مقدمة (muqaddima): A premise in a syllogism. Here it refers to the two premises that the logicians claim are necessary and sufficient for any theoretical proof. [Note: No significant shift; the term is stable.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because all proofs require three premises.
D) Because the logicians themselves do not follow this rule.

Key Takeaway
The logicians' two-premise rule is arbitrary because the logic of premise suppression has no inherent limit.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Logicians' Exclusive Claim on Demonstration
Ibn Taymiyya critiques logicians' error in the first figure and exclusive claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's distinction between affirming and negating errors in the logicians' system and explain why he rejects their exclusive claim that only demonstration yields theoretical knowledge.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya attacks the logicians' monopoly on defining valid demonstration, not logic itself.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya sets the stage for his broader critique by distinguishing between two types of errors: what the logicians wrongly affirm and what they wrongly deny. He focuses on the latter: their claim that only their form of demonstration (burhan) can yield theoretical knowledge. As you work through this lesson, you will learn to pinpoint this exclusive claim and understand why Ibn Taymiyya considers it a fundamental mistake that undermines the validity of other rational methods used in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two kinds of errors
Read the passage and notice that Ibn Taymiyya distinguishes between errors in affirmation (what they wrongly assert) and errors in negation (what they wrongly deny). He states clearly that his focus is on the latter.
The phrase 'ليس المقصود في هذا المقام بيان ما أخطأوا في إثباته، بل ما أخطأوا في نفيه' is key.
2
Locate the exclusive claim
Find the sentence where Ibn Taymiyya describes the logicians' claim that theoretical knowledge is only obtained through their demonstration. This is the error in negation he targets.
Look for 'زعموا أن العلم النظري لا يحصل إلا ببرهانهم'.
3
Understand the critique of the first figure
Ibn Taymiyya uses the first figure as an example. The logicians agree it contains apparent truths, but he argues they made the path lengthy and difficult without adding benefit. This illustrates their method's flaw.
The phrase 'طولوا فيه الطريق، وسلكوا الوعر والضيق' criticizes their unnecessary complexity.
4
Formulate Ibn Taymiyya's alternative position
Based on the critique, infer that Ibn Taymiyya accepts that valid knowledge can come from other forms of reasoning, such as analogical reasoning (qiyas) used in Islamic jurisprudence.
This is implicit; later sections of his work expand on this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وضلالهم في الإلهيات ظاهر لأكثر الناس، ولهذا كفّرهم فيها نظارُ المسلمين قاطبة . وإنما المنطق التبس الأمرُ فيه على طائفة لم يتصوّروا حقائقه ولوازمه، ولم يعرفوا ما قال سائر العقلاء في تناقضهم فيه . الشكل الأول واتفق أن فيه أموراً ظاهرة مثل الشكل الأول ، ولا يعرفون أن ما فيه من الحق لا يعرف بدون يحتاج إليهم فيه، بل طولوا فيه الطريق، وسلكوا الوَعِر والضيق، ولم يهتدوا فيه إلى ما يفيد المنطق التحقيق. وليس المقصود في هذا المقام بيان ما أخطأوا في إثباته، بل ما أخطأوا في نفيه، حيث زعموا أن العلم النظري لا يحصل إلا ببرهانهم، وهو من «القياس».
Their error in the divine matters is apparent to most people, and for this reason the Muslim theoreticians unanimously declared them disbelievers in that respect. As for logic, the matter became confused for a group who did not conceive its realities and necessary consequences, and did not know what other rational people said in their contradictions concerning it. The first figure: they agreed that it contains apparent matters like the first figure, but they do not know that the truth in it is not known without needing them in it; rather, they made the path lengthy, traversed the rough and narrow, and did not attain in it what benefits the logic of verification. The purpose in this context is not to show what they erred in affirming, but rather what they erred in negating, when they claimed that theoretical knowledge is only obtained through their demonstration, which is from 'syllogism'.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage is a critical opening where Ibn Taymiyya sets the stage for his attack on the logicians. He begins by noting that their errors in theology are obvious and have led to takfir by Muslim scholars. However, in logic the issue is more subtle: a group of people were confused because they did not fully grasp the realities and implications of logical concepts, nor were they aware of the contradictions among other rational thinkers. He then specifically mentions the first figure of the syllogism, pointing out that the logicians agree it contains apparent matters, but they fail to recognize that the truth within it does not require their elaborate methods; instead, they made the path unnecessarily long and difficult. The key point is that Ibn Taymiyya's aim here is not to correct what the logicians have incorrectly affirmed (their positive claims), but rather to reject what they have incorrectly negated: namely, their claim that theoretical knowledge can only be obtained through their form of demonstration (burhan), which they consider a type of syllogism (qiyas). In essence, he is arguing that the logicians have wrongly limited the means of acquiring certain knowledge.

Key Terms
المنطق
الشكل الأول
البرهان
القياس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main error of the logicians that Ibn Taymiyya focuses on?
A) Their error in affirming the first figure as valid.
B) Their error in negating that knowledge can come from other forms of reasoning besides their demonstration.
C) Their error in using Greek terminology.
D) Their error in declaring Muslims disbelievers.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's critique targets the logicians' exclusive claim that only their demonstration yields knowledge, not the validity of logic per se.

?
Identify the exclusive claim in the passage and explain why Ibn Taymiyya considers it an error in negation.
?
Find a real example from Islamic law where a ruling is derived via analogical reasoning (qiyas) and argue that it is a valid demonstration.

Ibn Taymiyya's Equivalence Thesis: Tamthil and Shumul
Ibn Taymiyya demonstrates equivalence of tamthil and shumul through multiple argument forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that analogical and categorical syllogisms are equivalent, and evaluate his critique of the logicians' hierarchy.

Core Idea
Material determines certainty, not form; all syllogistic forms yield the same result when the material is certain.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya directly challenges the logicians' classification of arguments. He argues that analogical and categorical syllogisms are not different in epistemic value—they are correlative. By presenting a single argument in multiple forms, he demonstrates that form is irrelevant; only the material matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim being attacked
The logicians claim that only categorical syllogism (qiyas shumul) yields certainty, while analogy (tamthil) does not. They classify arguments into three types: syllogism, induction, and analogy.
This is the target of Ibn Taymiyya's critique.
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's central thesis
He asserts that tamthil and shumul are correlative (mutalaziman): whatever knowledge or opinion is obtained by one, the same is obtained by the other if the material (mādda) is the same. Consideration is given to material, not form (sūra).
This is a direct rejection of the logicians' hierarchy.
3
Analyze the example of 'human' and its necessary attributes
Ibn Taymiyya uses the example of a human (insān) and its necessary concomitants (lawāzim) like being created, animal, sentient, etc. The relation is one of necessity: humanity necessitates these attributes.
The material here is the essential connection between humanity and these attributes.
4
Compare the five formulations
He lists five equivalent formulations: categorical ('This is a human, every human is created'), analogical ('He is human, so created like other humans'), conditional ('If he is human, then he is created'), and two others using necessity and disjunction. Each expresses the same logical core.
The variety demonstrates that form is interchangeable.
5
Assess the implication for the logicians' hierarchy
Since all forms convey the same certainty when the material is certain, the logicians' claim that only the categorical syllogism is demonstrative is unfounded. The material—the necessary relation between subject and predicate—is what matters.
This undermines the epistemic privilege of categorical syllogism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تلازم قياس الشمول وقياس التمثيل وبيانه بالأمثلة. وجعلوا أصناف الحجج ثلاثة - القياس، والاستقراء والتمثيل. وزعموا أن التمثيل لا يفيد اليقين، وإنما يفيده القياس الذي تكون مادته من القضايا التي ذكروها. وقد بينا في غير هذا الموضع أن قياس التمثيل و قياس الشمول متلازمان وأن ما حَصَل بأحدهما من علم أو ظنّ حَصَل بالآخر مثله إذا كانت المادة واحدة. والاعتبار بمادة العلم، لا بصورة القضيّة. بل إذا كانت المادة يقينية، فسواء كانت صورتها في صورة قياس التمثيل أو صورة قياس الشمول، فهي واحدة، وسواء كانت صورة القياس اقترانياً أو استثنائياً بعبارتهم أو بأي عبارة شئت، لا سيما في العبارات التي هي خير من عباراتهم وأبينُ في العقل، وأوجز في اللفظ، فالمعنى واحد. دليل واحد وحَدٌ هذا في أظهر الأمثلة إذا قلت: هذا إنسان، وكل إنسان مخلوق، أو حيوان، أو حسّاس، أو متحرك بالإرادة أو ناطق أو ما شئت من لوازم «الإنسان» . فإن شئت صوّرت الدليل على هذه الصورة وإن شئت قلت : هو إنسان، فهو مخلوق، أو حساس، أو حيوان، أو متحرك كغيره من الناس لاشتراكهما في الإنسانية المستلزمة لهذه الصفات. وإن شئت قلت: «هذا إنسان، والإنسانية مستلزمة لهذه الأحكام، فهي لازمة له». وإن شئت قلت : إن كان إنساناً فهو متّصف بهذه الصفات اللازمة للإنسان . وإن شئت قلت : إما أن يتصف بهذه الصفات وإما أن لا يتصف، والثاني باطل، فتعين الأول، لأن هذه لازمة للإنسان لا يتصوَّرُ وجوده بدونها.
The correlation of categorical syllogism and analogical syllogism and its explanation with examples. They made the types of arguments three: syllogism, induction, and analogy. They claimed that analogy does not yield certainty, but only the syllogism whose material is from the propositions they mentioned. We have explained elsewhere that analogical syllogism and categorical syllogism are correlative, and that whatever knowledge or opinion is obtained by one of them, the same is obtained by the other if the material is one. Consideration is given to the material of knowledge, not to the form of the proposition. Rather, if the material is certain, then whether its form is in the form of analogical syllogism or the form of categorical syllogism, it is one. And whether the form of the syllogism is conjunctive or exceptional in their terminology, or any expression you wish, especially expressions that are better than their expressions, clearer in the mind, and shorter in wording, the meaning is one. One proof and one limit. This is in the most apparent examples when you say: 'This is a human, and every human is created,' or 'animal,' or 'sentient,' or 'voluntary mover,' or 'rational,' or whatever you wish of the necessary concomitants of 'human.' If you wish, you formulate the proof in this form, and if you wish you say: 'He is a human, so he is created,' or 'sentient,' or 'animal,' or 'voluntary mover like other people due to their sharing in humanity which necessitates these attributes.' If you wish you say: 'This is a human, and humanity necessitates these judgments, so they are necessary for him.' If you wish you say: 'If he is a human, then he is characterized by these attributes necessary for the human.' If you wish you say: 'Either he is characterized by these attributes or he is not characterized, and the second is false, so the first is determined, because these are necessary for the human; his existence without them is inconceivable.'
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage is a critical exposition where Ibn Taymiyya argues that the logicians' classification of arguments into three types (syllogism, induction, analogy) and their claim that only the categorical syllogism (qiyas shumul) yields certainty is false. He asserts that analogical syllogism (qiyas tamthil) and categorical syllogism are correlative (mutalazimān): they produce the same result when the material (mādda) is the same. The determining factor is the material, not the form. If the material is certain, any form—categorical, analogical, conjunctive (iqtirānī), or exceptional (istithnā'ī)—conveys the same certainty. To illustrate, he uses the example of a human being (insān) and its necessary concomitants like being created, animal, sentient, etc. He then presents several equivalent formulations: a categorical syllogism (major premise: every human is created; minor: this is a human; conclusion: this is created), an analogical form (this is human, therefore created like other humans), a conditional (if human, then created), and a disjunctive (either created or not, not created is false, so created). This demonstrates that the logical core is the same—the necessary relation (luzūm) between humanity and being created—regardless of linguistic or logical form. Thus, the logicians' hierarchy is unfounded.

The Evidence-Ruling Relationship in Syllogism
Ibn Taymiyya explains the relationship between evidence, ruling, subject, and predicate in syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the relationship between evidence (dalil) and ruling (hukm) in a syllogism and determine whether the evidence is properly related to the ruling.

Core Idea
Evidence in a syllogism is never more general than the ruling it supports.

Ibn Taymiyya establishes a fundamental rule: the evidence (middle term) must be either equal in scope to the ruling or more specific than it. This rule ensures that the ruling follows logically from the evidence. In this lesson, we will dissect this rule and apply it to real examples from Islamic legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three elements
In any syllogism, identify the evidence (dalil), the ruling (hukm), and the subject (al-mawsuf). In the example, evidence = khamr, ruling = haram, subject = nabidh.
2
State Ibn Taymiyya's rule
The evidence cannot be more general than the ruling. It is either equal in generality or more specific. Check the example: khamr is more specific than haram, so the rule holds.
3
Test a counterexample
Imagine a claim: 'Nabidh is permissible because it is a drink.' Here evidence = drink, ruling = permissible. Drink is more general than permissible, violating the rule.
Such an inference would be invalid.
4
Apply to a new qiyas
Take the syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden. Grape wine is an intoxicant. Therefore grape wine is forbidden.' Identify evidence and ruling. Does the rule hold?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإما أن يكون أعم منه فالدليل إما أن يكون مساوياً للحكم المدلول في العموم والخصوص، وإما يكون أخص منه، لا يكون الدليل أعم منه. «القياس استدلال بكلي على ثبوت كلي آخر لجزئيات ذلك الكلي وإذا قالوا في القياس»: يستدل بالكلي على الجزئي، فليس الجزئي هو الحكم المدلول عليه، وإنما الجزئي هو الموصوف، المخبر عنه، محل الحكم، فهذا قد يكون أخص من الدليل، وقد يكون مساوياً له بخلاف الحكم الذي هو صفة هذا وحكمه الذي أخبر به عنه، فإنه لا يكون إلا أعم من الدليل، أو مساوياً له. فإن ذلك هو المدلول اللازم للدليل والدليل، هو لازم المخبر عنه، لازم المخبر عنه الموصوف. فإذا قيل: النبيذ حرام، لأنه خمر فكونه خمراً هو الدليل، وهو لازم للنبيذ، و«التحريم» لازم للخمر. ليس القياس المؤلّف من المقدمتين إذا قلت: «النبيذ المتنازع فيه مسكر أو خمر، وكل مسكر أو كل خمر حرام، فأنت لم تستدل بـ المسكر أو الخمر» الذي هو كلي على نفس محل النزاع الذي هو أخص من الخمر، وهو «النبيذ»؛ فليس هو استدلالاً بذلك الكلي على هذا الجزئي، بل استدللت به على تحريم هذا النبيذ». فلما هذا النبيذ مندرجاً في تحريم كل مسكر قال من قال: إنه «استدلال بالكلي على الجزئي والتحقيقُ أن ما ثبت للكلي فقد ثبت لكل واحد من جزئياته. و «التحريم هو أعم من الخمر، وهو ثابت لها، فهو ثابت لكل فرد فرد من جزئياتها. فهو استدلال بكلي على ثبوت كلي آخر لجزئيات ذلك الكلي، وذلك الدليل هو كالجزئي بالنسبة إلى ذلك الكلي الذي هو الحكم، وهو كلي بالنسبة إلى تلك الجزئيات التي هي المحكوم عليها. وهذا مما لا ينازعون فيه. فإن الدليل هو «الحد الأوسط»، وهو أعم من «الأصغر» أو مساو له، و«الأكبر» أعم منه أو مساو له. و«الأكبر» هو الحكم، والصفة، والخبر، وهو محمول النتيجة و«الأصغر» هو المحكوم عليه الموصوف، المبتدأ، وهو موضوع النتيجة.
Or it may be more general than it. So the evidence is either equal to the indicated ruling in generality and specificity, or it is more specific than it; the evidence cannot be more general than it. 'The syllogism is an inference from a universal to the establishment of another universal for the particulars of that universal.' And when they say concerning the syllogism: 'one infers from the universal to the particular,' the particular is not the ruling that is indicated; rather the particular is the described entity, the thing informed about, the locus of the ruling. So this may be more specific than the evidence, and may be equal to it, unlike the ruling which is the attribute of this and its judgment that is informed about concerning it, for it is only more general than the evidence or equal to it. For that is the indicated consequent of the evidence, and the evidence is a necessary concomitant of the thing informed about, a necessary concomitant of the described thing. So if it is said: 'Nabidh is forbidden, because it is khamr,' then its being khamr is the evidence, and it is a necessary concomitant of nabidh, and the 'prohibition' is a necessary concomitant of khamr. The syllogism composed of two premises when you say: 'The disputed nabidh is an intoxicant or khamr, and every intoxicant or every khamr is forbidden,' you have not inferred by means of 'the intoxicant or khamr' which is a universal about the very subject of dispute which is more specific than khamr, namely 'nabidh'; so it is not an inference by that universal about this particular, but rather you inferred by it about the prohibition of this nabidh. Since this nabidh is included in the prohibition of every intoxicant, someone said: it is 'inference from the universal to the particular.' And the investigation is: what is established for the universal is established for each one of its particulars. And 'prohibition' is more general than khamr, and it is established for it, so it is established for every individual of its particulars. So it is an inference from a universal to the establishment of another universal for the particulars of that universal, and that evidence is like the particular relative to that universal which is the ruling, and it is universal relative to those particulars which are the subjects of the ruling. And this is something they do not dispute. For the evidence is the 'middle term,' and it is more general than the 'minor term' or equal to it, and the 'major term' is more general than it or equal to it. And the 'major term' is the ruling, the attribute, the predicate, and it is the predicate of the conclusion. And the 'minor term' is the subject of the ruling, the described, the subject of the proposition, and it is the subject of the conclusion.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage is a critical clarification by Ibn Taymiyya regarding the nature of syllogistic reasoning. He begins by establishing a general rule about the relationship between evidence (dalil) and the ruling (hukm) being indicated: the evidence cannot be more general than the ruling; it must be either equal or more specific. He then addresses the common logicians' claim that syllogism is 'inference from the universal to the particular.' He refines this by distinguishing between the particular that is the subject of the ruling (al-mawsuf, al-mukhbar 'anhu) and the ruling itself. The subject may be more specific than the evidence or equal, but the ruling is always more general or equal. To illustrate, he uses the example: 'Nabidh is forbidden because it is khamr.' Here, 'being khamr' is the evidence (middle term), and it is a necessary attribute of nabidh; 'forbiddenness' is a necessary attribute of khamr. So the syllogism is not simply inferring from the universal 'khamr' to the particular 'this nabidh' as the subject, but rather from the universal 'khamr' to the universal 'forbidden' as a ruling that applies to all particulars of khamr, including nabidh. He thus rephrases the definition: syllogism is inference from a universal to the establishment of another universal for the particulars of the first universal. He then identifies the middle term (al-hadd al-awsat) as the evidence, which is either more general than the minor term (al-asghar) or equal; and the major term (al-akbar) is either more general than the middle or equal. The major term is the ruling/predicate, and the minor term is the subject. This aligns with standard Aristotelian terminology but with a clearer focus on the ontological relationship.

Key Terms
دليل
حكم
كلي
جزئي

A) The evidence is always more general than the ruling.

C) The evidence is never more general than the ruling; it is either equal or more specific.
D) The evidence can be either more general or more specific than the ruling.

Key Takeaway
In a valid syllogism, the evidence (middle term) must be either equal in scope to the ruling (major term) or more specific, but never more general.

Ibn Taymiyya's Corrected Definition of Syllogism
Ibn Taymiyya explains the relationship between evidence, ruling, subject, and predicate in syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to articulate Ibn Taymiyya's corrected definition of syllogism and identify the three terms (minor, middle, major) in a syllogism along with their scope relations.

Core Idea
Syllogism is inference from a universal to the establishment of another universal for the particulars of the first universal.

Ibn Taymiyya offers a precise correction to the standard definition of syllogism. He argues that the particular is always the subject, not the conclusion, and the logical movement is from one universal (the evidence) to another universal (the ruling) that applies to all particulars of the first. This lesson will walk through his redefinition and its implications for understanding qiyas in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Recall the logicians' definition
Many logicians define syllogism as 'inference from the universal to the particular.' Ibn Taymiyya critiques this as imprecise.
2
Identify the particular in the example
In 'Nabidh is forbidden because it is khamr,' the particular is 'nabidh' (the subject), not 'forbidden' (the ruling).
So the inference is not directly to the particular, but through the universal ruling.
3
Understand Ibn Taymiyya's redefinition
He defines syllogism as 'inference from a universal to the establishment of another universal for the particulars of that universal.'
The first universal is the evidence (khamr), the second is the ruling (forbidden), and the particulars are the subjects (like nabidh).
4
Map the terms
Identify the three terms: minor (asghar) = subject of conclusion (nabidh), middle (hadd al-awsat) = evidence (khamr), major (akbar) = ruling (forbidden). Check scope: middle > minor? khamr is more general than nabidh, yes. Major > middle? forbidden is more general than khamr, yes.
These scope relations ensure the validity of the syllogism.

And this is something they do not dispute. For the evidence is the 'middle term,' and it is more general than the 'minor term' or equal to it, and the 'major term' is more general than it or equal to it. And the 'major term' is the ruling, the attribute, the predicate, and it is the predicate of the conclusion. And the 'minor term' is the subject of the ruling, the described, the subject of the proposition, and it is the subject of the conclusion.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage is a critical clarification by Ibn Taymiyya regarding the nature of syllogistic reasoning. He begins by establishing a general rule about the relationship between evidence (dalil) and the ruling (hukm) being indicated: the evidence cannot be more general than the ruling; it must be either equal or more specific. He then addresses the common logicians' claim that syllogism is 'inference from the universal to the particular.' He refines this by distinguishing between the particular that is the subject of the ruling (al-mawsuf, al-mukhbar 'anhu) and the ruling itself. The subject may be more specific than the evidence or equal, but the ruling is always more general or equal. To illustrate, he uses the example: 'Nabidh is forbidden because it is khamr.' Here, 'being khamr' is the evidence (middle term), and it is a necessary attribute of nabidh; 'forbiddenness' is a necessary attribute of khamr. So the syllogism is not simply inferring from the universal 'khamr' to the particular 'this nabidh' as the subject, but rather from the universal 'khamr' to the universal 'forbidden' as a ruling that applies to all particulars of khamr, including nabidh. He thus rephrases the definition: syllogism is inference from a universal to the establishment of another universal for the particulars of the first universal. He then identifies the middle term (al-hadd al-awsat) as the evidence, which is either more general than the minor term (al-asghar) or equal; and the major term (al-akbar) is either more general than the middle or equal. The major term is the ruling/predicate, and the minor term is the subject. This aligns with standard Aristotelian terminology but with a clearer focus on the ontological relationship.

أصغر
أكبر

حكم
كلي
جزئي

B) Inference from the particular to the universal.
C) Inference from a universal to the establishment of another universal for the particulars of that universal.
D) Inference from two premises to a conclusion about a particular.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya redefines syllogism as inference from a universal (evidence) to the establishment of another universal (ruling) for the particulars of the evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden. Grape wine is an intoxicant. Therefore grape wine is forbidden.' Identify the three terms and state the scope relations. Definition of Qiyas al-Tamthil and Refutation of Particular-to-Particular Claim
Definition and refutation in qiyas al-tamthil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the misconception of analogy as mere particular-to-particular inference and the correct definition that includes a middle term (the common cause).

Core Idea
Analogy is not just similarity; it requires a shared cause that serves as the middle term.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya defines 'qiyas al-tamthil' (analogical reasoning) and corrects a common misunderstanding. Many assume analogy is simply inferring from one particular case to another without any connecting principle. The author insists that it operates through a middle term – the shared cause of the judgment. Let's break down his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of qiyas al-tamthil
Find the sentence where the author defines qiyas al-tamthil. He says it is 'the sharing of the two particulars in the cause of the judgment.' This means the analogy relies on a common reason (ʿilla) for the ruling.
The definition is repeated for emphasis.
2
Locate the false claim being refuted
Find the phrase 'استدلال بجزئي على جزئي' (inference from a particular to a particular). The author points out that if this is taken absolutely without a middle term, it is false.
This is the misconception many logicians and jurists might hold.
3
Understand the author's refutation
The author argues that qiyas al-tamthil only indicates through a middle term, which is the common cause or its indicator. Without this middle term, the inference would be arbitrary.
Thus, analogy is not mere comparison but must be grounded in a shared ratio legis.
4
Distinguish between Qiyas ʿIlla and Qiyas Dalala
The author mentions two types: Qiyas ʿIlla (cause-based) uses the cause itself; Qiyas Dalala (indication-based) uses a sign of the cause. Both still rely on a middle term.
This distinction is important in usul al-fiqh.

The third station - Qiyas al-Tamthil is the sharing of the two particulars in the cause of the judgment. And it is the predicate of the conclusion, and the minor term is the subject, the described, the starting point, and it is the subject of the conclusion. Qiyas al-Tamthil is the sharing of the two particulars in the cause of the judgment. As for their statement regarding al-Tamthil: that it is inference from a particular to a particular, if that is uttered absolutely and it is said: it is inference by mere particular to a particular, then it is false. For Qiyas al-Tamthil only indicates through a middle term, which is their sharing in the cause of the judgment, or the indicator of the judgment along with the cause, so it is a Qiyas of cause, or a Qiyas of indication.

Commentary
This passage defines Qiyas al-Tamthil and refutes a common misconception. The author begins by stating that Qiyas al-Tamthil is the sharing of two particular cases in the cause (or ratio legis) of the judgment. He also clarifies that the major term is the predicate of the conclusion, and the minor term is the subject. Then he reiterates the definition. He then criticizes the claim that analogy is inference from particular to particular without any middle term. He argues that if this is taken as mere particular-to-particular inference without a middle term, it is false. In reality, Qiyas al-Tamthil operates through a middle term, which is the common cause (or its indication). The author distinguishes between Qiyas 'Illa (cause-based analogy) and Qiyas Dalala (indication-based analogy).

The Syllogism

All Syllogisms Reduce to Entailment: Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Formalist Logic
Ibn Taymiyya argues all syllogisms reduce to entailment, then explains conditional types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze Ibn Taymiyya's argument that categorical and conditional syllogisms reduce to the same entailment relation and explain why he prioritizes material content over logical form.

Core Idea
All syllogistic forms are just expressions of entailment; matter matters most.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the logicians' division of syllogisms into three types. He argues that whether a syllogism is categorical (iqtirani) or conditional (istithna'i), both ultimately depend on a single relation: the entailment (luzum) of the indicated by the evidence. Moreover, he insists that the material content (madda) of the argument—what you actually know—is what gives certainty, not the artificial form (sura) imposed by Greek logic. This lesson unpacks his critique and its implications for Islamic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify Ibn Taymiyya's central thesis
Locate the sentence where he says both iqtirani and istithna'i return to one meaning. What is that meaning?
Look for the phrase 'عود الاقتراني والاستثنائي إلى معنى واحد'.
2
Examine the interconvertibility claim
Find where he says each can be represented in the form of the other. Why does this show they are fundamentally the same?
Consider how a categorical 'Every A is B' can be expressed as 'If A then B'.
3
Analyze the priority of matter over form
Look at his statement: 'The matter is not known from the forms... whoever knows the matter knows the indication.' What is he rejecting?
He rejects the notion that formal validity alone gives knowledge.
4
Evaluate the epistemological conclusion
He says Muslim expressions are 'vastly better' than the logicians' formulas. What does that tell us about his view of logical formalism?
Consider the role of natural language and intuitive knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقد تبين بعض ما في حضرهم من الخلل. وأما تقسيمهم إلى الأنواع الثلاثة فكلها تعود إلى ما ذكروه في استلزام الدليل للمدلول. عود «الاقتراني» و «الاستثنائي إلى معنى واحد وما ذكروه في «الاقتراني» يمكن تصويره بصورة الاستثنائي»، وكذلك «الاستثنائي» يمكن تصويره بصورة الاقتراني». فيعود الأمر إلى معنى واحد، وهو مادة الدليل . والمادة لا تُعلم من صور القياس» الذي ذكروه . بل مَنْ عرف المادة بحيث يعلم أن هذا مستلزم لهذا عَلِمَ الدلالة، سواء صُوِّرت بصورة «القياس» أو لم تصور، وسواء عُبّر عنها بعباراتهم أو بغيرها، بل العبارات التي صقلتها عقولُ المسلمين وألسنتهم خير من عباراتهم بكثير كثير . و «الاقتراني) كله يعود إلى لزوم هذا لهذا، وهذا لهذا، كما ذكر. وهذا بعينه هو «الاستثنائي» المؤلف من «المتصل» و «المنفصل». فإن الشرطي المتصل» استدلال باللزوم بثبوت الملزوم الذي هو المقدَّم، الشرط، على ثبوت اللازم الذي هو التالي، وهو الجزاء؛ أو بانتفاء اللازم وهو التالي الذي هو الجزاء، على انتفاء الملزوم الذي هو المقدّم، وهو الشرط . وأما الشرطي المنفصل»، وهو الذي يسميه الأصوليون السَّبر والتقسيم»، وقد تسميه أيضاً الجدليون التقسيم والترديد، فمضمونه الاستدلال بثبوت أحد النقيضين على انتفاء الآخر، وبانتفائه على ثبوته ؛ أو الاستدلال بثبوت أحد الضدين على انتفاء الآخر. وأقسامه أربعة . ولهذا كان في مانعة الجمع والخلو الاستثناءات الأربعة : وهو أنه إن ثبت هذا انتفى نقيضه، وكذلك الآخر ؛ وإن انتفى هذا ثبت نقيضه، وكذلك الآخر. و مانعة الجمع» الاستدلال بثبوت أحد الضدّين على انتفاء الآخر، والأمران متنافیان. و «مانعةُ الخلو» فيها تناقض ولزوم، والنقيضان لا يرتفعان، فمنعت الخلو منهما. ولكن جزاءها وجود شيء وعدم آخر، ليس هو وجود الشيء وعدمه. ووجود مانعة الخلو شيء وعدم آخر يكون أحدهما لازماً للآخر، وإن كانا لا يرتفعان، لأن ارتفاعهما يقتضي ارتفاع وجود شيء وعدمه معاً.
It has become clear some of the flaws in what they have. As for their division into three types, all of them return to what they mentioned regarding the entailment of the evidence (dalil) for the indicated (madlul). The return of 'connective' (iqtirani) and 'exceptive' (istithna'i) to one meaning. What they mentioned about the 'connective' can be represented in the form of the 'exceptive', and likewise the 'exceptive' can be represented in the form of the 'connective'. So the matter returns to one meaning, which is the matter (madda) of the evidence. And the matter is not known from the forms of syllogism that they mentioned. Rather, whoever knows the matter such that he knows that this entails this knows the indication, whether it is represented in the form of a 'syllogism' or not, and whether it is expressed in their terms or others. Indeed, the expressions refined by the minds and tongues of Muslims are vastly better than their expressions. And the 'connective' all returns to the entailment of this for this, and this for this, as mentioned. And this very thing is the 'exceptive' composed of the 'connected' and 'disjunctive' [conditionals]. For the 'connected conditional' (sharti muttasil) is an argument by entailment: through affirming the antecedent (malzum) which is the condition (muqaddam) to affirm the consequent (lazim) which is the apodosis (tali), i.e., the conclusion; or through denying the consequent which is the apodosis to deny the antecedent which is the condition. As for the 'disjunctive conditional' (sharti munfasil), which the usuliyyun call 'al-sabr wa al-taqsim' and which the dialecticians also call 'al-taqsim wa al-tardid', its content is argumentation through affirming one of two contradictories to deny the other, and through denying it to affirm the other; or argumentation through affirming one of two contraries to deny the other. Its divisions are four. For this reason, in the 'preventive of conjunction and gap' (mani'at al-jam' wa al-khuluw), the exceptions are four: that if this is affirmed, its contradictory is negated, and likewise the other; and if this is negated, its contradictory is affirmed, and likewise the other. And 'preventive of conjunction' (mani'at al-jam') is argumentation through affirming one of two contraries to deny the other, and the two matters are contradictory (mutanafiyan). And 'preventive of gap' (mani'at al-khuluw) involves contradiction and entailment, and the two contradictories cannot both be absent, so it prevents the gap from both. But its apodosis (jaza') is the existence of something and the non-existence of something else, not the existence and non-existence of the same thing. And the existence in 'preventive of gap' is something and non-existence of something else, one being entailed by the other, even though they cannot both be absent, because their simultaneous absence would entail the simultaneous absence of existence of something and non-existence of something else.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage is a critical and explanatory discussion of the logicians' classification of syllogisms. Ibn Taymiyya first notes that the three types of syllogism mentioned by logicians all reduce to the fundamental relation of entailment (luzum) between evidence and indicated. He then focuses on the two main forms: connective (iqtirani) and exceptive (istithna'i), arguing they are mutually interconvertible. The essence is the material (madda), not the form. He privileges the natural language of Muslims over the artificial forms of the logicians. Next, he explains that the exceptive syllogism is composed of two types of conditionals: connected (muttasil) and disjunctive (munfasil). For the connected conditional, the argument proceeds by affirming the antecedent or denying the consequent. For the disjunctive, it is based on either-or relations. He notes that the usuliyyun call the disjunctive 'al-sabr wa al-taqsim' (the method of division and elimination), and dialecticians call it 'taqsim wa tardid'. The disjunctive has four modes, corresponding to the types of disjunction: 'mani'at al-jam'' (preventive of conjunction), 'mani'at al-khuluw' (preventive of gap), and 'mani'at al-jam' wa al-khuluw' (preventive of both). He explains that in 'mani'at al-jam' wa al-khuluw', there are four valid exceptions: affirming one yields the denial of the other, and denying one yields the affirmation of the other. In 'mani'at al-jam'', the two things are contraries (diddayn) and mutually exclusive but not exhaustive; affirming one negates the other. In 'mani'at al-khuluw', the two contradictories cannot both be absent; it involves entailment such that the existence of one entails the non-existence of the other, and vice versa, but they are not mutually exclusive in the sense of possibility; the apodosis is not the existence and non-existence of the same thing but rather existence of one and non-existence of another. For example, 'Either this act is obligatory or it is forbidden' (preventive of conjunction) vs 'Either it is an obligation or it is not an obligation' (preventive of gap) vs 'Either it is permissible or it is obligatory' (both). In Islamic logic, these are applied in fiqh reasoning like sabr wa taqsim. Overall, Ibn Taymiyya aims to demystify the syllogistic forms and show they are fundamentally about concrete entailments known through material knowledge, not formal manipulation.

Key Terms
اقتراني
استثنائي
لزوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Conjunction
B) Entailment
C) Negation
D) Analogy

Key Takeaway
All syllogisms are reducible to entailment; the form matters less than the material knowledge of what entails what.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take a simple categorical syllogism from Islamic jurisprudence, such as: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; khamr is an intoxicant; therefore khamr is forbidden.' According to Ibn Taymiyya, what is the fundamental entailment relation here? Rewrite it as a conditional statement (if-then) to show it is the same relation.

The Connected Conditional: Modus Ponens and Modus Tollens
Ibn Taymiyya explains connected conditional as argument by entailment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct valid conditional arguments (modus ponens and modus tollens) using Islamic juridical examples.

Core Idea
The connected conditional is simply entailment in action: if the antecedent holds, so does the consequent.

Ibn Taymiyya explains that the 'connected conditional' (sharti muttasil) is an argument by entailment. There are two valid forms: affirming the antecedent to affirm the consequent (modus ponens) and denying the consequent to deny the antecedent (modus tollens). These are not arbitrary Greek inventions; they correspond to how Muslims naturally reason about rulings and causes. This lesson will walk you through these two forms and show how they appear in Islamic legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Define the connected conditional
What is a 'sharti muttasil'? Identify its components: antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tali).
It is an 'if...then...' statement.
2
Analyze modus ponens
Find the phrase: 'through affirming the antecedent to affirm the consequent.' Write an Islamic example: 'If the act is intoxicating, it is forbidden. This act is intoxicating, therefore it is forbidden.'
This is the form of the first two propositions.
3
Analyze modus tollens
Find the phrase: 'or through denying the consequent to deny the antecedent.' Example: 'If it is intoxicating, it is forbidden. This act is not forbidden, therefore it is not intoxicating.'
This is the form of the last two propositions.
4
Check validity
Why are these the only two valid forms? Why can't we affirm the consequent or deny the antecedent? Use a fiqh example to show the fallacy.
Fallacy example: 'If intoxicating, then forbidden; it is forbidden, therefore it is intoxicating' is invalid because there could be other reasons for prohibition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

It has become clear some of the flaws in what they have. As for their division into three types, all of them return to what they mentioned regarding the entailment of the evidence (dalil) for the indicated (madlul). The return of 'connective' (iqtirani) and 'exceptive' (istithna'i) to one meaning. What they mentioned about the 'connective' can be represented in the form of the 'exceptive', and likewise the 'exceptive' can be represented in the form of the 'connective'. So the matter returns to one meaning, which is the matter (madda) of the evidence. And the matter is not known from the forms of syllogism that they mentioned. Rather, whoever knows the matter such that he knows that this entails this knows the indication, whether it is represented in the form of a 'syllogism' or not, and whether it is expressed in their terms or others. Indeed, the expressions refined by the minds and tongues of Muslims are vastly better than their expressions. And the 'connective' all returns to the entailment of this for this, and this for this, as mentioned. And this very thing is the 'exceptive' composed of the 'connected' and 'disjunctive' [conditionals]. For the 'connected conditional' (sharti muttasil) is an argument by entailment: through affirming the antecedent (malzum) which is the condition (muqaddam) to affirm the consequent (lazim) which is the apodosis (tali), i.e., the conclusion; or through denying the consequent which is the apodosis to deny the antecedent which is the condition. As for the 'disjunctive conditional' (sharti munfasil), which the usuliyyun call 'al-sabr wa al-taqsim' and which the dialecticians also call 'al-taqsim wa al-tardid', its content is argumentation through affirming one of two contradictories to deny the other, and through denying it to affirm the other; or argumentation through affirming one of two contraries to deny the other. Its divisions are four. For this reason, in the 'preventive of conjunction and gap' (mani'at al-jam' wa al-khuluw), the exceptions are four: that if this is affirmed, its contradictory is negated, and likewise the other; and if this is negated, its contradictory is affirmed, and likewise the other. And 'preventive of conjunction' (mani'at al-jam') is argumentation through affirming one of two contraries to deny the other, and the two matters are contradictory (mutanafiyan). And 'preventive of gap' (mani'at al-khuluw) involves contradiction and entailment, and the two contradictories cannot both be absent, so it prevents the gap from both. But its apodosis (jaza') is the existence of something and the non-existence of something else, not the existence and non-existence of the same thing. And the existence in 'preventive of gap' is something and non-existence of something else, one being entailed by the other, even though they cannot both be absent, because their simultaneous absence would entail the simultaneous absence of existence of something and non-existence of something else.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage is a critical and explanatory discussion of the logicians' classification of syllogisms. Ibn Taymiyya first notes that the three types of syllogism mentioned by logicians all reduce to the fundamental relation of entailment (luzum) between evidence and indicated. He then focuses on the two main forms: connective (iqtirani) and exceptive (istithna'i), arguing they are mutually interconvertible. The essence is the material (madda), not the form. He privileges the natural language of Muslims over the artificial forms of the logicians. Next, he explains that the exceptive syllogism is composed of two types of conditionals: connected (muttasil) and disjunctive (munfasil). For the connected conditional, the argument proceeds by affirming the antecedent or denying the consequent. For the disjunctive, it is based on either-or relations. He notes that the usuliyyun call the disjunctive 'al-sabr wa al-taqsim' (the method of division and elimination), and dialecticians call it 'taqsim wa tardid'. The disjunctive has four modes, corresponding to the types of disjunction: 'mani'at al-jam'' (preventive of conjunction), 'mani'at al-khuluw' (preventive of gap), and 'mani'at al-jam' wa al-khuluw' (preventive of both). He explains that in 'mani'at al-jam' wa al-khuluw', there are four valid exceptions: affirming one yields the denial of the other, and denying one yields the affirmation of the other. In 'mani'at al-jam'', the two things are contraries (diddayn) and mutually exclusive but not exhaustive; affirming one negates the other. In 'mani'at al-khuluw', the two contradictories cannot both be absent; it involves entailment such that the existence of one entails the non-existence of the other, and vice versa, but they are not mutually exclusive in the sense of possibility; the apodosis is not the existence and non-existence of the same thing but rather existence of one and non-existence of another. For example, 'Either this act is obligatory or it is forbidden' (preventive of conjunction) vs 'Either it is an obligation or it is not an obligation' (preventive of gap) vs 'Either it is permissible or it is obligatory' (both). In Islamic logic, these are applied in fiqh reasoning like sabr wa taqsim. Overall, Ibn Taymiyya aims to demystify the syllogistic forms and show they are fundamentally about concrete entailments known through material knowledge, not formal manipulation.

The Disjunctive Conditional and Sabr wa Taqsim: Four Modes of Exclusion
Ibn Taymiyya explains disjunctive conditional and its relation to sabr wa taqsim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four modes of disjunctive conditional and connect them to the usuli method of sabr wa taqsim.

Core Idea
The disjunctive conditional is the logical backbone of the jurist's method of eliminating possibilities to identify the cause ('illa).

Ibn Taymiyya identifies the disjunctive conditional (sharti munfasil) as the same as al-sabr wa al-taqsim used by usuliyyun. He describes four modes: the preventive of conjunction (mani'at al-jam'), the preventive of gap (mani'at al-khuluw), and the combined type. These correspond to different logical relationships between the disjuncts. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for correctly applying the elimination method in legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive conditional's basic structure
It is an 'either A or B' statement. What does 'sabr wa taqsim' mean? (Testing and division.)
This method is used in usul al-fiqh to find the 'illa.
2
Distinguish the three types of disjunction
Mani'at al-jam' (exclusive but not exhaustive), mani'at al-khuluw (exhaustive but not exclusive), and both. Give examples from fiqh.
Example of mani'at al-jam': 'Either this act is obligatory or forbidden.' Not exhaustive (could be permissible).
3
Examine the four valid exceptions for the combined type
How does affirming one allow denial of the other, and denying one allow affirmation of the other?
This is the 'preventive of conjunction and gap' (mani'at al-jam' wa al-khuluw).
4
Connect to sabr wa taqsim in ijtihad
A mujtahid lists possible causes ('ills) for a ruling, then eliminates all but one. How does that use disjunctive logic?
The elimination process corresponds to affirming/denying disjuncts.
