Conjunctive Conditionals as Inference by Implication
Explains inference in conjunctive conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two valid forms of inference in conjunctive conditionals (affirming antecedent, denying consequent) and distinguish them from fallacious forms.

Core Idea
Conjunctive conditionals reduce to implication: if A then B means A implies B.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya explains the conjunctive conditional as an inference by implication (luzum). Understanding this reduction is key to seeing how all syllogisms, whether categorical or conditional, share a common logical core. We will focus on the valid directional inferences and their fallsacies.

Guided Path
1
Identify Antecedent and Consequent
Locate the part that states the condition (muqaddam) and the part that states the result (tali). In 'If the world is created, then it has a creator', the antecedent is 'the world is created' and the consequent is 'it has a creator'.
Also called 'shart' and 'jaza'.
2
Apply Modus Ponens
When the antecedent is affirmed, the consequent follows. This is the 'affirming the antecedent' inference. Check if the passage uses this pattern.
Ibn Taymiyya: 'affirmation of the implied to the affirmation of the necessary'.
3
Apply Modus Tollens
When the consequent is denied, the antecedent is denied. This is the 'denying the consequent' inference. Identify if the passage uses this pattern.
Ibn Taymiyya: 'negation of the necessary to the negation of the implied'.
4
Recognize Invalid Forms
Note that affirming the consequent or denying the antecedent are fallacious. The conjunctive conditional only supports these two valid directions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثالث - عودة الاقتراني» و «الاستثنائي إلى معنى واحد «الاستثنائي» المؤلف من «المتصل» و «المنفصل». الشرطي وهو المتصل فإن الشرطي المتصل» استدلال باللزوم بثبوت الملزوم الذي هو المقدَّم، الشرط، على ثبوت اللازم الذي هو التالي، وهو الجزاء؛ أو بانتفاء اللازم وهو التالي الذي هو الجزاء، على انتفاء الملزوم الذي هو المقدّم، وهو الشرط . وأما الشرطي المنفصل»، وهو الذي يسميه الأصوليون السَّبر والتقسيم»، وقد تسميه أيضاً الجدليون التقسيم والترديد، فمضمونه الاستدلال بثبوت أحد النقيضين على انتفاء الآخر، وبانتفائه على ثبوته ؛ أو الاستدلال بثبوت أحد الضدين على انتفاء الآخر. وأقسامه أربعة . ولهذا كان في مانعة الجمع والخلو الاستثناءات الأربعة : وهو أنه إن ثبت هذا انتفى نقيضه، وكذلك الآخر ؛ وإن انتفى هذا ثبت نقيضه، وكذلك الآخر . و مانعة الجمع» الاستدلال بثبوت أحد الضدّين على انتفاء الآخر، والأمران متنافیان. و «مانعةُ الخلو» فيها تناقض ولزوم، والنقيضان لا يرتفعان، فمنعت الخلو منهما. ولكن جزاءها وجود شيء وعدم آخر، ليس هو وجود الشيء وعدمه. ووجود مانعة الخلو شيء وعدم آخر يكون أحدهما لازماً للآخر، وإن كانا لا يرتفعان، لأن ارتفاعهما يقتضي ارتفاع وجود شيء وعدمه معاً. إذا انحصر الأمر فيهما فلم يمكن عدمهما جميعاً، كما لم يمكن وجودهما جميعاً.
Third Station – Returning the conjunctive and the exceptional to a single meaning. The exceptional is composed of the conjunctive and the disjunctive. As for the conjunctive conditional: it is an inference by implication, by the affirmation of the implied (the antecedent, the condition) to the affirmation of the necessary (the consequent, the result); or by the negation of the necessary (the consequent, the result) to the negation of the implied (the antecedent, the condition). As for the disjunctive conditional – which the usuliyyun call 'sabr and taqsim' and which the dialecticians also call 'division and disjunction' – its content is inference by the affirmation of one of the two contradictories to the negation of the other, and by its negation to its affirmation; or inference by the affirmation of one of the two contraries to the negation of the other. Its divisions are four. Therefore, in the inclusive disjunction (mani'at al-jam' wal-khuluww), there are four exclusions: that if this is affirmed, its contradictory is negated, and likewise the other; and if this is negated, its contradictory is affirmed, and likewise the other. And the exclusive disjunction (mani'at al-jam') is inference by the affirmation of one of the two contraries to the negation of the other, the two matters being mutually exclusive. And the exhaustive disjunction (mani'at al-khuluww) involves contradiction and implication; the two contradictories cannot be removed together, so it prevents their removal. But its result is the existence of one thing and the non-existence of another, not the existence and non-existence of the same thing. The existence of an exhaustive disjunction is a thing and the non-existence of another, one of them being necessary for the other, even though they cannot be removed together, because their removal would require the removal of existence and non-existence together. If the matter is restricted to them, it is not possible for both to be non-existent, just as it is not possible for both to exist.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage explains Ibn Taymiyya's reduction of conditional syllogisms (both conjunctive and disjunctive) to a single underlying inferential structure. He begins by stating the objective: to show that the 'exceptive' syllogism, composed of conjunctive and disjunctive conditionals, ultimately reduces to one meaning. For the conjunctive conditional, inference proceeds either by affirming the antecedent (the condition) to deduce the consequent (the result), or by denying the consequent to deny the antecedent. For the disjunctive conditional, which usul al-fiqh scholars call 'sabr and taqsim' and dialecticians call 'division and disjunction', the inference is based on the mutual exclusivity of contradictories or contraries: affirming one entails negating the other, and negating one entails affirming the other. He then divides disjunctives into four types, focusing on the inclusive disjunction (mani'at al-jam' wal-khuluww) which has four possible inferences: affirming one and negating the other, and vice versa. He clarifies that exclusive disjunction (mani'at al-jam') deals with contraries that cannot coexist, while exhaustive disjunction (mani'at al-khuluww) deals with contradictories that cannot both be absent. In the latter, the result is not the existence and non-existence of the same thing, but existence of one and non-existence of another, with a necessary relationship between them. Finally, he notes that if only two alternatives are possible, they cannot both be absent or both present. This exposition uses an example from kalam: 'The world is either eternal (qadim) or originated (muhdath)' – an inclusive disjunction where affirming one negates the other.

D) Negation of antecedent and affirmation of consequent

Key Takeaway
The conjunctive conditional provides two and only two valid inference patterns: affirming the antecedent (modus ponens) and denying the consequent (modus tollens).

7 min
Disjunctive Syllogisms in Islamic Logic: Sabr wa Taqsim
Explains types of disjunctive conditionals and their inferences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify disjunctive syllogisms into exclusive, exhaustive, and inclusive types, and determine the correct inference for each.

Core Idea
Disjunctive conditionals reduce to inference by exclusion: affirming one alternative negates the other (or vice versa).

Ibn Taymiyya presents the disjunctive conditional (al-shartiyya al-munfasila) as a key tool in usul al-fiqh (where it is called sabr wa taqsim) and dialectics. He distinguishes three types of disjunction based on whether the alternatives are contraries, contradictories, or both. Mastering these distinctions is essential for applying valid disjunctive reasoning in Islamic legal theory and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Type of Disjunction
Determine if the alternatives are contraries (cannot both be true) or contradictories (cannot both be false, exactly one is true). The passage describes exclusive (mani'at al-jam': contraries), exhaustive (mani'at al-khuluww: contradictories), and inclusive (mani'at al-jam' wal-khuluww: both conditions).
Inclusive disjunction allows both directions: affirming one negates the other, and negating one affirms the other.
2
Analyze Exclusive Disjunction (Mani'at al-Jam')
If the disjunction is exclusive (contraries), affirming one alternative entails negating the other. However, negating one does not necessarily affirm the other because both could be false.
Example: 'The action is either obligatory or forbidden.' They cannot both be true, but could both be false (e.g., permissible).
3
Analyze Exhaustive Disjunction (Mani'at al-Khuluww)
If the disjunction is exhaustive (contradictories), negating one alternative affirms the other. However, affirming one does not necessarily negate the other if they could both be true? Wait: Ibn Taymiyya defines it as 'the two contradictories cannot be removed together' – so they cannot both be false. He clarifies that it's about existence and non-existence of two different things, not the same thing. So precisely one is true.
Example: 'The world is either eternal or originated.' Both cannot be false, but can both be true? No, contradictory. So affirming one negates the other, and negating one affirms the other.
4
Analyze Inclusive Disjunction (Mani'at al-Jam' wal-Khuluww)
This combines both: the alternatives are both mutually exclusive and exhaustive. Therefore, all four inferences are valid: affirming one negates the other, and negating one affirms the other.
Ibn Taymiyya lists 'four exclusions' for this type.

المقام الثالث - عودة الاقتراني» و «الاستثنائي إلى معنى واحد «الاستثنائي» المؤلف من «المتصل» و «المنفصل». الشرطي وهو المتصل فإن الشرطي المتصل» استدلال باللزوم بثبوت الملزوم الذي هو المقدَّم، الشرط، على ثبوت اللازم الذي هو التالي، وهو الجزاء؛ أو بانتفاء اللازم وهو التالي الذي هو الجزاء، على انتفاء الملزوم الذي هو المقدّم، وهو الشرط . وأما الشرطي المنفصل»، وهو الذي يسميه الأصوليون السَّبر والتقسيم»، وقد تسميه أيضاً الجدليون التقسيم والترديد، فمضمونه الاستدلال بثبوت أحد النقيضين على انتفاء الآخر، وبانتفائه على ثبوته ؛ أو الاستدلال بثبوت أحد الضدين على انتفاء الآخر. وأقسامه أربعة . ولهذا كان في مانعة الجمع والخلو الاستثناءات الأربعة : وهو أنه إن ثبت هذا انتفى نقيضه، وكذلك الآخر ؛ وإن انتفى هذا ثبت نقيضه، وكذلك الآخر . و مانعة الجمع» الاستدلال بثبوت أحد الضدّين على انتفاء الآخر، والأمران متنافیان. و «مانعةُ الخلو» فيها تناقض ولزوم، والنقيضان لا يرتفعان، فمنعت الخلو منهما. ولكن جزاءها وجود شيء وعدم آخر، ليس هو وجود الشيء وعدمه. ووجود مانعة الخلو شيء وعدم آخر يكون أحدهما لازماً للآخر، وإن كانا لا يرتفعان، لأن ارتفاعهما يقتضي ارتفاع وجود شيء وعدمه معاً. إذا انحصر الأمر فيهما فلم يمكن عدمهما جميعاً، كما لم يمكن وجودهما جميعاً.
Third Station – Returning the conjunctive and the exceptional to a single meaning. The exceptional is composed of the conjunctive and the disjunctive. As for the conjunctive conditional: it is an inference by implication, by the affirmation of the implied (the antecedent, the condition) to the affirmation of the necessary (the consequent, the result); or by the negation of the necessary (the consequent, the result) to the negation of the implied (the antecedent, the condition). As for the disjunctive conditional – which the usuliyyun call 'sabr and taqsim' and which the dialecticians also call 'division and disjunction' – its content is inference by the affirmation of one of the two contradictories to the negation of the other, and by its negation to its affirmation; or inference by the affirmation of one of the two contraries to the negation of the other. Its divisions are four. Therefore, in the inclusive disjunction (mani'at al-jam' wal-khuluww), there are four exclusions: that if this is affirmed, its contradictory is negated, and likewise the other; and if this is negated, its contradictory is affirmed, and likewise the other. And the exclusive disjunction (mani'at al-jam') is inference by the affirmation of one of the two contraries to the negation of the other, the two matters being mutually exclusive. And the exhaustive disjunction (mani'at al-khuluww) involves contradiction and implication; the two contradictories cannot be removed together, so it prevents their removal. But its result is the existence of one thing and the non-existence of another, not the existence and non-existence of the same thing. The existence of an exhaustive disjunction is a thing and the non-existence of another, one of them being necessary for the other, even though they cannot be removed together, because their removal would require the removal of existence and non-existence together. If the matter is restricted to them, it is not possible for both to be non-existent, just as it is not possible for both to exist.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage explains Ibn Taymiyya's reduction of conditional syllogisms (both conjunctive and disjunctive) to a single underlying inferential structure. He begins by stating the objective: to show that the 'exceptive' syllogism, composed of conjunctive and disjunctive conditionals, ultimately reduces to one meaning. For the conjunctive conditional, inference proceeds either by affirming the antecedent (the condition) to deduce the consequent (the result), or by denying the consequent to deny the antecedent. For the disjunctive conditional, which usul al-fiqh scholars call 'sabr and taqsim' and dialecticians call 'division and disjunction', the inference is based on the mutual exclusivity of contradictories or contraries: affirming one entails negating the other, and negating one entails affirming the other. He then divides disjunctives into four types, focusing on the inclusive disjunction (mani'at al-jam' wal-khuluww) which has four possible inferences: affirming one and negating the other, and vice versa. He clarifies that exclusive disjunction (mani'at al-jam') deals with contraries that cannot coexist, while exhaustive disjunction (mani'at al-khuluww) deals with contradictories that cannot both be absent. In the latter, the result is not the existence and non-existence of the same thing, but existence of one and non-existence of another, with a necessary relationship between them. Finally, he notes that if only two alternatives are possible, they cannot both be absent or both present. This exposition uses an example from kalam: 'The world is either eternal (qadim) or originated (muhdath)' – an inclusive disjunction where affirming one negates the other.

Key Terms

السبر والتقسيم
مانعة الجمع
مانعة الخلو
مانعة الجمع والخلو

C) One: only affirmation to negation.
D) Three: affirmation to negation, negation to affirmation, and double negation.

Key Takeaway
Disjunctive syllogisms have three main types; only inclusive disjunction supports all four inference patterns, while exclusive and exhaustive each support a subset.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Ibn Taymiyya's Refutation of the Epistemic Distinction Between Categorical and Analogical Syllogisms
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the epistemic distinction between categorical and analogical syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to demonstrate the structural equivalence between analogical syllogism (qiyas al-tamthil) and categorical syllogism (qiyas al-shumul) using Ibn Taymiyya's critique and the example of wine and nabidh.

Core Idea
Analogy and categorical syllogism differ only in psychological packaging, not in logical structure.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya confronts a common claim made by Greek-inspired logicians: that categorical syllogisms yield certainty while analogical syllogisms yield only probability. He argues that this distinction is false, because epistemic force depends entirely on the content of the premises, not the outer form. He then shows that analogical reasoning can be recast as a categorical syllogism, proving their structural identity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim being attacked
The logicians claim that qiyas al-shumul (categorical syllogism) gives certainty while qiyas al-tamthil (analogical syllogism) gives only probability. Locate this claim in the first line of the Arabic passage.
Ibn Taymiyya immediately declares this distinction false.
2
Follow Ibn Taymiyya's rebuttal
He argues that the epistemic value depends on the content of the premises, not the form. If premises are certain, both forms yield certainty; if probable, both yield probability.
3
Examine his structural comparison
He equates the 'middle term' (al-hadd al-awsat) in categorical syllogism with the 'common description' (al-wasf al-mushtarak) in analogy. The major premise expresses the effect of the common description.
This shows the underlying logical identity.
4
Analyze the concrete example of wine and nabidh
The analogical argument 'Nabidh is forbidden because wine is forbidden due to intoxication, and nabidh is intoxicating' is reformulated as a categorical syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; every nabidh is intoxicant; therefore, nabidh is forbidden.' Identify the minor term (nabidh), major term (forbidden), and middle term (intoxicant).
This demonstrates the structural equivalence.
5
Note the psychological addition in analogy
Ibn Taymiyya explains that the mention of the original (wine) in analogy is only for psychological reinforcement, because the soul feels stronger identification with a specific example.
This is the only difference; logically, it is superfluous.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيقال : تفريقهم بين قياس الشمول وقياس التمثيل، بأن الأول يفيد اليقين والثاني لا يفيد إلا الظن فرق باطل . بل حيث أفاد أحدهما اليقين أفاد الآخر اليقين، وحيث لا يفيد أحدهما إلا الظن لا يفيد الآخر إلا الظن فإن إفادة الدليل لليقين أو الظن ليس لكونه على صورة أحدهما دون الآخر، بل باعتبار تضمن أحدهما لما يفيد اليقين . فإن كان أحدهما اشتمل على أمر مستلزم للحكم يقيناً حصل به اليقين، وإن لم يشتمل إلا على ما يفيد الحكم ظنا لم يقد إلا الظن . والذي يسمى في أحدهما حدا أوسط هو في الآخر الوصف المشترك. والقضية الكبرى المتضمنة لزوم الحد الأكبر للأوسط هو بيان تأثير الوصف المشترك بين الأصل والفرع. فما به يتبين صدق القضية الكبرى به يتبين أن الجامع المشترك مستلزم للحكم . فلزوم الأكبر للأوسط هو لزوم الحكم للمشترك . فإذا قلت: النبيذ حرام قياسا على الخمر، لأن الخمر إنما حرمت لكونها مثال تحريم مسكرة، وهذا الوصف موجود في النبيذ كان بمنزلة قولك : كل نبيذ مسكر، وكل مسكر حرام فالنتيجة قولك : النبيذ حرام والنبيذ هو موضوعها، وهو الحد الأصغر، والحرام محمولها، وهو الحد الأكبر، والمسكر هو المتوسط بين الموضوع والمحمول وهو الحد الأوسط، المحمول في الصغرى، الموضوع في الكبرى. فإذا قلت: النبيذ حرام قياسا على خمر العنب، لأن العلة في الأصل هو الإسكار، وهو موجود في الفرع، فثبت التحريم لوجود علته. فإنما استدللت على تحريم النبيذ بالمسكر، وهو الحد الأوسط، لكن زدت في قياس التمثيل ذكر الأصل الذي ضربته مثلا للفرع وهذا لأن شعور النفس بنظير الفرع أقوى
So it is said: Their distinction between categorical syllogism (qiyas al-shumul) and analogical syllogism (qiyas al-tamthil)—that the first yields certainty and the second yields only probability—is a false distinction. Rather, where one yields certainty the other yields certainty, and where one yields only probability the other yields only probability. For the yielding of certainty or probability by the evidence is not because it is in the form of one without the other, but because it contains what yields certainty. If one includes something necessitating the judgment with certainty, certainty is obtained; if it includes only what makes the judgment probable, it yields only probability. What is called the middle term (hadd awsat) in one is in the other the common description (al-wasf al-mushtarak). The major premise containing the connection of the major term to the middle is an expression of the effect of the common description between original and branch. Thus that by which the truth of the major premise is clarified is that by which it is clarified that the commonality is necessitating the judgment. For the connection of the major to the middle is the connection of the judgment to the commonality. So if you say: 'Nabidh is forbidden by analogy to wine, because wine was only forbidden because it is an intoxicant, and this attribute exists in nabidh,' it is equivalent to your saying: 'Every nabidh is an intoxicant, and every intoxicant is forbidden,' so the result is your saying: 'Nabidh is forbidden.' Nabidh is its subject, which is the minor term, and 'forbidden' is its predicate, which is the major term, and 'intoxicant' is the middle between subject and predicate, and it is the middle term, being the predicate in the minor premise and the subject in the major premise. So if you say: 'Nabidh is forbidden by analogy to wine made from grapes, because the cause in the original is intoxication, and it exists in the branch, so the prohibition is established due to the existence of its cause,' then you have inferred the prohibition of nabidh by 'intoxicant,' which is the middle term, but you have added in analogical syllogism the mention of the original which you gave as an example for the branch, and this is because the soul's awareness of the branch's counterpart is stronger...
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage is Ibn Taymiyya's direct refutation of the logicians' claim that categorical syllogism (qiyas al-shumul) yields certainty while analogical syllogism (qiyas al-tamthil) yields only probability. He declares this distinction false, arguing that epistemic value depends on the content of the premises, not the form. Both types produce certainty if premises are certain, and probability if premises are probable. He then demonstrates structural equivalence: the middle term in categorical syllogism is the common description in analogy, and the major premise in categorical syllogism expresses the effect of that common description. Using the example of wine and nabidh, he shows that the analogical argument 'Nabidh is forbidden because wine is forbidden due to intoxication, and nabidh is intoxicating' can be recast as a categorical syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; every nabidh is intoxicant; therefore, nabidh is forbidden.' The extra mention of the original (wine) in analogical reasoning is only for psychological reinforcement, as the soul feels stronger identification with a specific example. Thus, there is no essential logical difference.

Key Terms
قياس الشمول
قياس التمثيل
الحد الأوسط
الوصف المشترك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Categorical syllogism yields certainty, analogy yields only probability.
B) Analogy includes an explicit reference to the original case for psychological reassurance.
C) Categorical syllogism uses a middle term, analogy uses a common description.
D) Analogy has a major premise, categorical syllogism does not.

Key Takeaway
The logical structure of analogy is identical to that of categorical syllogism; the difference is only in psychological presentation.

Ibn Taymiyya's Rejection of the Distinction Between Syllogism and Analogy
Ibn Taymiyya argues that qiyas al-tamthil is structurally identical to qiyas al-shamul and can yield certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structural equivalence between a categorical syllogism and an analogical argument, and explain why Ibn Taymiyya holds that both can yield certainty.

Core Idea
Syllogism and analogy are structurally identical; certainty depends on the premises, not the form.

Logicians claimed that categorical syllogism (qiyas al-shamul) yields certainty while analogical reasoning (qiyas al-tamthil) yields only probability. Ibn Taymiyya rejects this distinction outright. In this passage, he argues that the two forms are structurally identical: what they call the middle term is the same as the common attribute in analogy. Using the example of nabidh and khamr, he shows that an analogical argument is simply a categorical syllogism with an extra mention of the original case.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
Logicians distinguish between qiyas al-shamul (categorical syllogism) and qiyas al-tamthil (analogy), asserting that the first yields certainty (yaqin) and the second only probability (zann).
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's refutation
He calls this distinction 'batil' (false). The epistemic value of an argument depends on whether its premises contain something that necessitates the judgment with certainty, not on its form.
3
Find the structural equivalence
The middle term (al-hadd al-awsat) in a syllogism is exactly the common attribute (al-wasf al-mushtarak) in analogy. The major premise corresponds to establishing that the common attribute necessitates the judgment.
4
Apply the equivalence to the nabidh example
The analogical argument 'Nabidh is forbidden because it is intoxicating like khamr' is equivalent to the categorical syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; every nabidh is intoxicating; therefore, nabidh is forbidden.' Identify the minor term (nabidh), major term (forbidden), and middle term (intoxicant).
5
Conclude the epistemological point
Certainty or probability comes from the content of the premises, not from being in the form of syllogism or analogy. Thus, both forms can achieve certainty if the premises are certain.

المقام الثالث - رد المصنف إشكالاتهم على قياس التمثيل بين الشمول والتمثيل فيقال : تفريقهم بين قياس الشمول و قياس التمثيل، بأن الأول قد يفيد اليقين والثاني لا يفيد إلا الظن فرق باطل . بل حيث أفاد أحدهما اليقين أفاد الآخر اليقين، وحيث لا يفيد أحدهما إلا الظن لا يفيد الآخر إلا الظن فإن إفادة الدليل لليقين أو الظن ليس لكونه على صورة أحدهما دون الآخر، بل باعتبار تضمن أحدهما لما يفيد اليقين . فإن كان أحدهما اشتمل على أمر مستلزم للحكم يقيناً حصل به اليقين، وإن لم يشتمل إلا على ما يفيد الحكم ظنا لم يفد إلا الظن . والذي يسمى في أحدهما حداً أوسط هو في الآخر الوصف المشترك. والقضية الكبرى المتضمنة لزوم الحد الأكبر للأوسط هو بيان تأثير الوصف المشترك بين الأصل والفرع. فما به يتبين صدق القضية الكبرى به يتبين أن الجامع المشترك مستلزم للحكم . فلزوم الأكبر للأوسط هو لزوم الحكم للمشترك . فإذا قلت: النبيذ حرام قياساً على الخمر، لأن الخمر إنما حرمت لكونها مسكرة، وهذا الوصف موجود في النبيذ كان بمنزلة قولك : كل مسكر حرام، وكل النبيذ مسكر فالنبيذ حرام و النبيذ هو موضوعها، وهو الحد الأصغر، و الحرام محمولها، وهو الحد الأكبر، و المسكر هو المتوسط بين الموضوع والمحمول وهو الحد الأوسط، المحمول في الصغرى، الموضوع في الكبرى. فإذا قلت: النبيذ حرام قياساً على خمر العنب، لأن العلة في الأصل هو الإسكار، وهو موجود في الفرع، فثبت التحريم لوجود علته. فإنما استدللت على تحريم النبيذ بالمسكر، وهو الحد الأوسط، لكن زدت في قياس التمثيل ذكر الأصل الذي ضربته مثلاً للفرع وهذا لأن شعور النفس بنظير الفرع أقوى في المعرفة من مجرد دخوله في الجامع الكلي. وإذا قام الدليل على تأثير الوصف المشترك لم يكن ذكر الأصل محتاجاً إليه . إبداء الجامع و القياس لا يخلو إما أن يكون بـ إبداء الجامع أو بـ إلغاء الفارق. وإلغاء الفارق و الجامع إما العلة وإما دليلها وأما القياس بإلغاء الفارق فهنا إلغاء الفارق هو أن ما به الحد الأوسط. فإذا قيل : هذا مساو لهذا ومساوي المساوي مساو كانت المساواة هي الحد الأوسط، وإلغاء الفارق عبارة عن المساواة. فإذا قيل: لا فرق بين الفرع والأصل إلا كذا ، وهو مهدر ، فهو بمنزلة قولك : هذا مساو لهذا، وحكم المساوي حكم مساويه . وأما قولهم : كل ما يدل على أن ما به الاشتراك علة للحكم فظني. فيقال : لا نسلم، فإن هذه دعوى كلية، ولم تقيموا عليها دليلاً. ثم نقول : الذي يدل به على علية المشترك هو الذي يدل به على صدق القضية الكبرى وكل ما يدل به على صدق الكبرى فظني في قياس الشمول يدل به على علية المشترك في قياس التمثيل سواء كان علمياً أو ظنياً. فإن الجامع المشترك في التمثيل هو الحد الأوسط، ولزوم الحكم له هو لزوم الأكبر للأوسط، ولزوم الأوسط للأصغر هو لزوم الجامع المشترك للأصغر، وهو ثبوت العلة في الفرع . فإذا كان الوصف المشترك، وهو المسمى بالجامع، و العلة، أو دليل العلة، أو المناط، أو ما كان من الأسماء، إذا كان ذلك الوصف ثابتاً في الفرع، لازماً له كان ذلك موجباً لصدق المقدمة الصغرى. وإذا كان الحكم ثابتاً للوصف، لازماً له، كان ذلك موجباً لصدق المقدمة الكبرى. وذكر الأصل ليتوسل به إلى إثبات إحدى المقدمتين .
The Third Station: The author's refutation of their objections against analogical reasoning (qiyas al-tamthil). Between inclusive syllogism (qiyas al-shamul) and analogy (tamthil). It is said: Their distinction between inclusive syllogism and analogical reasoning, that the first yields certainty and the second yields only probability, is a false distinction. Rather, wherever one yields certainty, the other yields certainty; and wherever one yields only probability, the other yields only probability. For the evidence (dalil) giving certainty or probability is not because it takes the form of one rather than the other, but because of what it contains that yields certainty. So if one includes something that entails the judgment with certainty, certainty is obtained; if it includes only what yields the judgment probabilistically, it yields only probability. What is called the middle term (hadd awsat) in one is called the common attribute (wasf mushtarak) in the other. The major premise that entails the connection of the major term to the middle term is the explanation of the effect of the common attribute between the original (asl) and the branch (far'). So that by which the truth of the major premise is known is that by which the common ground (jami' mushtarak) is seen to entail the judgment. Thus the connection of the major term to the middle term is the connection of the judgment to the common attribute. So if you say: 'Nabidh is forbidden by analogy to khamr, because khamr was only forbidden because it is an intoxicant, and this attribute is present in nabidh,' it is equivalent to your saying: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden, and every nabidh is intoxicating, so nabidh is forbidden.' Nabidh is its subject, which is the minor term; 'forbidden' is its predicate, which is the major term; and 'intoxicant' is the intermediate between subject and predicate, which is the middle term, the predicate in the minor premise and the subject in the major premise. So if you say: 'Nabidh is forbidden by analogy to wine of grapes, because the cause ('illa) in the original is intoxication, and it is present in the branch, so the prohibition is established because its cause exists,' you have inferred the prohibition of nabidh by means of 'intoxicant,' which is the middle term, but you have added in analogical reasoning the mention of the original which you took as an example for the branch. This is because the mind's awareness of the analogue of the branch is stronger in knowledge than mere inclusion under the universal common attribute. If evidence is established for the effect of the common attribute, the mention of the original is not needed. The indication of the common ground (ibda' al-jami') and the inference (qiyas) are either by indicating the common ground (ibda' al-jami') or by eliminating the difference (ilgha' al-fariq). The elimination of the difference and the common ground are either the cause ('illa) or its evidence. As for inference by eliminating the difference, here the elimination of the difference is that by which the middle term is established. So if it is said: 'This is equal to this, and the equal of the equal is equal,' equality is the middle term, and elimination of the difference is an expression of equality. So if it is said: 'There is no difference between the branch and the original except such and such, and it is disregarded (muhdar),' it is equivalent to your saying: 'This is equal to this, and the ruling of the equal is the ruling of its equal.' As for their saying: 'Everything that indicates that the common attribute is the cause of the ruling is probabilistic (zanni),' it is said: We do not accept that, for this is a universal claim, and you have not provided evidence for it. Then we say: That by which the causality of the common attribute is indicated is that by which the truth of the major premise is indicated, and everything by which the truth of the major premise is indicated is probabilistic (zanni) in inclusive syllogism (qiyas al-shamul) – it indicates the causality of the common attribute in analogical reasoning equally, whether it is certain or probabilistic. For the common ground in analogy is the middle term, and the connection of the judgment to it is the connection of the major term to the middle term, and the connection of the middle term to the minor term is the connection of the common ground to the minor term, which is the establishment of the cause in the branch. So if the common attribute, which is called the common ground (jami'), the cause ('illa), or the evidence of the cause, or the connection (manat), or whatever names it may have, if that attribute is established in the branch and is necessary to it, that necessitates the truth of the minor premise. And if the judgment is established for the attribute and is necessary to it, that necessitates the truth of the major premise. The mention of the original is a means to establish one of the two premises.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation by Ibn Taymiyya of the logicians' claim that qiyas al-shamul (categorical syllogism) yields certainty while qiyas al-tamthil (analogy) yields only probability. He begins by stating their distinction and immediately declares it false. The key point is that the certainty or probability of a proof does not depend on its form (shamul vs tamthil) but on whether the premises contain something that necessitates the judgment with certainty. He then demonstrates the structural identity of the two: what logicians call the 'middle term' in a categorical syllogism is exactly the 'common attribute' in analogy. The major premise in a syllogism (e.g., 'Every intoxicant is forbidden') corresponds to establishing that the common attribute (intoxication) is the effective cause of the ruling (forbiddenness). Using the example of nabidh (date wine) and khamr (grape wine), he shows that the analogical argument 'Nabidh is forbidden because it is intoxicating like khamr' is equivalent to the categorical syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; every nabidh is intoxicating; therefore, nabidh is forbidden.' The only difference is that in analogy, one explicitly mentions the original (asl) to strengthen the mind's awareness, but if the causal connection is already established, the original is unnecessary. He then discusses two methods of establishing the common ground: by directly indicating the common attribute (ibda' al-jami') or by eliminating differences (ilgha' al-fariq), which reduces to a statement of equality. Finally, he responds to the objection that proofs of causality are only probabilistic. He counters that this is a universal claim without proof, and any probabilistic evidence in a categorical syllogism is equally probabilistic in analogy. Thus, the two forms are epistemologically equivalent.

الوصف المشترك

A) They are different in structure; qiyas al-shamul yields certainty while qiyas al-tamthil yields only probability.
B) They are identical in structure but qiyas al-shamul always yields certainty while qiyas al-tamthil always yields probability.
C) They are structurally identical and can both yield certainty or probability depending on the premises.
D) Qiyas al-tamthil is superior because it includes an additional original term for clarity.

The Syllogism

Methods of Establishing the Common Ground: Ibdā‘ al-Jāmi‘ and Ilghā’ al-Fāriq
Ibn Taymiyya explains methods of establishing the common ground and rebuts the probabilistic objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two methods of establishing the common attribute in analogical reasoning (ibdā‘ al-jāmi‘ and ilghā’ al-fāriq) and evaluate Ibn Taymiyya's response to the claim that causality proofs are inherently probabilistic.

Core Idea
Eliminating differences (ilghā’ al-fāriq) is a valid method to establish the middle term in analogy.

After establishing the structural equivalence between syllogism and analogy, Ibn Taymiyya explains how the common attribute (al-jāmi‘) is identified. He describes two methods: directly pointing to the common ground (ibdā‘ al-jāmi‘) and eliminating differences (ilghā’ al-fāriq). He then addresses the logicians' objection that all proofs of causality are merely probabilistic. This lesson breaks down these methods and the rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two methods
Ibn Taymiyya states that indicating the common ground (ibdā‘ al-jāmi‘) and eliminating differences (ilghā’ al-fāriq) are the two ways to establish the middle term in analogy. The common ground may be the cause ('illa) or its evidence.
2
Analyze ilghā’ al-fāriq
Eliminating differences is essentially a claim of equality (musāwāt) between the branch and original. Equality itself becomes the middle term: 'This is equal to that; the equal of the equal is equal.'
3
Examine the logicians' objection
They argue that everything indicating the common attribute as the cause of the ruling is probabilistic (ẓannī). Hence, analogy is probabilistic.
4
Follow Ibn Taymiyya's rebuttal
He refutes by saying this is an unsubstantiated universal claim. The same evidence that indicates the truth of the major premise in a categorical syllogism indicates the causality of the common attribute in analogy. If that evidence is probabilistic in syllogism, it is equally probabilistic in analogy; if certain, then certain in both.
5
Conclude the epistemic symmetry
Thus, analogy and syllogism stand on the same epistemic footing. The objection does not differentiate them.

الفرع، فثبت التحريم لوجود علته. فإنما استدللت على تحريم النبيذ بالمسكر، وهو الحد الأوسط، لكن زدت في قياس التمثيل ذكر الأصل الذي ضربته مثلاً للفرع وهذا لأن شعور النفس بنظير الفرع أقوى في المعرفة من The Third Station: The author's refutation of their objections against analogical reasoning (qiyas al-tamthil). Between inclusive syllogism (qiyas al-shamul) and analogy (tamthil). It is said: Their distinction between inclusive syllogism and analogical reasoning, that the first yields certainty and the second yields only probability, is a false distinction. Rather, wherever one yields certainty, the other yields certainty; and wherever one yields only probability, the other yields only probability. For the evidence (dalil) giving certainty or probability is not because it takes the form of one rather than the other, but because of what it contains that yields certainty. So if one includes something that entails the judgment with certainty, certainty is obtained; if it includes only what yields the judgment probabilistically, it yields only probability. What is called the middle term (hadd awsat) in one is called the common attribute (wasf mushtarak) in the other. The major premise that entails the connection of the major term to the middle term is the explanation of the effect of the common attribute between the original (asl) and the branch (far'). So that by which the truth of the major premise is known is that by which the common ground (jami' mushtarak) is seen to entail the judgment. Thus the connection of the major term to the middle term is the connection of the judgment to the common attribute. So if you say: 'Nabidh is forbidden by analogy to khamr, because khamr was only forbidden because it is an intoxicant, and this attribute is present in nabidh,' it is equivalent to your saying: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden, and every nabidh is intoxicating, so nabidh is forbidden.' Nabidh is its subject, which is the minor term; 'forbidden' is its predicate, which is the major term; and 'intoxicant' is the intermediate between subject and predicate, which is the middle term, the predicate in the minor premise and the subject in the major premise. So if you say: 'Nabidh is forbidden by analogy to wine of grapes, because the cause ('illa) in the original is intoxication, and it is present in the branch, so the prohibition is established because its cause exists,' you have inferred the prohibition of nabidh by means of 'intoxicant,' which is the middle term, but you have added in analogical reasoning the mention of the original which you took as an example for the branch. This is because the mind's awareness of the analogue of the branch is stronger in knowledge than mere inclusion under the universal common attribute. If evidence is established for the effect of the common attribute, the mention of the original is not needed. The indication of the common ground (ibda' al-jami') and the inference (qiyas) are either by indicating the common ground (ibda' al-jami') or by eliminating the difference (ilgha' al-fariq). The elimination of the difference and the common ground are either the cause ('illa) or its evidence. As for inference by eliminating the difference, here the elimination of the difference is that by which the middle term is established. So if it is said: 'This is equal to this, and the equal of the equal is equal,' equality is the middle term, and elimination of the difference is an expression of equality. So if it is said: 'There is no difference between the branch and the original except such and such, and it is disregarded (muhdar),' it is equivalent to your saying: 'This is equal to this, and the ruling of the equal is the ruling of its equal.' As for their saying: 'Everything that indicates that the common attribute is the cause of the ruling is probabilistic (zanni),' it is said: We do not accept that, for this is a universal claim, and you have not provided evidence for it. Then we say: That by which the causality of the common attribute is indicated is that by which the truth of the major premise is indicated, and everything by which the truth of the major premise is indicated is probabilistic (zanni) in inclusive syllogism (qiyas al-shamul) – it indicates the causality of the common attribute in analogical reasoning equally, whether it is certain or probabilistic. For the common ground in analogy is the middle term, and the connection of the judgment to it is the connection of the major term to the middle term, and the connection of the middle term to the minor term is the connection of the common ground to the minor term, which is the establishment of the cause in the branch. So if the common attribute, which is called the common ground (jami'), the cause ('illa), or the evidence of the cause, or the connection (manat), or whatever names it may have, if that attribute is established in the branch and is necessary to it, that necessitates the truth of the minor premise. And if the judgment is established for the attribute and is necessary to it, that necessitates the truth of the major premise. The mention of the original is a means to establish one of the two premises.

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation by Ibn Taymiyya of the logicians' claim that qiyas al-shamul (categorical syllogism) yields certainty while qiyas al-tamthil (analogy) yields only probability. He begins by stating their distinction and immediately declares it false. The key point is that the certainty or probability of a proof does not depend on its form (shamul vs tamthil) but on whether the premises contain something that necessitates the judgment with certainty. He then demonstrates the structural identity of the two: what logicians call the 'middle term' in a categorical syllogism is exactly the 'common attribute' in analogy. The major premise in a syllogism (e.g., 'Every intoxicant is forbidden') corresponds to establishing that the common attribute (intoxication) is the effective cause of the ruling (forbiddenness). Using the example of nabidh (date wine) and khamr (grape wine), he shows that the analogical argument 'Nabidh is forbidden because it is intoxicating like khamr' is equivalent to the categorical syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; every nabidh is intoxicating; therefore, nabidh is forbidden.' The only difference is that in analogy, one explicitly mentions the original (asl) to strengthen the mind's awareness, but if the causal connection is already established, the original is unnecessary. He then discusses two methods of establishing the common ground: by directly indicating the common attribute (ibda' al-jami') or by eliminating differences (ilgha' al-fariq), which reduces to a statement of equality. Finally, he responds to the objection that proofs of causality are only probabilistic. He counters that this is a universal claim without proof, and any probabilistic evidence in a categorical syllogism is equally probabilistic in analogy. Thus, the two forms are epistemologically equivalent.

The Logical Equivalence of Qiyas Tamthil and Qiyas Shumul
Ibn Taymiyya exemplifies the equivalence of qiyas tamthil and shumul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's example showing that qiyas tamthil and qiyas shumul have identical logical structure, and identify the role of the middle term in both.

Core Idea
Qiyas tamthil and qiyas shumul differ only in presentation, not in logical necessity.

Ibn Taymiyya has argued that the so-called 'analogical' qiyas (tamthil) and the categorical syllogism (shumul) are logically identical. Here he gives a concrete example: proving that 'human is a body'. By walking through both forms, you will see that the middle term—'animal'—plays exactly the same role. The only difference is that in tamthil, one uses an original case (asl) to establish the premises, but that is merely a procedural aid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the standard categorical syllogism (qiyas shumul)
Find the three terms: minor (human), middle (animal), major (body). Note the premises: 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every animal is a body', leading to the conclusion 'Every human is a body'.
2
Translate the same reasoning into qiyas tamthil form
See how the analogical argument runs: 'The horse is a body because it is an animal; human shares the attribute of animality, so human is a body.' Recognize that the common attribute (animality) is the middle term.
3
Observe the necessity of the middle term
Determine that the truth of the major premise ('every animal is a body') and the minor premise ('every human is an animal') is required for both forms. If the major premise is disputed (e.g., 'we do not concede that animality necessitates bodyhood'), that dispute applies equally to both qiyas types.
4
Understand the role of the original (asl)
In tamthil, the original (e.g., horse) is used as a concrete example to establish the major premise, but it is logically unnecessary if the universal claim is accepted. The original is merely a means to prove the 'illa's causality.
Ibn Taymiyya notes that in 'qiyas by presentation of cause' (ibda' al-illa), the original may be omitted entirely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن الجامع المشترك في التمثيل هو الحد الأوسط، ولزوم الحكم له هو لزوم الأكبر للأوسط، ولزوم الأوسط للأصغر هو لزوم الجامع المشترك للأصغر، وهو ثبوت الاشتراك في الفرع. فإذا كان الوصف المشترك، وهو المسمى بـ «الجامع»، و «العلة»، أو دليل العلة، أو «المَنَاط»، أو ما كان من الأسماء، إذا كان ذلك الوصف ثابتاً في الفرع، لازماً له كان ذلك موجباً لصدق المقدمة الصغرى. وإذا كان الحكم ثابتاً للوصف، لازماً له، كان ذلك موجباً لصدق المقدمة الكبرى. وذكر الأصل ليتوسل به إلى إثبات إحدى المقدمتين. فإن كان القياس بـ «إلغاء الفارق فلا بد من الأصل المعيَّن، فإن المشترك هو المساواة بينهما وتماثلهما، وهو إلغاء الفارق، وهو الحد الأوسط. وإن كان القياس بـ «إبداء العلة» فقد يستغنى عن ذكر الأصل إذا كان الاستدلال على علية الوصف لا يفتقر إليه. وأما إذا احتاج إثبات علية الوصف إليه فيذكر الأصلُ، لأنه من تمام ما يدل على علية المشترك، وهو الحد الأكبر. وهؤلاء الذين فرقوا بين قياس التمثيل وقياس الشمول أخذوا يظهرون كون المواد اليقينية أحدهما ظنّياً في مواد معيّنة. وتلك المواد التي لا تفيد إلا الظن في قياس التمثيل لا في القياسين تفيد إلا الظن في قياس الشمول وإلا فإذا أخذوه فيما يُستفاد به اليقين من قياس الشمول أفاد اليقين في قياس التمثيل أيضاً، وكان ظهور اليقين به هناك أتم. فإذا قيل في قياس الشمول كل إنسان حيوان وكل حيوان جسم، فكل إنسان جسم، كان «الحيوان» هو الحد الأوسط. وهو المشترك في قياس التمثيل بأن يقال: قياساً على الفَرَس وغيره من الحيوانات. فإن كون تلك الحيوانات جسم هو مستلزم لكونها أجساماً، سواءً كان علة أو دليل العلة، والحيوانية موجودة في الإنسان، فيكون جسماً. وإذا نوزع في علية الحكم في الأصل، فقيل له : لا نسلّم أن الحيوانية تستلزم الجسمية. كان هذا نزاعاً في قوله : كل حيوان جسم. وذلك أن المشترك بين الأصل والفرع إذا سُمي علة فإنما يراد به «ما يستلزم الحكم»، سواء كان هو العلة الموجبة لوجوده في الخارج أو كان مستلزماً لذلك. ومن الناس مَنْ يسمّي الأول قياس علة، والثاني قياس دلالة. ومن الناس من يسمي الجميع «علة»، لا سيما من يقول : إن «العلة» إنما يراد بها «المعرف»، وهو الأمارة، والعلامة والدليل، لا يراد بها «الباعث» و «الداعي». ومن قال : إنه قد يراد بها الداعي، وهو «الباعث» - وهذا قول أئمة الفقهاء وجمهور المسلمين - فإنه يقول ذلك في علل الأفعال وأما غير الأفعال فقد تفسر «العلة» فيها بـ «الوصف المستلزم»، كاستلزام الإنسانية لـ «الحيوانية»، والحيوانية
For the common shared [description] in tamthil is the middle term, and the necessity of the ruling for it is the necessity of the major for the middle, and the necessity of the middle for the minor is the necessity of the common shared for the minor, and that is the affirmation of the commonality in the branch. So if the shared description, which is called 'the common', 'the cause', or 'the evidence of the cause', or 'the manat', or whatever names, if that description is established in the branch and necessary for it, that necessitates the truth of the minor premise. And if the ruling is established for the description and necessary for it, that necessitates the truth of the major premise. And mentioning the original is to use it to establish one of the two premises. If the qiyas is by elimination of the difference, then the specific original is necessary, for the common is the equality between them and their similarity, which is the elimination of the difference, and it is the middle term. And if the qiyas is by presentation of the cause, then it is sometimes independent of mentioning the original if the inference on the causality of the description does not require it. But if establishing the causality of the description requires it, then the original is mentioned because it is part of what indicates the causality of the common, which is the major term. And those who distinguished between qiyas tamthil and qiyas shumul began to show that certain materials are conjectural in one of them in specific materials. Those materials which yield only conjecture in qiyas tamthil do not yield in both qiyas except conjecture in qiyas shumul. Otherwise, if they take it for what yields certainty from qiyas shumul, it yields certainty in qiyas tamthil as well, and the appearance of certainty there is more complete. So if it is said in qiyas shumul: Every human is an animal, and every animal is a body, therefore every human is a body, 'animal' is the middle term. And it is the common in qiyas tamthil by saying: by analogy to the horse and other animals. For the fact that those animals are bodies necessitates that they are bodies, whether it is a cause or evidence of the cause, and animality is present in the human, so he is a body. And if one disputes the causality of the ruling in the original, and it is said to him: We do not concede that animality necessitates bodyhood, this is a dispute in his statement: Every animal is a body. For the common between original and branch, if it is called a cause, it only means 'what necessitates the ruling', whether it is the cause that necessitates its existence in the external or that necessitates that. Some people call the first 'cause qiyas' and the second 'indication qiyas'. And some people call all of it 'cause', especially those who say that 'cause' is only meant as 'identifier', which is the indicator, sign, and evidence, not meant as 'motivator' or 'impulse'. And whoever says that it may be meant as 'impulse', which is the 'motivator' - and this is the saying of the leading jurists and the majority of Muslims - then he says that in causes of actions. As for non-actions, 'cause' is interpreted there as 'the necessitating description', like the necessitation of humanity for animality, and animality
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage provides a concrete example to illustrate Ibn Taymiyya's earlier point that qiyas tamthil and qiyas shumul are logically equivalent. He takes the standard syllogism: 'Every human is an animal, every animal is a body, therefore every human is a body.' In qiyas shumul, 'animal' is the middle term. In qiyas tamthil, one reasons: 'The horse and other animals are bodies, and the human shares the attribute of animality, therefore the human is a body.' The common factor (animality) is the middle term. Ibn Taymiyya then discusses the term 'illa (cause), noting its various meanings. In qiyas tamthil, 'illa can mean either the efficient cause (al-bā'ith) that motivates action, or the descriptive indicator (al-mu'arraf) that merely signals the ruling. The jurists often use 'illa for actions, while for non-actions it means the necessitating description (al-wasf al-mustalzim). He emphasizes that the same epistemic standards apply to both types of qiyas if the materials are certain.

Key Terms
العلة (al-'illa): A description that necessitates the ruling; includes both the efficient cause (al-bā'ith) and the indicator (al-mu'arraf) depending on context. [Note: In usul al-fiqh, 'illa often means the effective cause for legal analogy; here Ibn Taymiyya expands it to include any necessitating description.]
المَنَاط (al-manāt): Another term for the common factor or cause in qiyas, synonymous with 'illa or dālīl al-'illa. [Note: Commonly used in usul al-fiqh for the 'ratio legis'; stable meaning here.]
قياس التمثيل (qiyās al-tamthīl): Analogical reasoning where a branch is compared to an original based on a shared attribute; Ibn Taymiyya argues it has the same logical form as qiyas shumul. [Note: In classical logic, qiyas tamthil is often considered weaker; Ibn Taymiyya redefines it as equivalent in structure to categorical syllogism.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example of 'every human is a body' through both qiyas shumul and tamthil, what is the middle term?
A) Human.
B) Body.
C) Animal.
D) Original.

Key Takeaway
The middle term (al-ḥadd al-awsaṭ) is the same in both qiyas tamthil and qiyas shumul; the difference is merely in how the premises are presented.

The Syllogism
advanced
8 min
Tamthil and Shumul Both Yield Certainty
Ibn Taymiyya uses an example to equate analogical and syllogistic certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that analogical reasoning (tamthil) and categorical syllogism (shumul) both yield certainty when their materials are certain, using the example of proving man is a body.

Core Idea
Certainty does not depend on logical form but on material certainty.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the logicians' hierarchy that places syllogism above analogy. Using a concrete example from biology—proving that humans are bodies—he shows that the same material certainty that validates the syllogism also validates the analogy. This lesson breaks down his argument and its implications for the epistemology of reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two forms of reasoning
In the passage, Ibn Taymiyya presents analogical reasoning (qiyās al-tamthīl) and syllogistic reasoning (qiyās al-shumūl). Note how each is constructed: the syllogism uses universal premises, while the analogy draws on a specific original case (aṣl) and a shared attribute.
2
Trace the middle term / common attribute
In the syllogism 'Every human is an animal; every animal is a body → human is body', the middle term is 'animal'. In the analogy, 'animal' is the common attribute (al-mushtarak) between humans and horses. See how the same term plays a role in both forms.
3
Compare the certainty claim
Logicians claim that tamthil yields only conjecture (ẓann) while shumul yields certainty. Ibn Taymiyya refutes this: if the material (e.g., the causal link between animality and corporeality) is certain, then both forms produce certainty. In fact, he says certainty appears more complete in analogy.
This is the core of his critique.
4
Examine the dispute over causality
If an opponent denies that animality necessitates corporeality, this is actually a dispute over the major premise 'Every animal is a body'. The disagreement is about material truth, not logical form.
Ibn Taymiyya uses this to show that the distinction between the two forms is superficial.
5
Understand the broader critique of syllogism
Ibn Taymiyya asserts that the syllogism is of little benefit because the same knowledge that proves the major premise also proves its instances and the conclusion directly, as in self-evident truths like 'the whole is greater than the part'.
This echoes his earlier arguments about the limited utility of formal syllogistics.

The third station: An example of proving corporeality for man from the two syllogisms. For if the analogy is by 'elimination of the differential', then the specific original case is indispensable, for the common factor is the equality and similarity between them, that is the elimination of the differential, and it is the middle term. And if the analogy is by 'showing the cause', it may suffice without mentioning the original case if the inference upon the causality of the attribute does not depend on it. But if the proof of the causality of the attribute needs it, then the original case is mentioned, for it is part of what indicates the causality of the common factor, and it is the major term. They inform certainty. And those who distinguished between analogical reasoning and syllogistic reasoning began to show that certain materials are conjectural in one of them in specific materials. And those materials that yield only conjecture in analogical reasoning, not in the two syllogisms, yield only conjecture in syllogistic reasoning as well. Otherwise, if they take it in what yields certainty from syllogistic reasoning, it yields certainty in analogical reasoning too, and the manifestation of certainty there is more complete. So if it is said in a syllogism: Every human is an animal, and every animal is a body, then every human is a body, the example of proving corporeality for man: 'animal' is the middle term. And it is the common factor in analogical reasoning by saying: by analogy to the horse and other animals. For that those animals are bodies entails that they are bodies, whether it is the cause or the indicator of the cause, and animality exists in man, so he is a body. And if one disputes the causality of the judgement in the original case, saying: We do not concede that animality necessitates corporeality. This is a dispute over his statement: Every animal is a body. That is because the common factor between the original and the branch, if called a cause, is intended only as 'what necessitates the judgement', whether it is the cause necessitating its existence externally or is necessitating that. Some people call the first 'analogy of cause' and the second 'analogy of indication'. And some people call all of it 'cause', especially those who say that 'cause' is only intended as 'the indicator', i.e., the sign, mark, and proof, not intended as 'the motivator' and 'the incentive'. And whoever says that it may be intended as 'the incentive', i.e., 'the motivator' – and this is the statement of the leading jurists and the majority of Muslims – he says that regarding causes of actions. As for non-actions, 'cause' may be interpreted there as 'the necessitating attribute', like the necessitation of humanity for animality, and animality for corporeality, even if one of the two attributes is not the effecter in the other. Moreover, we have explained elsewhere that what makes known that an animal is a body is also what makes known that a man is a body. For we have shown that the syllogism they mention is of little benefit or none, and that what makes known the truth of the major premise in intellectual matters [also] makes known the truth of its particulars, among which is the minor premise. Rather, by that the truth of the conclusion is also known, as in the statement: 'The whole is greater than the part', and 'Things equal to the same thing are equal to each other', and 'Two opposites do not combine', and 'Two contradictories neither combine nor disappear.'
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of the distinction logicians draw between analogical reasoning (qiyās al-tamthīl) and syllogistic reasoning (qiyās al-shumūl). Ibn Taymiyya uses the concrete example of proving that a human being is a body (jism). He begins by explaining two forms of analogical reasoning: 'elimination of the differential' (ilghā' al-fāriq), which requires a specific original case (aṣl), and 'showing the cause' (ibdā' al-ʿilla), which may or may not require the original case. The common factor between the original and the branch is the middle term (al-ḥadd al-awsaṭ) in syllogistic terms. He then argues that those who separate the two types do so based on the material certainty: they claim that analogical reasoning yields only conjecture (ẓann) in certain materials. Ibn Taymiyya refutes this by stating that if the material is certain in the syllogism, it is equally certain in the analogy; in fact, certainty appears more complete there. He illustrates with the syllogism: Every human is an animal, every animal is a body, so every human is a body. Here 'animal' is the middle term. In the analogical version, one says: by analogy to horses and other animals that are bodies, and since animality exists in humans, humans are bodies. If an opponent disputes the causality (ʿilliyya) of animality for corporeality by denying that animality necessitates corporeality, this is actually a dispute over the major premise of the syllogism ('Every animal is a body'). Ibn Taymiyya then delves into the terminology of 'cause' (ʿilla): some distinguish between 'cause of the ruling' (qiyās ʿilla) and 'indication of the ruling' (qiyās dalāla), while others call all of it 'cause' meaning 'indicator' (muʿarrif). He notes that jurists define 'cause' as the motivator (bāʿith) for actions, but for non-actions, 'cause' can mean the necessitating attribute (al-waṣf al-mustalzim). Finally, he asserts that the syllogism is of little benefit because the same knowledge that proves the major premise also proves its instances and the conclusion itself—as seen in self-evident truths like 'the whole is greater than the part'.

The Syllogism

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Universal Syllogism
Ibn Taymiyya argues that universal syllogism produces no new knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that universal syllogism is epistemically redundant and evaluate its validity.

Core Idea
Universal syllogism adds no new knowledge; the major premise already contains the minor.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya delivers a sharp critique of the universal syllogism (qiyas al-shumul), a cornerstone of Aristotelian logic. He argues that in rational matters, the major premise already entails the truth of its instances, including the minor premise and the conclusion. Therefore, the syllogism is either useless or of minimal benefit. We will walk through his reasoning and then examine its implications for Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target
Locate the term 'قياس الشمول' (universal syllogism) in the Arabic. This is the deductive form Ibn Taymiyya is attacking.
Standard logicians use this form for certain knowledge, but Ibn Taymiyya questions its utility.
2
Extract the core claim
Find the sentence stating that what makes the major premise true also makes its individuals true. The phrase 'ما به يعلم صدق الكبرى في العقليات يعلم صدق أفرادها' is key.
Ibn Taymiyya claims that rational self-evident principles do not need syllogistic instantiation.
3
Examine the supporting examples
The passage lists four self-evident axioms: the whole is greater than the part, things equal to same are equal, opposites do not coexist, contradictories cannot coexist or both be absent. Verify that these axioms typically serve as major premises in syllogisms.
These examples are chosen because their truth is immediately known without inference.
4
Evaluate the argument
Assess whether Ibn Taymiyya's critique holds. If the major premise is self-evident, does that make the syllogism redundant? Consider the role of the minor premise in connecting the conclusion.
This is a debate; logicians would argue that even if the major is known, the syllogism still shows application to a particular case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أنا قد بينا في غير هذا الموضع أن ما به يُعلم كون الحيوان جسماً به يعلم أن الإنسان جسم. حيث بينا أن قياس الشمول الذي يذكرونه قليل الفائدة أو عديمها، وأن ما به يعلم صدقُ الكبرى في العقليات يعلم صدق أفرادها التي منها الصغرى. بل وبذلك يعلم صدق النتيجة، كما في قول القائل : «الكل أعظم من الجزء»، و «الأشياء المساوية لشيء واحد متساوية»، و «الضدان لا يجتمعان»، و «النقيضان لا يجتمعان ولا يرتفعان».
We have already shown elsewhere that what makes known that an animal is a body makes known that man is a body. We showed that the universal syllogism they mention is of little or no benefit, and that what makes known the truth of the major premise in rational matters makes known the truth of its individuals, among which is the minor premise. Rather, by that the truth of the conclusion is known, as in the sayings: 'The whole is greater than the part', 'Things equal to the same thing are equal', 'Two opposites do not coexist', and 'Two contradictories neither coexist nor cease to exist'.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument against the utility of universal syllogism (قياس الشمول). Ibn Taymiyya begins by stating a prior proof: the knowledge that an animal is a body is the same knowledge that man is a body — the major premise already contains the knowledge of its instances. He then argues that the syllogism they use (the universal categorical syllogism) is either of little use or completely fruitless. The reason is that the truth of the major premise (e.g., 'Every whole is greater than its part') already entails the truth of its particular instances (e.g., 'This whole is greater than this part'), which include the minor premise. Consequently, the conclusion is also known from the major premise alone. Ibn Taymiyya supports his point by citing self-evident axioms: 'The whole is greater than the part', 'Things equal to the same thing are equal', 'Two opposites do not coexist', and 'Two contradictories do not coexist and do not cease to exist'. These axioms do not require a syllogism to derive their instances. Thus, the universal syllogism is epistemologically redundant.

Key Terms
قياس الشمول
كبرى
صغرى

Ibn Taymiyya's critique challenges the epistemic utility of universal syllogism, claiming it is redundant when the major premise is self-evident or already known.

Refuting the Analogy of Divine Unity
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the philosophers' analogy for divine unity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key premises of the philosophers' analogy for divine unity and explain Ibn Taymiyya's refutation of the definition of 'sudur' and the conditionality of natural causes.

Core Idea
No agent acts alone except Allah; all natural causes are conditional.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya takes on the philosophers' principle that 'from one only one proceeds' by forcing them to clarify what they mean by 'production' (sudur). He then demonstrates that natural agents like heat and sunlight are never independent—they require a suitable substrate and absence of obstacles. Therefore, their analogy between created causes and God collapses. He reinforces his argument with Quranic verses and a sharp distinction between voluntary and natural agency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the philosophers' claim
Locate the initial statement: 'The hot does not produce except the hot because it is one, and the one does not produce except one.' This is the analogy that Ibn Taymiyya will dismantle.
2
Analyze the ambiguity of 'sudur'
Ibn Taymiyya asks: do you mean independent causation (the agent alone suffices) or partial causation (agent contributes within a set of conditions)? This is crucial because the answer determines whether the analogy holds.
3
Examine the conditionality of natural agents
Take his examples: heat requires a substrate capable of being heated and no hindrance; sunlight requires a reflective body and no obstacle like clouds. These examples show that no created cause is independent.
4
Note the exception: only Allah acts alone
He asserts repeatedly: 'There does not exist in existence any one that acts alone except Allah alone.' This is the core claim. It is supported by Quranic verses about creation and the impossibility of generation (tawallud) from a single being.
5
Understand the role of will and knowledge
He concludes by distinguishing voluntary agency (with will and knowledge) from natural agency (without consciousness). God, being All-Knowing, acts by will, making the analogy with unconscious natural processes invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنهم إذا قالوا : الحار لا يصدر عنه إلا الحار لأنه واحد، والواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا واحد»، فإنه قد يقال لهم : ما تعنون بـ «الصدور» ؟ أتعنون به استقلاله بصدور الأثر عنه»، أو أن يكون سبباً في صدور الأثر بحيث إذا انضم إلى غيره حصل المؤثر التام»؟ ليس في الوجود واحد يفعل وحده إلا الله وحده. فإن أردتم الأول لم نسلّم لكم أن في الوجود ما هو مؤثر تام، ولا شيء مستقل بالفعل غير الله تعالى. والحار الذي أثر حرارة، والبارد الذي أثر برودة، إنما أثر في محل قابل للتسخين والتبريد. فكانت الحرارة الحاصلة في القابل بسببه وبسبب الحارّ معاً. وأيضاً فذلك مشروط بانتفاء العائق المانع وإلا فلو حصل ما يمنع وصول الأثر إليه لم يحصل - وكذلك الشُّعاع، إذا قيل : الشمس مستقلة به»، لم يسلم ذلك . فإنه مشروط بالجسم الذي ينعكس الشعاع عليه ومشروط بعدم المانع، كالسحاب والسَّقْف . وعلى هذا فليس في الوجود واحد يفعل وحده إلا الله وحده. قال تعالى: ﴿وَمِن كُلِّ شَيْءٍ خَلَقْنَا زَوْجَيْنِ لَعَلَّكُمْ تَذَكَّرُونَ ﴾ قال مجاهد وغيره: تذكرون فتعلمون أن خالق الأزواج واحد . قال تعالى: ﴿ أَنَّى يَكُونُ لَهُ وَلَدٌ وَلَمْ تَكُن لَهُ صَحِبَةٌ وَخَلَقَ كُلَّ شَيْءٍ وَهُوَ بِكُلِّ شَيْءٍ عَلِيمٌ ) فنفى التولّد عنه لامتناع التولد من شيء واحد، وأن التولد إنما يكون بين اثنين، وهو سبحانه لا صاحبة له وأيضاً فإنه خلق كل شيء ، وخلقه لكل شيء يناقض أن يتولد عنه شيء. وهو بكل شيء عليم، وعلمه بكل شيء يستلزم أن يكون فاعلاً بإرادته، فإن الشعور فارق بين الفاعل بالإرادة والفاعل بالطبع، فيمتنع مع كونه عالماً أن يكون كالأمور الطبيعية التي تتولد عنها الأشياء بلا شعور، كالحار والبارد . فلا يجوز إضافة الولد إليه بوجه سبحانه
For if they say: 'The hot does not produce except the hot because it is one, and the one does not produce except one,' then it may be said to them: What do you mean by 'production'? Do you mean by it its independence in the emanation of the effect from it, or that it is a cause in the emanation of the effect such that when it is joined to another the complete efficient cause results? There does not exist in existence any one that acts alone except Allah alone. If you mean the first, we do not grant you that there exists in existence any complete efficient cause, nor anything independent in action other than Allah. The hot which caused heat, and the cold which caused coldness, only caused in a substrate capable of being heated and cooled. So the heat occurring in the substrate was due to it and due to the hot together. Also, that is conditioned by the absence of the hindering obstacle; otherwise, if something prevented the effect from reaching it, it would not occur. Similarly, regarding radiation, if it is said that the sun is independent in it, that is not granted. For it is conditioned by the body upon which the radiation is reflected and conditioned by the absence of an obstacle like clouds and a roof. On this basis, there does not exist in existence any one that acts alone except Allah alone. He said: 'And of everything We have created pairs, that you may remember.' Mujahid and others said: You remember and know that the Creator of pairs is one. He said: 'How can He have a son when He has no consort? And He created everything, and He is of everything All-Knowing.' He negated generation from Him because generation from one thing is impossible, and generation only occurs between two, and He has no consort. Also, He created everything, and His creation of everything contradicts that something be generated from Him. And He is All-Knowing of everything, and His knowledge of everything necessitates that He is an agent by His will, for consciousness distinguishes between the agent by will and the agent by nature. So it is impossible, given that He is All-Knowing, that He be like natural things from which things are generated without consciousness, like hot and cold. So it is not permissible to attribute a child to Him in any way, glory be to Him.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' analogical argument for the principle 'from one only one proceeds' as applied to God. Ibn Taymiyqa first questions their definition of 'production' (sudur): does it mean independent causation or partial causation? He argues that no being in existence acts independently except Allah; all other causes are conditional and require substrates and absence of obstacles. He gives examples: heat requires a suitable substrate, the sun's rays require a reflective body. Therefore the philosophers' analogy between natural agents and God fails. He then brings Quranic evidence: God created all things in pairs, negating the possibility of generation from Him alone. He also cites the verse that God has no consort and created everything, which precludes any 'generation' from Him. Finally, he distinguishes between voluntary and natural agents: God is conscious and acts by will, unlike natural things that act without consciousness. Thus the analogy is invalid.

Key Terms
الصدور (al-sudur): Emanation or production of an effect from a cause; here debated whether it means independent causation or partial causation. [Note: In falsafah, sudur often means necessary emanation; Ibn Taymiyya challenges this necessitarian view.]
المؤثر التام (al-mu'aththir al-tamm): Complete efficient cause, one that is sufficient alone without any other factor. [Note: Standard term in kalam and falsafah; Ibn Taymiyya uses it to argue that only God is such.]
التولد (al-tawallud): Generation or natural production, specifically the coming into being of something from another without deliberate will. [Note: In Islamic thought, tawallud is often discussed in the context of causality; here it is negated for God.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, why is the analogy of heat producing heat not applicable to God?
A) Because heat is not a created thing.
B) Because heat production requires a substrate and is conditional, whereas God's action is not.
C) Because God is not one.
D) Because analogy is always invalid.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya shows that the philosophers' analogy fails because natural causes are never independent, and only Allah acts alone, a truth confirmed by the Quran and rational analysis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify an analogy used in theological debates (e.g., 'God's knowledge is like human knowledge because both know'). Apply Ibn Taymiyya's method: define key terms, check if the base of the analogy is independent or conditional, and see if the analogy holds. Write a brief analysis.
?
Construct a refutation of the claim: 'The sun produces light independently, so God produces the world independently in the same way.' Use the concepts of substrate, conditionality, and the distinction between voluntary and natural agency.
