The Syllogism
advanced
15 min
Refuting the Argument that Attributes Entail Composition
Refutation of the attribute-negators' syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the attribute-negators' syllogism and identify the ambiguous terms that, according to Ibn Taymiyya, make it sophistical.

Core Idea
Ambiguous terms turn a logical proof into sophistry.

The attribute-negators argued that if God had attributes, He would be composite, and every composite is dependent and therefore not necessary. Ibn Taymiyya refutes this by showing that their key terms—'composite', 'dependence', 'other'—are ambiguous. In this lesson, we will dissect their syllogism and apply Ibn Taymiyya's method of clarification to expose its weakness.

Guided Path
1
Reconstruct the attribute-negators' syllogism
Extract the premises and conclusion from the passage. Their argument: (1) Every composite is dependent on its parts. (2) Its part is other than it. Therefore, the composite is dependent on another. (3) What is dependent on another is not necessary in itself. Conclusion: So if God had attributes, He would be composite and hence not necessary.
This is a chain of two syllogisms.
2
Identify the ambiguous terms
Ibn Taymiyya points out that 'composite', 'dependence', and 'other' are ambiguous. 'Composite' could mean physical composition or having inseparable attributes. 'Dependence' could mean required concomitance rather than causal dependence. 'Other' could mean separate or separable.
Each term admits a true and a false interpretation.
3
Apply Ibn Taymiyya's clarifications
For each term, substitute the clarified meaning. If 'composite' means 'a self with inseparable attributes', then the claim 'every composite is dependent' becomes trivial (it is necessarily concomitant, not causally dependent). The part is not 'other' in the sense of separate. Thus the conclusion that God is not necessary does not follow.
The ambiguity allows the argument to appear valid but is actually fallacious.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجواهر المفردة، أو من أجزاء عقلية، وهي المادة ،والصورة أو لا من هذا ولا من هذا، على ثلاثة أقوال والصحيح عندنا هو القول الثالث . ثم يليه قول من جعله مركباً من الأجزاء الحسّيّة. وأفسد الثلاثة قول من جعله مركباً من الأجزاء العقلية، كما قد بسط في موضعه . وحينئذ فمن قال : إن الباري جسم، وإن الجسم مركب من الأجزاء الحسّية أو العقلية، كان الاستدلال على بطلان هذا التركيب استدلالاً مقبولاً ممن يقوله فإن عليه تعالى مطلوبه صحيح ، لكن يبقى النظر هل يُحسِن هذا المستدل الاستدلال عليه أو لا يحسنه . وأما من قال: إنه ليس بمركّب، لا هذا التركيب ولا هذا التركيب، وإنما أسمّيه جسماً أو جوهراً، لأن الجسم والجوهر عندي اسم لكل موجود قائم بنفسه، فهذا النزاع معه في اسم الجسم والجوهر نزاع لفظي، لا عقلي ولا شرعي. فإن الشرع لم ينطق بهذا الاسم لا نفياً ولا إثباتاً، والعقل إنما ينظر في المعاني لا في مجرد اللفظ . فالنظر مع هذا إما في إثبات كون الجسم مركباً أحد التركيبين، وهذا بحث عقلي معروف ؛ وإما في كون لفظ الجسم في اللغة لكل مركب، وهذا مركب، وهذا بحث لغوي له موضع آخر . نفي الصفات ، وهؤلاء ليس مقصودهم بنفي التركيب هذا المعنى فقط، فإن هذا يوافقهم عليه كثير من مثبتة الصفات، لكن مقصودهم أنه لا يتصف بصفة ثبوتية أصلاً. وأخذوا لفظ التركيب الذي وافقهم بعضُ أهل الكلام على نفي معناه، وتوسعوا فيه حتى جعلوه أعمّ مما وافقهم عليه أولئك المتكلمون ونفوه وصاروا كالجهمية المحضة التي تنفي الأسماء والصفات، أو تثبتها على وجه المجاز . والمقصود هنا أن نقول قولهم : الموصوف بالحياة، والعلم، والقدرة، مركب من هذا وهذا يقال لهم : سموا هذا تركيباً، أو تجسيماً، أو ما شئتم فما الدليل على نفي هذا عقلاً أو سمعاً؟ قالوا : الدليل على ذلك أن كل مركب مفتقر إلى أجزائه، وجزؤه غيره . فالمركب مفتقر إلى غيره، والمفتقر إلى غيره ليس واجباً بنفسه . فيقال : أجزاء هذا الدليل وألفاظه التي تسمونها حدوداً كلها ألفاظ مجملة تحتمل حقاً وباطلاً واستعمال الألفاظ المجملة في الحدود والقياس من باب السفسطة . فيقال لكم: قد عرف أن لفظ المركب مجمل، وأن المراد به هنا ذات تقوم بها الافتقار هو صفات. وحينئذ فالمراد بالافتقار تلازم الذات والصفات بمعنى أنه لا توجد الذات إلا مع وجود صفتها الملازمة لها، ولا توجد الصفة إلا مع وجود الذات الملازمة لها. ولو قدر أنه أريد بالتركيب التركيب من الأجزاء الحسية أو العقلية مع تلازم الأجزاء، فهذا معناه . فإذا قيل : كل مركب مفتقر إلى جزئه، إن عني به أنه مستلزم لجزئه، وأنه لا وجود يوجد إلا بوجود جزئه، فهذا صحيح. فإن وجود المجموع بدون كلّ من آحاده ممتنع . وإن أريد أنه مفتقر إليه افتقار المفعول إلى فاعله والمعلول إلى علته الفاعلة، أو القابلة، أو الغائية أو الصورية فهذا باطل فإن الواحد من العشرة، والجزء من الجملة : لا يجوز أن يكون فاعلاً، ولا غاية، ولا محلاً لغيره، ولا هو الصورة . ثم قولكم وجزؤه غيره ، يقال : لفظ الغير يراد به ما كان مبايناً للشيء، وما يجوز مفارقته له وما ليس إياه. فإن أردتم أن جزء المجموع ما هو مباين له باطل، فإنه يمتنع أن يكون مبايناً له مع كونه جزءاً منه فيمتنع أن يكون غيراً له بهذا الاعتبار. وإن قلتم : يجوز أن يفارقه، فهذا ليس بمسلم على الإطلاق، بل يجوز في بعض الأفراد أن يفارق غيره من الأجزاء، ويفارق المجموع الذي هو الهيئة الاجتماعية . ولا يلزم ذلك في كل مجموع ، لا سيما على أصلهم . فإن الفلك عندهم مركب من أجزائه وصفاته، ولا يجوز عندهم على أجزائه التفرق . والمسلمون وجمهور العقلاء أن الله حي، عليم، قدير؛ ولا يجوز أن تكون صفات الله مفارقة له كونه حياً عالماً قادراً؛ بل لم يزل ولا يزال كذلك. وكونه حياً، عالماً، قادراً، لوازم ذاته
The simple substances, or from intellectual parts, which are matter and form, or from neither of these, according to three opinions, and the correct one in our view is the third. Then follows the opinion of one who made it composed of physical parts. And the most corrupt of the three is the opinion of one who made it composed of intellectual parts, as has been expanded upon in its place. Then, whoever says that the Creator is a body, and that the body is composed of physical or intellectual parts, then the inference upon the invalidity of this composition is an acceptable inference from whoever says it, for his desired conclusion concerning the Exalted is correct, but the consideration remains whether this inferrer infers it well or not. As for one who says that it is not composite, neither this composition nor that composition, but rather I call it a body or a substance, because 'body' and 'substance' in my view are names for every existent that subsists by itself, then this dispute with him over the name 'body' and 'substance' is a verbal dispute, not intellectual or legal. For the Law has not spoken with this name, neither negation nor affirmation, and the intellect only considers meanings, not mere wording. So the consideration with this is either in proving that 'body' is composed of one of the two compositions, and this is a known intellectual inquiry, or in that the word 'body' in the language is for every composite, and this is composite, and this is a linguistic inquiry that has another place. Negation of attributes. These people do not intend by negating composition only this meaning, for many attribute-affirmers agree with them on this, but their intention is that He is not described by any affirmative attribute at all. They took the word 'composition' which some of the theologians agreed with them on negating its meaning, and expanded it until they made it more general than what those theologians agreed with them on, and they negated it, and they became like the pure Jahmiyya who negate the names and attributes, or affirm them figuratively. What we intend here is to say: their statement that the one described by life, knowledge, and power is composed of this and that. It is said to them: Name this 'composition' or 'incarnation' (tajsim) or whatever you wish, what is the proof for negating this, intellectually or by transmission? They said: The proof for that is that every composite is dependent on its parts, and its part is other than it. So the composite is dependent on what is other than it, and what is dependent on what is other than it is not necessary by itself. So it is said: The parts of this proof and its words, which you call 'definitions', are all ambiguous words that admit truth and falsehood, and the use of ambiguous words in definitions and syllogisms is from the category of sophistry. So it is said to you: It is known that the word 'composite' is ambiguous, and what is meant by it here is a self that possesses dependence, which is attributes. Then what is meant by 'dependence' is the mutual necessity of the self and the attributes, meaning that the self does not exist except with the existence of its inseparable attribute, and the attribute does not exist except with the existence of the self that is inseparable from it. And if it were supposed that what is meant by 'composition' is composition from physical or intellectual parts with the mutual necessity of the parts, then this is its meaning. So when it is said: Every composite is dependent on its part, if what is meant is that it requires its part and that it does not exist except with the existence of its part, then this is correct. For the existence of the whole without each of its individuals is impossible. But if what is meant is that it is dependent on it with the dependence of the effect on its agent, the effect on its efficient cause, or the material, or the final, or the formal cause, then this is false, for the one out of ten, and the part of the whole, cannot be an agent, nor a final cause, nor a locus for something else, nor is it the form. Then your statement: 'And its part is other than it.' It is said: The word 'other' is used to mean what is separate from the thing, and what may be separated from it, and what is not it. If you intend that the part of the whole is what is separate from it, it is false, for it is impossible for it to be separate from it while being a part of it, so it is impossible for it to be 'other' than it in this sense. And if you say: It may be separated from it, this is not conceded absolutely; rather, it is possible in some individuals that a part is separated from another part, and separated from the whole which is the social configuration. But this does not necessarily follow in every whole, especially according to their own principle. For the celestial sphere, according to them, is composed of its parts and attributes, and according to them it is not permissible for its parts to separate. And the Muslims and the majority of rational people hold that God is living, knowing, powerful; and it is not permissible for the attributes of God to be separated from Him being living, knowing, powerful; rather, He has never ceased and will never cease to be such. And His being living, knowing, powerful are necessary concomitants of His essence.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation by Ibn Taymiyya of the logicians' argument that God cannot have attributes because that would necessitate composition (tarkib) and hence contingency. He first contextualizes the debate by discussing different definitions of 'body' (jism) and composition. He notes three views on what a body is composed of: simple substances, intellectual parts (matter and form), or neither. He states the correct view is the third, and that the most corrupt is that it is composed of intellectual parts. Then he considers the case of someone who asserts that God is a body: if they mean a body composed of physical or intellectual parts, the argument against such composition is acceptable, but one must check the validity of the inference. However, if someone merely uses the word 'body' or 'substance' as a name for any self-subsisting existent, the dispute is merely verbal, not rational or religious, since the Law does not mention these terms, and reason considers meanings not words. Then Ibn Taymiyya turns to the main target: the attribute-negators (nufāt al-ṣifāt). They do not merely reject physical composition; they intend to deny all positive attributes entirely. They have taken the term 'composition' and expanded its meaning to include any attribute, thus turning the debate into a sophistical one. He presents their argument: 'Every composite is dependent (muftaqir) on its parts; its part is other than it; so the composite is dependent on another; what is dependent on another is not necessary in itself.' Therefore, since God would be composite if He had attributes, He cannot have them. Ibn Taymiyya's refutation focuses on the ambiguity (ijmāl) of the key terms 'composite', 'dependence', and 'other'. He argues that these terms are used in definitions (ḥudūd) and syllogisms (qiyās), but their ambiguity makes the reasoning sophistical. He clarifies that by 'composite' here they mean a self that possesses attributes, and by 'dependence' they mean the necessary concomitance (talāzum) between essence and attributes. He then analyzes the statement 'Every composite is dependent on its part': if it means that the whole cannot exist without its parts, that is true. But if it means dependence like that of an effect on its efficient cause, etc., that is false, because parts are not causes of the whole. Similarly, the word 'other' (ghayr) can mean separate (mubāyin) or separable. A part cannot be separate from the whole while being a part; nor is it always separable. For example, the celestial sphere (al-falak) according to the philosophers is composed of parts and attributes that cannot separate. Finally, he asserts the Islamic position: God has always been and will always be living, knowing, powerful—these attributes are necessary concomitants (lawāzim) of His essence, not separable parts.

Key Terms
المركب
الافتقار
الغير
السفسطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what is the main flaw in the attribute-negators' syllogism?
A) It is based on scripture, not reason.
B) It uses ambiguous terms that admit truth and falsehood.
C) It is too long and complex.
D) It denies the attribute of life.

Key Takeaway
Ambiguous terms in a syllogism can turn a seemingly valid argument into sophistry; Ibn Taymiyya teaches us to clarify meanings before accepting logical conclusions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Reconstruct the attribute-negators' syllogism as Ibn Taymiyya presents it. Then, rewrite the syllogism after replacing each ambiguous term with two possible clarifications (e.g., 'composite' as physical composition vs. having attributes). Determine which interpretation makes the premises true and whether the conclusion follows in each case.

Identifying Verbal Disputes: The Definition of 'Body' and 'Composition'
Introduction to definitions of body and composition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between substantive and verbal disputes in theological arguments by analyzing the different definitions of 'body' and 'composition' presented by Ibn Taymiyya.

Core Idea
Not every disagreement is substantive; some are merely verbal.

Before tackling the attribute-negators' argument, Ibn Taymiyya discusses the definition of 'body' (jism) and composition. He shows that disputes over whether God is a body can be verbal if the term is defined loosely. In this lesson, we will learn to identify such verbal disputes and understand why they are irrelevant to the real theological issues.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three definitions of body
Ibn Taymiyya lists three opinions on what a body is composed of: simple substances, intellectual parts (matter and form), or neither (the correct view). Then there is a view that it is composed of physical parts. Understand these definitions.
2
Analyze the two scenarios for calling God a body
If someone says God is a body meaning physical or intellectual composition, the argument against such composition is acceptable but must be checked for validity. But if someone calls God a body merely meaning a self-subsisting existent, the dispute is only verbal.
Ibn Taymiyya says the Law does not use these terms, and reason considers meanings, not words.
3
Recognize the verbal dispute
A dispute is verbal when both parties agree on the underlying reality but disagree on the label. Here, if the attribute-negators and attribute-affirmers agree that God is not composed physically, but differ on using the word 'body', it is a verbal dispute.
Ibn Taymiyya dismisses such disputes as irrelevant to real theological inquiry.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجواهر المفردة، أو من أجزاء عقلية، وهي المادة ،والصورة أو لا من هذا ولا من هذا، على ثلاثة أقوال والصحيح عندنا هو القول الثالث . ثم يليه قول من جعله مركباً من الأجزاء الحسّيّة. وأفسد الثلاثة قول من جعله مركباً من الأجزاء العقلية، كما قد بسط في موضعه . وحينئذ فمن قال : إن الباري جسم، وإن الجسم مركب من الأجزاء الحسّية أو العقلية، كان الاستدلال على بطلان هذا التركيب استدلالاً مقبولاً ممن يقوله فإن عليه تعالى مطلوبه صحيح ، لكن يبقى النظر هل يُحسِن هذا المستدل الاستدلال عليه أو لا يحسنه . وأما من قال: إنه ليس بمركّب، لا هذا التركيب ولا هذا التركيب، وإنما أسمّيه جسماً أو جوهراً، لأن الجسم والجوهر عندي اسم لكل موجود قائم بنفسه، فهذا النزاع معه في اسم الجسم والجوهر نزاع لفظي، لا عقلي ولا شرعي. فإن الشرع لم ينطق بهذا الاسم لا نفياً ولا إثباتاً، والعقل إنما ينظر في المعاني لا في مجرد اللفظ . فالنظر مع هذا إما في إثبات كون الجسم مركباً أحد التركيبين، وهذا بحث عقلي معروف ؛ وإما في كون لفظ الجسم في اللغة لكل مركب، وهذا مركب، وهذا بحث لغوي له موضع آخر . نفي الصفات ، وهؤلاء ليس مقصودهم بنفي التركيب هذا المعنى فقط، فإن هذا يوافقهم عليه كثير من مثبتة الصفات، لكن مقصودهم أنه لا يتصف بصفة ثبوتية أصلاً. وأخذوا لفظ التركيب الذي وافقهم بعضُ أهل الكلام على نفي معناه، وتوسعوا فيه حتى جعلوه أعمّ مما وافقهم عليه أولئك المتكلمون ونفوه وصاروا كالجهمية المحضة التي تنفي الأسماء والصفات، أو تثبتها على وجه المجاز . والمقصود هنا أن نقول قولهم : الموصوف بالحياة، والعلم، والقدرة، مركب من هذا وهذا يقال لهم : سموا هذا تركيباً، أو تجسيماً، أو ما شئتم فما الدليل على نفي هذا عقلاً أو سمعاً؟ قالوا : الدليل على ذلك أن كل مركب مفتقر إلى أجزائه، وجزؤه غيره . فالمركب مفتقر إلى غيره، والمفتقر إلى غيره ليس واجباً بنفسه . فيقال : أجزاء هذا الدليل وألفاظه التي تسمونها حدوداً كلها ألفاظ مجملة تحتمل حقاً وباطلاً واستعمال الألفاظ المجملة في الحدود والقياس من باب السفسطة . فيقال لكم: قد عرف أن لفظ المركب مجمل، وأن المراد به هنا ذات تقوم بها الافتقار هو صفات. وحينئذ فالمراد بالافتقار تلازم الذات والصفات بمعنى أنه لا توجد الذات إلا مع وجود صفتها الملازمة لها، ولا توجد الصفة إلا مع وجود الذات الملازمة لها. ولو قدر أنه أريد بالتركيب التركيب من الأجزاء الحسية أو العقلية مع تلازم الأجزاء، فهذا معناه . فإذا قيل : كل مركب مفتقر إلى جزئه، إن عني به أنه مستلزم لجزئه، وأنه لا وجود يوجد إلا بوجود جزئه، فهذا صحيح. فإن وجود المجموع بدون كلّ من آحاده ممتنع . وإن أريد أنه مفتقر إليه افتقار المفعول إلى فاعله والمعلول إلى علته الفاعلة، أو القابلة، أو الغائية أو الصورية فهذا باطل فإن الواحد من العشرة، والجزء من الجملة : لا يجوز أن يكون فاعلاً، ولا غاية، ولا محلاً لغيره، ولا هو الصورة . ثم قولكم وجزؤه غيره ، يقال : لفظ الغير يراد به ما كان مبايناً للشيء، وما يجوز مفارقته له وما ليس إياه. فإن أردتم أن جزء المجموع ما هو مباين له باطل، فإنه يمتنع أن يكون مبايناً له مع كونه جزءاً منه فيمتنع أن يكون غيراً له بهذا الاعتبار. وإن قلتم : يجوز أن يفارقه، فهذا ليس بمسلم على الإطلاق، بل يجوز في بعض الأفراد أن يفارق غيره من الأجزاء، ويفارق المجموع الذي هو الهيئة الاجتماعية . ولا يلزم ذلك في كل مجموع ، لا سيما على أصلهم . فإن الفلك عندهم مركب من أجزائه وصفاته، ولا يجوز عندهم على أجزائه التفرق . والمسلمون وجمهور العقلاء أن الله حي، عليم، قدير؛ ولا يجوز أن تكون صفات الله مفارقة له كونه حياً عالماً قادراً؛ بل لم يزل ولا يزال كذلك. وكونه حياً، عالماً، قادراً، لوازم ذاته
The simple substances, or from intellectual parts, which are matter and form, or from neither of these, according to three opinions, and the correct one in our view is the third. Then follows the opinion of one who made it composed of physical parts. And the most corrupt of the three is the opinion of one who made it composed of intellectual parts, as has been expanded upon in its place. Then, whoever says that the Creator is a body, and that the body is composed of physical or intellectual parts, then the inference upon the invalidity of this composition is an acceptable inference from whoever says it, for his desired conclusion concerning the Exalted is correct, but the consideration remains whether this inferrer infers it well or not. As for one who says that it is not composite, neither this composition nor that composition, but rather I call it a body or a substance, because 'body' and 'substance' in my view are names for every existent that subsists by itself, then this dispute with him over the name 'body' and 'substance' is a verbal dispute, not intellectual or legal. For the Law has not spoken with this name, neither negation nor affirmation, and the intellect only considers meanings, not mere wording. So the consideration with this is either in proving that 'body' is composed of one of the two compositions, and this is a known intellectual inquiry, or in that the word 'body' in the language is for every composite, and this is composite, and this is a linguistic inquiry that has another place. Negation of attributes. These people do not intend by negating composition only this meaning, for many attribute-affirmers agree with them on this, but their intention is that He is not described by any affirmative attribute at all. They took the word 'composition' which some of the theologians agreed with them on negating its meaning, and expanded it until they made it more general than what those theologians agreed with them on, and they negated it, and they became like the pure Jahmiyya who negate the names and attributes, or affirm them figuratively. What we intend here is to say: their statement that the one described by life, knowledge, and power is composed of this and that. It is said to them: Name this 'composition' or 'incarnation' (tajsim) or whatever you wish, what is the proof for negating this, intellectually or by transmission? They said: The proof for that is that every composite is dependent on its parts, and its part is other than it. So the composite is dependent on what is other than it, and what is dependent on what is other than it is not necessary by itself. So it is said: The parts of this proof and its words, which you call 'definitions', are all ambiguous words that admit truth and falsehood, and the use of ambiguous words in definitions and syllogisms is from the category of sophistry. So it is said to you: It is known that the word 'composite' is ambiguous, and what is meant by it here is a self that possesses dependence, which is attributes. Then what is meant by 'dependence' is the mutual necessity of the self and the attributes, meaning that the self does not exist except with the existence of its inseparable attribute, and the attribute does not exist except with the existence of the self that is inseparable from it. And if it were supposed that what is meant by 'composition' is composition from physical or intellectual parts with the mutual necessity of the parts, then this is its meaning. So when it is said: Every composite is dependent on its part, if what is meant is that it requires its part and that it does not exist except with the existence of its part, then this is correct. For the existence of the whole without each of its individuals is impossible. But if what is meant is that it is dependent on it with the dependence of the effect on its agent, the effect on its efficient cause, or the material, or the final, or the formal cause, then this is false, for the one out of ten, and the part of the whole, cannot be an agent, nor a final cause, nor a locus for something else, nor is it the form. Then your statement: 'And its part is other than it.' It is said: The word 'other' is used to mean what is separate from the thing, and what may be separated from it, and what is not it. If you intend that the part of the whole is what is separate from it, it is false, for it is impossible for it to be separate from it while being a part of it, so it is impossible for it to be 'other' than it in this sense. And if you say: It may be separated from it, this is not conceded absolutely; rather, it is possible in some individuals that a part is separated from another part, and separated from the whole which is the social configuration. But this does not necessarily follow in every whole, especially according to their own principle. For the celestial sphere, according to them, is composed of its parts and attributes, and according to them it is not permissible for its parts to separate. And the Muslims and the majority of rational people hold that God is living, knowing, powerful; and it is not permissible for the attributes of God to be separated from Him being living, knowing, powerful; rather, He has never ceased and will never cease to be such. And His being living, knowing, powerful are necessary concomitants of His essence.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation by Ibn Taymiyya of the logicians' argument that God cannot have attributes because that would necessitate composition (tarkib) and hence contingency. He first contextualizes the debate by discussing different definitions of 'body' (jism) and composition. He notes three views on what a body is composed of: simple substances, intellectual parts (matter and form), or neither. He states the correct view is the third, and that the most corrupt is that it is composed of intellectual parts. Then he considers the case of someone who asserts that God is a body: if they mean a body composed of physical or intellectual parts, the argument against such composition is acceptable, but one must check the validity of the inference. However, if someone merely uses the word 'body' or 'substance' as a name for any self-subsisting existent, the dispute is merely verbal, not rational or religious, since the Law does not mention these terms, and reason considers meanings not words. Then Ibn Taymiyya turns to the main target: the attribute-negators (nufāt al-ṣifāt). They do not merely reject physical composition; they intend to deny all positive attributes entirely. They have taken the term 'composition' and expanded its meaning to include any attribute, thus turning the debate into a sophistical one. He presents their argument: 'Every composite is dependent (muftaqir) on its parts; its part is other than it; so the composite is dependent on another; what is dependent on another is not necessary in itself.' Therefore, since God would be composite if He had attributes, He cannot have them. Ibn Taymiyya's refutation focuses on the ambiguity (ijmāl) of the key terms 'composite', 'dependence', and 'other'. He argues that these terms are used in definitions (ḥudūd) and syllogisms (qiyās), but their ambiguity makes the reasoning sophistical. He clarifies that by 'composite' here they mean a self that possesses attributes, and by 'dependence' they mean the necessary concomitance (talāzum) between essence and attributes. He then analyzes the statement 'Every composite is dependent on its part': if it means that the whole cannot exist without its parts, that is true. But if it means dependence like that of an effect on its efficient cause, etc., that is false, because parts are not causes of the whole. Similarly, the word 'other' (ghayr) can mean separate (mubāyin) or separable. A part cannot be separate from the whole while being a part; nor is it always separable. For example, the celestial sphere (al-falak) according to the philosophers is composed of parts and attributes that cannot separate. Finally, he asserts the Islamic position: God has always been and will always be living, knowing, powerful—these attributes are necessary concomitants (lawāzim) of His essence, not separable parts.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, when does a dispute over whether God is a body become merely verbal?
A) When both parties agree that God is not composed of parts but differ on the name.
B) When the disputants cannot agree on the definition.
C) When the dispute is based on scripture.
D) When one party uses reason and the other uses revelation.

Key Takeaway
Not all theological disputes are substantive; some are merely verbal, and Ibn Taymiyya teaches us to identify and set them aside.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find a contemporary theological debate (e.g., over the term 'metaphor' in divine attributes) and apply Ibn Taymiyya's method to determine if it is a verbal dispute. Explain your reasoning.
?
Identify a term in Islamic theology that has multiple definitions (e.g., 'iman'). Discuss how different definitions lead to different theological conclusions, and whether the dispute is verbal or substantive.

The Syllogism
advanced
12 min
Redefining 'Other' and 'Need' in the Divine Context
Ibn Taymiyya redefines 'other' and 'need' to deny causal implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply Ibn Taymiyya's redefinition of 'other' (ghayr) and 'need' (iftiqar) to evaluate whether divine attributes entail causal dependence.

Core Idea
Redefining terms dismantles the logicians' causal argument before it begins.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya targets the conceptual foundation of the logicians' claim that God's essence and attributes form a composite requiring a cause. He argues that their use of 'other' and 'need' is ambiguous. By clarifying these terms, he shows that the supposed dependence is merely concomitance, not genuine causal need. This lesson trains you to identify and apply such terminological refinements—a key tool in theological dialectic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' original claim
Find the logicians' assertion that the composite of God's essence and attributes is in need of another (i.e., a cause). What exactly do they mean by 'other' and 'need'?
The logicians typically treat 'other' as anything distinct and 'need' as causal dependence.
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's redefinition of 'other' (ghayr)
He restricts 'other' to that which is separable or not part of the composite. Attributes that are necessary concomitants are not 'other' in this sense.
Contrast with the logicians' broader sense: conceptual distinctness is not enough for 'otherness'.
3
Extract his redefinition of 'need' (iftiqar)
He reinterprets 'need' as mere concomitant relation (luzum), not causal dependence. The attribute's need for the essence is like a state needing its locus.
This shifts the meaning from efficient causality to receptive dependence.
4
Apply the redefinitions to the divine context
Show that the attribute's dependence on the essence does not imply an efficient, final, or formal cause. It is only a receptive relation, which the logicians themselves sometimes call a 'receptive cause'.
Thus, the logicians' premise that 'what needs another is not necessary' is rendered harmless.
5
Evaluate the shift's effect
Determine how this redefinition neutralizes the logicians' objection. Does it fully refute them or only clarify the terms?
Ibn Taymiyya uses this to deny any external cause for the composite.

من لوازم ذاته، وهي ملازمة لذاته، لا يجوز عليها الافتراقُ بوجه من الوجوه. فامتنع أن تكون صفاته هذه «أغياراً» بهذا الاعتبار.
وإن فُسّر الغَيْرانِ) بما ليس أحدهما هو الآخر، أو بما [٢١٧] يجوز العلم بأحدهما مع عدم العلم بالآخر ؛ فلا ريب أن صفة الموصوف التي يمكن معرفتها «غير» بالموصوف له بهذا الاعتبار. لكن إذا كانت تلك الصفة لازمة له وهو لازم لها، لم يكن في ذلك ما أن يكون أحدهما مفتقراً إلى الآخر، مفعولاً للآخر، ولا علة فاعلة، ولا غائية، ولا صُورية. أكثر ما في ذلك أن تكون الصفة مفتقرة إلى الذات افتقار الحال إلى محله القابل له . وهم يسمون القابل علة قابلة لكن فيما يحدث لها من المقبولات، لا فيما يكون لازماً لها أزلاً وأبداً. وإن قدر أنهم يسمون جميع ذلك «علة» و «معلولاً»، فتكون الذات علة قابلة للصفة بهذا الاعتبار. وكون الصفة «معلولة) هو معنى كونها صفة قائمة بالموصوف .
فهذا فليس مجموع الذات والصفة مفتقراً إلى شيء من أنواع العلل الأربع، لا افتقار الحال إلى الفاعل ولا إلى الغاية ولا إلى القابل، ولا إلى الصورة فبطل أن يقال : المجموع مفتقر إلى جزئه افتقار المعلول إلى علته بوجه من الوجوه، لكن غايته أن فيه افتقار الصفة إلى الموصوف افتقار الحال اللازم لمحله إلى محله المستلزم له. فيقال لهم : وأي شيء في كونه موجوداً بنفسه لا فاعِل له ما يوجب نفي هذا التلازم الذي سموه «افتقاراً» نحو ذاته وصفاته؟
وقولهم : : «ما افتقر إلى غيره لم يكن واجباً بنفسه، يقال لهم : قد علم أن المراد بـ «الافتقار ) : التلازم ؛ والمراد بـ «الغير»: ما هو داخل في المجموع، إما الذات وإما الصفات، ليس المراد به ما هو مباين له، وما يجوز مُفارقته ،له وغايته أن يراد أن الصفة لا بد لها من الموصوف . فليس المراد افتقارُ المعلول إلى علته الفاعلة. وحينئذ فليس في هذا التلازم الذي سميتموه «افتقاراً» ولا في هذه الصفات التي سميتموها «أغياراً» [٢١٨] ما يوجب أن يكون شيء من ذلك مفعولاً لفاعل ولا لعلة فاعلة
المقام الثالث - مجموع الذات والصفة لا يفتقر إلى العلل الأربع
وواجب الوجود الذي دلت الممكنات عليه هو الموجود بنفسه، القائم بنفسه، ربُّ العالمين، الذي لا يفتقر إلى فاعل ولا علة فاعلة، بل هو نفسه نفسه وصفاته لا يفتقر إلى شيء من العلل الأربع .

كون صفاته تعالى واجبة الوجود»
وأما نفس صفاته فليس لها ،فاعل ولا علة فاعلة ولا علة غائية، ولا صُورية .
فهي واجبة الوجود إذا عُني بـ «واجب الوجود أحد هذه المعاني .
وإن عنى بـ واجب الوجود» ما هو أعم من ذلك حتى يدخل فيه ما ليس له مَحَلٌّ كون الذات محلا للصفات يقوم به فليست واجبة الوجود بهذا التفسير، بل هي ممكنة الوجود، والذات مستلزمة لها، وهي محلّ لها . وإذا قيل : «فيلزم أن تكون الذات فاعلة وقابلة وذاك «باطل»، قيل : كلا المقدمتين امتناع كون الذات فاعلة لصفتها ممنوعة . فإن كون الذات مستلزمة لصفتها القائمة بها لا يقتضي أن تكون فاعلة لها؛ بل يمتنع أن تكون الذاتُ فاعلة للصفة اللازمة في الممكن ، فكيف في رب العالمين؟ بل أن يكون الرب فاعلاً لما هو لازم له وإن كان بائناً عنه، كالفلك، فكيف يكون فاعلاً لصفته اللازمة له؟ وإن قدر أنهم سموا هذا الاستلزام «فعلاً»، وقالوا : فاعلة هي بهذا الاعتبار قيل لهم فلا نسلم أنه لا يجوز كون الشيء الواحد فاعلاً وقابلاً بهذا الاعتبار، بل ولا باعتبار آخر .
فإن استدلوا على ذلك بحجتهم المعروفة المبنية على نفي التركيب، فلا يمكنهم إثبات كل جعل ذلك مقدمة في نفس الدليل، لأن هذا مُصَادَرَةٌ على المطلوب، وهو جعل من المقدمتين المطلوب مقدمة في إثبات نفسه بعبارة أخرى . فإنهم إذا نفوا التركيب» عن «الواجب» بالأخرى بناءً على مقدمات، منها : أن الواحد لا يكون فاعلاً وقابلاً ، وأثبتوا هذه المقدمة بناءً على نفي «التركيب»، كانوا قد أثبتوا كلاً من المقدمتين بالأخرى، فلا تكون واحدة منهما ثابتة .
(from the necessary concomitants of His essence, and it is inseparable from His essence; it is not permissible for separation to occur in any way. So it is impossible that His attributes be 'other' in this consideration. And if 'the two others' are interpreted as that which is not the one the other, or as that which [٢١٧] it is possible to know one of them without knowledge of the other, then there is no doubt that the attribute of the described which can be known is 'other' than the described in this consideration. But if that attribute is inseparable from Him and He is inseparable from it, then there is in that nothing that one of them is in need of the other, an effect of the other, nor an efficient cause, nor a final cause, nor a formal cause. The most that is in that is that the attribute is in need of the essence with the need of a state for its receptive locus. And they call the receptive locus a receptive cause, but regarding what occurs to it of receptions, not regarding what is inseparable from it eternally and forever. And if it is assumed that they call all of that 'cause' and 'effect', then the essence is a receptive cause for the attribute in this consideration. And the attribute being 'effected' is the meaning of its being an attribute subsisting in the described. So this is that the totality of essence and attribute is not in need of any of the four types of causes, neither the need of the state for the agent nor for the final cause nor for the receptive cause nor for the formal cause. So it is false to say: the totality is in need of its part with the need of the effect for its cause in any respect. Rather, its ultimate extent is that in it there is the need of the attribute for the described, the need of the necessary state for its locus that entails it. So it is said to them: What is there in its being existent by itself, having no agent, that necessitates the negation of this concomitance which they called 'need' regarding His essence and attributes? And their saying: 'What is in need of another is not necessary by itself.' It is said to them: It is known that what is meant by 'need' is concomitance; and what is meant by 'other' is that which is included in the totality, either the essence or the attributes, not what is separate from it and what is permissible to separate from it. Its ultimate extent is that the attribute must have the described. So what is meant is not the need of the effect for its efficient cause. Therefore, there is not in this concomitance which you called 'need' nor in these attributes which you called 'others' [٢١٨] anything that necessitates that any of that be an effect of an agent or of an efficient cause. The third station - The totality of essence and attribute is not in need of the four causes. And the Necessary Existence which the contingent things indicate is the existent by itself, the self-subsistent, the Lord of the worlds, who is not in need of an agent nor an efficient cause. Rather, He is Himself and His attributes are not in need of any of the four causes. [٢٢٨] / The fact that His attributes are necessary of existence. And as for His attributes themselves, they have no agent nor efficient cause nor final cause nor formal cause. So they are necessary of existence if by 'necessary of existence' one means one of these meanings. And if by 'necessary of existence' one means something more general than that, to include that which has no locus, the essence being a locus for the attributes subsisting in it, then they are not necessary of existence in this interpretation. Rather, they are possible of existence, and the essence entails them, and it is a locus for them. And if it is said: 'Then it follows that the essence is agent and recipient, and that is false.' It is said: Both premises are denied: the impossibility of the essence being an agent for its attribute is denied. For the essence's entailment of its attribute subsisting in it does not necessitate that it be an agent for it. Rather, it is impossible that the essence be an agent for the necessary attribute in the contingent, so how about the Lord of the worlds? Rather, that the Lord be an agent for what is necessary for Him even if it is separate from Him, like the celestial sphere, so how can He be an agent for His attribute that is necessary for Him? And if it is assumed that they call this entailment 'act' and say: 'It is an agent in this consideration.' It is said to them: Then we do not concede that it is not permissible for one thing to be both agent and recipient in this consideration, nor even in another consideration. For if they argue for that with their well-known argument built on the negation of composition, then they cannot establish each of them making that a premise in the very proof, because this is begging the question, and it is making one of the two required premises a premise in proving itself in other words. For if they negate 'composition' from 'the Necessary' by [other premises] based on premises, including that the one cannot be agent and recipient, and they establish this premise based on the negation of 'composition', then they have established each of the two premises by the other, so neither of them is established.)
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the logicians' claim that God's essence and attributes form a composite that requires a cause. Ibn Taymiyya begins by clarifying the meaning of 'other' (ghayr): if attributes are necessary concomitants of the essence, they cannot be considered 'other' in a sense that implies separation or independence. He then argues that even if they are called 'other' in the sense of being conceptually distinct, this does not entail that the composite needs a cause. The most that can be said is that the attribute depends on the essence as a state depends on its locus, which is simply a receptive cause—not an efficient, final, or formal cause. This dependence is not the kind that makes the composite need an external agent. He then attacks the logicians' premise that 'what is in need of another is not necessary by itself' by reinterpreting 'need' as mere concomitance and 'other' as part of the composite. Thus, the supposed need does not imply an efficient cause. In the third station, he explicitly states that the totality of essence and attributes does not require any of the four causes. He then discusses the status of the attributes themselves: if 'necessary of existence' means not having a cause, then the attributes are necessary in that sense; but if it means not needing a locus, then they are possible because they inhere in the essence. He rejects the objection that the essence would then be both agent and recipient, arguing that entailment does not imply agency. Finally, he exposes the circularity in the logicians' argument against composition: they prove that composition is impossible by assuming that one thing cannot be both agent and recipient, and then prove that premise by assuming composition is impossible. This is a classic case of petitio principii. Throughout, Ibn Taymiyya uses examples from theology, focusing on God's attributes.

Ibn Taymiyya's redefinition of 'other' and 'need' shows that the logicians' argument from composition rests on ambiguous terms, not genuine causal necessity.

The Syllogism
advanced
10 min
Denying the Four Causes for the Divine Composite
Ibn Taymiyya denies the four causes for the divine composite and clarifies necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply Ibn Taymiyya's argument that the composite of God's essence and attributes does not require any of the four Aristotelian causes.

Core Idea
The composite of essence and attributes needs only a receptive cause, not efficient, final, or formal.

Ibn Taymiyya explicitly states that the totality of God's essence and attributes does not require any of the four causes—efficient, final, formal, or material (receptive). He reinterprets the dependence as merely receptive, which does not make the composite dependent on an external agent. He also distinguishes two senses of 'necessary of existence' to clarify the status of attributes. This lesson guides you through his systematic denial and its implications for theology.

Guided Path
1
Recall the four Aristotelian causes
The four causes are: efficient (agent), final (purpose), formal (essence), and material (receptive). Ibn Taymiyya denies that any of these apply to the composite of God's essence and attributes.
In Islamic philosophy, these are standard; Ibn Taymiyya refutes their applicability to God.
2
Find Ibn Taymiyya's explicit denial
Locate the statement: 'the totality of essence and attribute is not in need of any of the four types of causes.' He lists them: agent, final, receptive, formal.
This is a direct refutation of the logicians' assumption.
3
Understand the receptive cause only
He concedes that the attribute depends on the essence as a state on its locus—this is a receptive cause. But this is not an external cause; it is an internal relation.
The logicians themselves call this a 'receptive cause', but Ibn Taymiyya restricts it to what is inseparable, not temporally occuring.
4
Examine the two senses of 'necessary of existence'
Ibn Taymiyya distinguishes: (1) not having an efficient cause—attributes are necessary in this sense; (2) not needing a locus—attributes are possible in this sense because they inhere.
This clarifies that attributes are not contingent in the sense of needing an external agent.
5
Refute the objection of essence being both agent and recipient
He denies that entailment (luzum) makes the essence an agent for the attribute. The essence is not an efficient cause; it is simply the substrate.
This responds to the charge that the same thing cannot be both agent and recipient.

وإن فُسّر الغَيْرانِ) بما ليس أحدهما هو الآخر، أو بما [٢١٧] يجوز العلم بأحدهما مع عدم العلم بالآخر ؛ فلا ريب أن صفة الموصوف التي يمكن معرفتها «غير» بالموصوف له بهذا الاعتبار. لكن إذا كانت تلك الصفة لازمة له وهو لازم لها، لم يكن في ذلك ما أن يكون أحدهما مفتقراً إلى الآخر، مفعولاً للآخر، ولا علة فاعلة، ولا غائية، ولا صُورية. أكثر ما في ذلك أن تكون الصفة مفتقرة إلى الذات افتقار الحال إلى محله القابل له . وهم يسمون القابل علة قابلة لكن فيما يحدث لها من المقبولات، لا فيما يكون لازماً لها أزلاً وأبداً. وإن قدر أنهم يسمون جميع ذلك «علة» و «معلولاً»، فتكون الذات علة قابلة للصفة بهذا الاعتبار. وكون الصفة «معلولة) هو معنى كونها صفة قائمة بالموصوف .

(from the necessary concomitants of His essence, and it is inseparable from His essence; it is not permissible for separation to occur in any way. So it is impossible that His attributes be 'other' in this consideration. And if 'the two others' are interpreted as that which is not the one the other, or as that which [٢١٧] it is possible to know one of them without knowledge of the other, then there is no doubt that the attribute of the described which can be known is 'other' than the described in this consideration. But if that attribute is inseparable from Him and He is inseparable from it, then there is in that nothing that one of them is in need of the other, an effect of the other, nor an efficient cause, nor a final cause, nor a formal cause. The most that is in that is that the attribute is in need of the essence with the need of a state for its receptive locus. And they call the receptive locus a receptive cause, but regarding what occurs to it of receptions, not regarding what is inseparable from it eternally and forever. And if it is assumed that they call all of that 'cause' and 'effect', then the essence is a receptive cause for the attribute in this consideration. And the attribute being 'effected' is the meaning of its being an attribute subsisting in the described. So this is that the totality of essence and attribute is not in need of any of the four types of causes, neither the need of the state for the agent nor for the final cause nor for the receptive cause nor for the formal cause. So it is false to say: the totality is in need of its part with the need of the effect for its cause in any respect. Rather, its ultimate extent is that in it there is the need of the attribute for the described, the need of the necessary state for its locus that entails it. So it is said to them: What is there in its being existent by itself, having no agent, that necessitates the negation of this concomitance which they called 'need' regarding His essence and attributes? And their saying: 'What is in need of another is not necessary by itself.' It is said to them: It is known that what is meant by 'need' is concomitance; and what is meant by 'other' is that which is included in the totality, either the essence or the attributes, not what is separate from it and what is permissible to separate from it. Its ultimate extent is that the attribute must have the described. So what is meant is not the need of the effect for its efficient cause. Therefore, there is not in this concomitance which you called 'need' nor in these attributes which you called 'others' [٢١٨] anything that necessitates that any of that be an effect of an agent or of an efficient cause. The third station - The totality of essence and attribute is not in need of the four causes. And the Necessary Existence which the contingent things indicate is the existent by itself, the self-subsistent, the Lord of the worlds, who is not in need of an agent nor an efficient cause. Rather, He is Himself and His attributes are not in need of any of the four causes. [٢٢٨] / The fact that His attributes are necessary of existence. And as for His attributes themselves, they have no agent nor efficient cause nor final cause nor formal cause. So they are necessary of existence if by 'necessary of existence' one means one of these meanings. And if by 'necessary of existence' one means something more general than that, to include that which has no locus, the essence being a locus for the attributes subsisting in it, then they are not necessary of existence in this interpretation. Rather, they are possible of existence, and the essence entails them, and it is a locus for them. And if it is said: 'Then it follows that the essence is agent and recipient, and that is false.' It is said: Both premises are denied: the impossibility of the essence being an agent for its attribute is denied. For the essence's entailment of its attribute subsisting in it does not necessitate that it be an agent for it. Rather, it is impossible that the essence be an agent for the necessary attribute in the contingent, so how about the Lord of the worlds? Rather, that the Lord be an agent for what is necessary for Him even if it is separate from Him, like the celestial sphere, so how can He be an agent for His attribute that is necessary for Him? And if it is assumed that they call this entailment 'act' and say: 'It is an agent in this consideration.' It is said to them: Then we do not concede that it is not permissible for one thing to be both agent and recipient in this consideration, nor even in another consideration. For if they argue for that with their well-known argument built on the negation of composition, then they cannot establish each of them making that a premise in the very proof, because this is begging the question, and it is making one of the two required premises a premise in proving itself in other words. For if they negate 'composition' from 'the Necessary' by [other premises] based on premises, including that the one cannot be agent and recipient, and they establish this premise based on the negation of 'composition', then they have established each of the two premises by the other, so neither of them is established.)
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the logicians' claim that God's essence and attributes form a composite that requires a cause. Ibn Taymiyya begins by clarifying the meaning of 'other' (ghayr): if attributes are necessary concomitants of the essence, they cannot be considered 'other' in a sense that implies separation or independence. He then argues that even if they are called 'other' in the sense of being conceptually distinct, this does not entail that the composite needs a cause. The most that can be said is that the attribute depends on the essence as a state depends on its locus, which is simply a receptive cause—not an efficient, final, or formal cause. This dependence is not the kind that makes the composite need an external agent. He then attacks the logicians' premise that 'what is in need of another is not necessary by itself' by reinterpreting 'need' as mere concomitance and 'other' as part of the composite. Thus, the supposed need does not imply an efficient cause. In the third station, he explicitly states that the totality of essence and attributes does not require any of the four causes. He then discusses the status of the attributes themselves: if 'necessary of existence' means not having a cause, then the attributes are necessary in that sense; but if it means not needing a locus, then they are possible because they inhere in the essence. He rejects the objection that the essence would then be both agent and recipient, arguing that entailment does not imply agency. Finally, he exposes the circularity in the logicians' argument against composition: they prove that composition is impossible by assuming that one thing cannot be both agent and recipient, and then prove that premise by assuming composition is impossible. This is a classic case of petitio principii. Throughout, Ibn Taymiyya uses examples from theology, focusing on God's attributes.

Key Takeaway

The Syllogism
advanced
10 min
Exposing Circular Reasoning in the Argument Against Composition
Ibn Taymiyya exposes circularity in the logicians' proof against composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify begging the question (musadara ala al-matlub) in theological arguments and reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's exposure of circularity in the logicians' proof against composition in God.

Core Idea
The logicians prove composition is impossible by assuming one thing cannot be both agent and recipient, and prove that by assuming composition is impossible—a vicious circle.

Ibn Taymiyya concludes his critique by exposing a logical fallacy in the logicians' argument against composition (tarkib) in the Necessary Being. He shows that they rely on two premises that each depend on the other, resulting in a classic case of begging the question (musadara ala al-matlub). This lesson teaches you to detect such circularity in theological reasoning, a vital skill for critical engagement with philosophical theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' argument against composition
The logicians argue that the Necessary Being cannot be composed (i.e., cannot have distinct essence and attributes as parts). They base this on the principle that one thing cannot be both agent and recipient.
This is a standard argument in Islamic philosophy (e.g., Avicenna).
2
Understand the charge of circularity
Ibn Taymiyya points out that the premise 'one thing cannot be both agent and recipient' is itself proven by assuming that composition is impossible. Thus, each premise is used to prove the other, making the argument circular.
This is begging the question because the conclusion is already assumed in the premises.
3
Locate the two interdependent premises
Premise 1: The Necessary Being is not composed (based on agent-recipient impossibility). Premise 2: One thing cannot be both agent and recipient (based on composition impossibility).
Check the text: 'they negate composition from the Necessary by [other premises] based on premises, including that the one cannot be agent and recipient, and they establish this premise based on the negation of composition.'
4
See how neither premise is independently established
Because each premise relies on the other, neither is proven. Therefore, the entire argument collapses.
Ibn Taymiyya calls this 'making one of the two required premises a premise in proving itself in other words.'
5
Recognize the fallacy in other theological contexts
Begging the question often appears when proving God's attributes. Practise detecting it: if an argument uses a premise that is essentially the same as the conclusion, it is circular.
Example: 'The Quran is created because it is composite; it is composite because it is created.'

وإن فُسّر الغَيْرانِ) بما ليس أحدهما هو الآخر، أو بما [٢١٧] يجوز العلم بأحدهما مع عدم العلم بالآخر ؛ فلا ريب أن صفة الموصوف التي يمكن معرفتها «غير» بالموصوف له بهذا الاعتبار. لكن إذا كانت تلك الصفة لازمة له وهو لازم لها، لم يكن في ذلك ما أن يكون أحدهما مفتقراً إلى الآخر، مفعولاً للآخر، ولا علة فاعلة، ولا غائية، ولا صُورية. أكثر ما في ذلك أن تكون الصفة مفتقرة إلى الذات افتقار الحال إلى محله القابل له . وهم يسمون القابل علة قابلة لكن فيما يحدث لها من المقبولات، لا فيما يكون لازماً لها أزلاً وأبداً. وإن قدر أنهم يسمون جميع ذلك «علة» و «معلولاً»، فتكون الذات علة قابلة للصفة بهذا الاعتبار. وكون الصفة «معلولة) هو معنى كونها صفة قائمة بالموصوف .
فهذا فليس مجموع الذات والصفة مفتقراً إلى شيء من أنواع العلل الأربع، لا افتقار الحال إلى الفاعل ولا إلى الغاية ولا إلى القابل، ولا إلى الصورة فبطل أن يقال : المجموع مفتقر إلى جزئه افتقار المعلول إلى علته بوجه من الوجوه، لكن غايته أن فيه افتقار الصفة إلى الموصوف افتقار الحال اللازم لمحله إلى محله المستلزم له. فيقال لهم : وأي شيء في كونه موجوداً بنفسه لا فاعِل له ما يوجب نفي هذا التلازم الذي سموه «افتقاراً» نحو ذاته وصفاته؟
وقولهم : : «ما افتقر إلى غيره لم يكن واجباً بنفسه، يقال لهم : قد علم أن المراد بـ «الافتقار ) : التلازم ؛ والمراد بـ «الغير»: ما هو داخل في المجموع، إما الذات وإما الصفات، ليس المراد به ما هو مباين له، وما يجوز مُفارقته ،له وغايته أن يراد أن الصفة لا بد لها من الموصوف . فليس المراد افتقارُ المعلول إلى علته الفاعلة. وحينئذ فليس في هذا التلازم الذي سميتموه «افتقاراً» ولا في هذه الصفات التي سميتموها «أغياراً» [٢١٨] ما يوجب أن يكون شيء من ذلك مفعولاً لفاعل ولا لعلة فاعلة
المقام الثالث - مجموع الذات والصفة لا يفتقر إلى العلل الأربع
وواجب الوجود الذي دلت الممكنات عليه هو الموجود بنفسه، القائم بنفسه، ربُّ العالمين، الذي لا يفتقر إلى فاعل ولا علة فاعلة، بل هو نفسه نفسه وصفاته لا يفتقر إلى شيء من العلل الأربع .

كون صفاته تعالى واجبة الوجود»
وأما نفس صفاته فليس لها ،فاعل ولا علة فاعلة ولا علة غائية، ولا صُورية .
فهي واجبة الوجود إذا عُني بـ «واجب الوجود أحد هذه المعاني .
وإن عنى بـ واجب الوجود» ما هو أعم من ذلك حتى يدخل فيه ما ليس له مَحَلٌّ كون الذات محلا للصفات يقوم به فليست واجبة الوجود بهذا التفسير، بل هي ممكنة الوجود، والذات مستلزمة لها، وهي محلّ لها . وإذا قيل : «فيلزم أن تكون الذات فاعلة وقابلة وذاك «باطل»، قيل : كلا المقدمتين امتناع كون الذات فاعلة لصفتها ممنوعة . فإن كون الذات مستلزمة لصفتها القائمة بها لا يقتضي أن تكون فاعلة لها؛ بل يمتنع أن تكون الذاتُ فاعلة للصفة اللازمة في الممكن ، فكيف في رب العالمين؟ بل أن يكون الرب فاعلاً لما هو لازم له وإن كان بائناً عنه، كالفلك، فكيف يكون فاعلاً لصفته اللازمة له؟ وإن قدر أنهم سموا هذا الاستلزام «فعلاً»، وقالوا : فاعلة هي بهذا الاعتبار قيل لهم فلا نسلم أنه لا يجوز كون الشيء الواحد فاعلاً وقابلاً بهذا الاعتبار، بل ولا باعتبار آخر .
فإن استدلوا على ذلك بحجتهم المعروفة المبنية على نفي التركيب، فلا يمكنهم إثبات كل جعل ذلك مقدمة في نفس الدليل، لأن هذا مُصَادَرَةٌ على المطلوب، وهو جعل من المقدمتين المطلوب مقدمة في إثبات نفسه بعبارة أخرى . فإنهم إذا نفوا التركيب» عن «الواجب» بالأخرى بناءً على مقدمات، منها : أن الواحد لا يكون فاعلاً وقابلاً ، وأثبتوا هذه المقدمة بناءً على نفي «التركيب»، كانوا قد أثبتوا كلاً من المقدمتين بالأخرى، فلا تكون واحدة منهما ثابتة .
(from the necessary concomitants of His essence, and it is inseparable from His essence; it is not permissible for separation to occur in any way. So it is impossible that His attributes be 'other' in this consideration. And if 'the two others' are interpreted as that which is not the one the other, or as that which [٢١٧] it is possible to know one of them without knowledge of the other, then there is no doubt that the attribute of the described which can be known is 'other' than the described in this consideration. But if that attribute is inseparable from Him and He is inseparable from it, then there is in that nothing that one of them is in need of the other, an effect of the other, nor an efficient cause, nor a final cause, nor a formal cause. The most that is in that is that the attribute is in need of the essence with the need of a state for its receptive locus. And they call the receptive locus a receptive cause, but regarding what occurs to it of receptions, not regarding what is inseparable from it eternally and forever. And if it is assumed that they call all of that 'cause' and 'effect', then the essence is a receptive cause for the attribute in this consideration. And the attribute being 'effected' is the meaning of its being an attribute subsisting in the described. So this is that the totality of essence and attribute is not in need of any of the four types of causes, neither the need of the state for the agent nor for the final cause nor for the receptive cause nor for the formal cause. So it is false to say: the totality is in need of its part with the need of the effect for its cause in any respect. Rather, its ultimate extent is that in it there is the need of the attribute for the described, the need of the necessary state for its locus that entails it. So it is said to them: What is there in its being existent by itself, having no agent, that necessitates the negation of this concomitance which they called 'need' regarding His essence and attributes? And their saying: 'What is in need of another is not necessary by itself.' It is said to them: It is known that what is meant by 'need' is concomitance; and what is meant by 'other' is that which is included in the totality, either the essence or the attributes, not what is separate from it and what is permissible to separate from it. Its ultimate extent is that the attribute must have the described. So what is meant is not the need of the effect for its efficient cause. Therefore, there is not in this concomitance which you called 'need' nor in these attributes which you called 'others' [٢١٨] anything that necessitates that any of that be an effect of an agent or of an efficient cause. The third station - The totality of essence and attribute is not in need of the four causes. And the Necessary Existence which the contingent things indicate is the existent by itself, the self-subsistent, the Lord of the worlds, who is not in need of an agent nor an efficient cause. Rather, He is Himself and His attributes are not in need of any of the four causes. [٢٢٨] / The fact that His attributes are necessary of existence. And as for His attributes themselves, they have no agent nor efficient cause nor final cause nor formal cause. So they are necessary of existence if by 'necessary of existence' one means one of these meanings. And if by 'necessary of existence' one means something more general than that, to include that which has no locus, the essence being a locus for the attributes subsisting in it, then they are not necessary of existence in this interpretation. Rather, they are possible of existence, and the essence entails them, and it is a locus for them. And if it is said: 'Then it follows that the essence is agent and recipient, and that is false.' It is said: Both premises are denied: the impossibility of the essence being an agent for its attribute is denied. For the essence's entailment of its attribute subsisting in it does not necessitate that it be an agent for it. Rather, it is impossible that the essence be an agent for the necessary attribute in the contingent, so how about the Lord of the worlds? Rather, that the Lord be an agent for what is necessary for Him even if it is separate from Him, like the celestial sphere, so how can He be an agent for His attribute that is necessary for Him? And if it is assumed that they call this entailment 'act' and say: 'It is an agent in this consideration.' It is said to them: Then we do not concede that it is not permissible for one thing to be both agent and recipient in this consideration, nor even in another consideration. For if they argue for that with their well-known argument built on the negation of composition, then they cannot establish each of them making that a premise in the very proof, because this is begging the question, and it is making one of the two required premises a premise in proving itself in other words. For if they negate 'composition' from 'the Necessary' by [other premises] based on premises, including that the one cannot be agent and recipient, and they establish this premise based on the negation of 'composition', then they have established each of the two premises by the other, so neither of them is established.)
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the logicians' claim that God's essence and attributes form a composite that requires a cause. Ibn Taymiyya begins by clarifying the meaning of 'other' (ghayr): if attributes are necessary concomitants of the essence, they cannot be considered 'other' in a sense that implies separation or independence. He then argues that even if they are called 'other' in the sense of being conceptually distinct, this does not entail that the composite needs a cause. The most that can be said is that the attribute depends on the essence as a state depends on its locus, which is simply a receptive cause—not an efficient, final, or formal cause. This dependence is not the kind that makes the composite need an external agent. He then attacks the logicians' premise that 'what is in need of another is not necessary by itself' by reinterpreting 'need' as mere concomitance and 'other' as part of the composite. Thus, the supposed need does not imply an efficient cause. In the third station, he explicitly states that the totality of essence and attributes does not require any of the four causes. He then discusses the status of the attributes themselves: if 'necessary of existence' means not having a cause, then the attributes are necessary in that sense; but if it means not needing a locus, then they are possible because they inhere in the essence. He rejects the objection that the essence would then be both agent and recipient, arguing that entailment does not imply agency. Finally, he exposes the circularity in the logicians' argument against composition: they prove that composition is impossible by assuming that one thing cannot be both agent and recipient, and then prove that premise by assuming composition is impossible. This is a classic case of petitio principii. Throughout, Ibn Taymiyya uses examples from theology, focusing on God's attributes.

Key Terms
مصادرة على المطلوب
التركيب
الواحد لا يكون فاعلاً وقابلاً

The Syllogism
advanced
10 min
Refutation of the Superiority of Categorical Syllogism over Analogical Reasoning
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the logicians' claim that categorical syllogism is superior to analogical reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the logicians' hierarchy and identify his argument that analogical reasoning is more fundamental than categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
Analogical reasoning is the foundation, not the inferior cousin, of categorical syllogism.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges a core assumption of Aristotelian logicians: that categorical syllogism yields certainty while analogical reasoning only yields probability. He argues that in reality, all rational people rely more on analogical reasoning, and that categorical syllogism itself depends on analogical reasoning for its universal premises. In this lesson, we'll unpack his critique and see why he considers analogical reasoning more worthy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
Find where Ibn Taymiyya states the logicians' position: that they magnify categorical syllogism (qiyas al-shumul) and belittle analogical reasoning (qiyas al-tamthil), claiming it only yields probability (zann).
The passage begins with 'رد القول بأن قياس التمثيل لا يفيد إلا الظن'.
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's counter-assertion
He says 'ليس الأمر كذلك' and asserts that both are of one kind, and sound analogical reasoning is more worthy of yielding the desired object, whether knowledge or probability.
He also notes that all rational people use analogical reasoning more.
3
Analyze the dependence claim
He claims that categorical syllogism is not valid in a universal matter except through the mediation of analogical reasoning ('لا يصح قياس الشمول في الأمر العام إلا بتوسط قياس التمثيل').
This is the core of his argument.
4
Examine the example and explanation
He gives examples of universal propositions like 'the whole is greater than the part' and explains that these are known through particular instances, from which the mind abstracts a universal description.
The process of abstraction and extending judgment is the reality of analogical reasoning.
5
Evaluate the refutation's conclusion
He concludes that what they negated (tamthil) is actually more perfect than what they affirmed (shumul), because knowledge of the universal together with its particular instances is more complete.
Thus the logicians' hierarchy is inverted.

Refutation of the statement that analogical reasoning only yields probability. Analogical reasoning: the purpose here is to discuss logic and what they mentioned of demonstration, and that they magnify categorical syllogism and belittle analogical reasoning, claiming that it only yields probability, and that the desired knowledge from categorical syllogism is not obtained except by it. It is not so; rather they are in reality of one kind. Sound analogical reasoning is more worthy of yielding the desired object—whether knowledge or probability—than mere categorical syllogism. For this reason, all rational people use analogical reasoning more than they use categorical syllogism. Indeed, categorical syllogism is not valid in a universal matter except through the mediation of analogical reasoning. Every proof that is used for the validity of categorical syllogism in some forms is also used for the validity of analogical reasoning in that form. We exemplified this with their statement: 'The One does not proceed from it except one,' which is among their most famous corrupt divine statements. As for correct statements, this is also apparent in them. For categorical syllogism necessarily requires analogical reasoning. The foundation of syllogism is a universal affirmative proposition; two negatives or two particulars do not produce a conclusion by their agreement. The universal is only universal in the mind. So if the realization of some of its individuals in the external world is known, that helps in knowing that it is a universal affirmative. For when a person perceives some external individuals, he extracts from them a universal description, especially if its individuals are many. So the knowledge of the existence of the shared description in an origin in the external world is the foundation of the knowledge of the universal proposition. Then analogical reasoning is the foundation for categorical syllogism, either being a cause for its attainment, or it can be said that it does not exist without it. So how can it alone be stronger than it? These people represent universals by such statements as 'The whole is greater than the part,' 'Contradictories neither coexist nor vanish,' 'Things equal to one thing are equal,' and the like. There is no individual of these universals except that many things have been known from its external individuals. When one wants to realize this universal in the soul, an example is made of an individual of its individuals, and the absence of a difference between it and others, or the existence of the common attribute, is shown. Then the mind judges the existence of the judgment for that shared universal. This is the reality of analogical reasoning. Even if we suppose that categorical syllogism does not need analogical reasoning and that knowledge of universal propositions does not need knowledge of any specific [instance] at all, it cannot be said that when the universal is known along with knowledge of the existence of some of its individuals in the external world, it is more deficient than knowing it without knowledge of that specific [instance]; for knowledge of the specific only adds perfection to it. Thus it is shown that what they negated of the form of reasoning is more perfect than what they affirmed. Their belittling of analogical reasoning is from the speech of their later scholars. Know that they in divine logic—even natural—changed some of what Aristotle said, but what they added in divine matters is better than Aristotle's speech. For I have seen both statements. And Aristotle and his followers in...
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the logicians' claim that analogical reasoning (qiyas al-tamthil) only yields probability (zann) while categorical syllogism (qiyas al-shumul) yields certainty. Ibn Taymiyya argues that both are of the same kind, and in fact analogical reasoning is more fundamental. He points out that all rational people use analogical reasoning more often. More importantly, categorical syllogism depends on analogical reasoning because the universal premise (e.g., 'all A are B') is derived from knowledge of particular instances. The mind abstracts a universal description from observing individuals, and then uses analogical reasoning to extend the judgment to other similar cases. Without this, the universal premise cannot be known. He gives examples from their own statements, such as 'the whole is greater than the part,' which are known through particulars. Even if we suppose categorical syllogism could exist without analogical reasoning, knowing the universal together with its particular instances is more perfect, not less. Therefore, the form of reasoning they dismissed (tamthil) is actually more perfect than the one they affirmed (shumul). He concludes by noting that the later logicians have altered Aristotle's teachings, but that is not the main point.

قياس التمثيل
قياس الشمول
القضية الكلية

The Syllogism
