Analogical Reasoning as the Foundation of Categorical Syllogism
Ibn Taymiyya argues analogical reasoning is the basis of categorical syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that analogical reasoning is the necessary foundation for categorical syllogism and evaluate its refutation of the logicians' position.

Core Idea
Analogical reasoning is not inferior—it is the necessary foundation of categorical syllogism.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya directly challenges a core claim of Aristotelian logic: that categorical syllogisms yield certainty while analogical reasoning (qiyas al-tamthil) yields only probability. He argues the opposite: categorical syllogisms depend on analogical reasoning for their universal premises. Understanding this argument is essential for grasping Ibn Taymiyya's broader critique of Greek logic and his defense of inductive and analogical methods used in Islamic jurisprudence. This lesson will guide you through his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim being refuted
Find the claim that analogical reasoning only yields probability (لا يفيد إلا الظن) and that categorical syllogism (القياس الشمولي) is stronger. Ibn Taymiyya states this in the first line to set up his refutation.
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's premise about universals
He states that the foundation of syllogism is a universal affirmative proposition (قضية كلية موجبة), but universals are only in the mind (الكلي لا يكون كلياً إلا في الذهن). They are known by extracting a common description from external individuals.
This premise connects epistemology to logic: universal knowledge arises from particular experiences.
3
Trace the argument that analogical reasoning is prior
Ibn Taymiyya argues that to know a universal, one must perceive particulars and extract a common description—this is exactly the process of analogical reasoning (قياس تمثيلي). He concludes that analogical reasoning is either a cause of categorical syllogism or a necessary condition for it (أصل لـ«القياس الشمولي»).
He uses examples like 'the whole is greater than the part' to show universals are known through instances.
4
Assess the conclusion that the 'denied form' is more complete
Even if categorical syllogism could exist without analogical reasoning (a hypothetical he challenges), knowing the universal along with its particular instances increases perfection, not diminishes it. Thus, the form of reasoning that includes analogical reasoning (which they deny) is more complete than the purely abstract categorical syllogism.
This is a rhetorical and logical coup: what they reject is actually superior.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثالث - رد القول بأن قياس التمثيل لا يفيد إلا الظن
فإن القياس الشمولي لا بد فيه من القياس التمثيلي. أصل القياس قضية كلية موجبة، ولا نتاج عن سالبتين، ولا عن جزئيتين باتفاقهم. والكلي لا يكون كلياً إلا في الذهن. فإذا عُرف تحقق بعض أفراده في الخارج كان ذلك مما يعين على العلم بكونه كلياً موجباً. فإنه إذا أحسن الإنسان ببعض الأفراد الخارجية انتزع منه وصفاً كلياً، لا سيما إذا كثرت أفراده. فالعلم بثبوت الوصف المشترك لأصل في الخارج هو أصل العلم بالقضية الكلية. وحينئذ فـ«القياس التمثيلي» أصل لـ«القياس الشمولي»، إما أن يكون سبباً في حصوله، وإما أن يقال: لا يوجد بدونه. فكيف يكون وحده أقوى منه؟
وهؤلاء يمثلون الكليات بمثل قول القائل: «الكل أعظم من الجزء»، و«النقيضان لا يجتمعان ولا يرتفعان»، و«الأشياء المساوية لشيء واحد متساوية»، ونحو ذلك. وما من فرد من أفراد هذه الكليات إلا وقد عُلم من أفراده الخارجة أمور كثيرة. وإذا أريد تحقق هذه الكلية في النفس ضُرِب لها المَثَل بفرد من أفرادها، وبين انتفاء الفارق بينه وبين غيره، أو ثبوت الجامع. وحينئذ يحكم العقل بثبوت الحكم لذلك المشترك الكلي. وهذا حقيقة قياس التمثيل.
ولو قدرنا أن قياس الشمول لا يفتقر إلى التمثيل، وأن العلم بالقضايا الكلية لا يفتقر إلى العلم بمعين أصلاً، فلا يمكن أن يقال: إذا عُلم الكلي مع العلم بثبوت بعض أفراده في الخارج كان أنقص من أن يعلمه بدون العلم بذاك المعين؛ فإن العلم بالمعين ما زاده إلا كمالاً. فتبين أن ما نفوه من صورة القياس أكمل مما أثبتوه.
The third station - Refutation of the claim that analogical reasoning yields only probability. For the categorical syllogism necessarily requires analogical reasoning. The foundation of syllogism is a universal affirmative proposition, and nothing is produced from two negatives, nor from two particulars, by their consensus. And the universal is only universal in the mind. So if the actualization of some of its individuals externally is known, that helps in knowing that it is a universal affirmative. For if a person perceives some external individuals, he extracts from them a universal description, especially if its individuals are many. So knowledge of the subsistence of the common description for an origin externally is the foundation of knowledge of the universal proposition. Thus, analogical reasoning is the foundation for categorical syllogism, either being a cause for its attainment, or it can be said: it does not exist without it. So how can it alone be stronger than it? And they represent universals by such statements as 'the whole is greater than the part', 'the two contradictories neither coexist nor can both be absent', and 'things equal to the same thing are equal', and so on. And there is no individual of these universals except that many things are known from its external individuals. And when it is desired to realize this universal in the soul, an example is given of an individual of its individuals, and the absence of a difference between it and others is shown, or the presence of a commonality. Then the intellect judges the subsistence of the ruling for that common universal. And this is the reality of analogical reasoning. And if we suppose that categorical syllogism does not need analogical reasoning, and that knowledge of universal propositions does not need knowledge of any specific particular at all, it cannot be said: when the universal is known along with knowledge of the subsistence of some of its individuals externally, it is inferior to knowing it without knowledge of that specific particular; for knowledge of the particular only increases it in perfection. So it has become clear that what they denied of the form of syllogism is more complete than what they affirmed.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the logicians' claim that analogical reasoning (qiyas al-tamthil) only yields probability, while categorical syllogism (qiyas al-shumul) yields certainty. Ibn Taymiyya argues the opposite: analogical reasoning is actually foundational to categorical syllogism. He begins by stating that categorical syllogism necessarily depends on analogical reasoning. The foundation of any syllogism is a universal affirmative proposition, but universals only exist in the mind. To know that a universal is truly affirmative, one must have knowledge of some of its individuals in the external world. This is achieved through analogical abstraction: from particular instances, the mind extracts a common description. Thus, knowledge of the common description's subsistence in an external origin is the basis of the universal proposition. Therefore, analogical reasoning is either the cause of categorical syllogism or a necessary condition for it. If it is necessary, how can it be weaker? He then illustrates how universals like 'the whole is greater than the part' are known through examples—i.e., through analogical reasoning. Finally, he addresses a hypothetical objection: even if categorical syllogism could exist without analogical reasoning, knowing the universal along with particular instances is more complete, not less. So, the form of reasoning that includes analogical reasoning (which they deny) is actually superior to their purely abstract categorical form.

Key Terms
قياس تمثيلي
قياس شمولي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Categorical syllogism is independent and more certain than analogical reasoning.
B) Analogical reasoning is the foundation of categorical syllogism and necessary for it.
C) Both are independent methods with no relation.
D) Analogical reasoning is derived from categorical syllogism.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya reverses the epistemic hierarchy: analogical reasoning is not weaker but is the necessary foundation for categorical syllogism, and any logical system that excludes it is incomplete.

Why analogical reasoning is not inherently weak: Ibn Taymiyya's critique
Ibn Taymiyya argues critics confuse material weakness with formal weakness in analogical arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a criticism of analogical reasoning targets its form or its material content.

Core Idea
The weakness of analogical arguments comes from their premises, not their logical form.

Logicians often dismiss analogical reasoning as merely probable, while claiming categorical syllogisms yield certainty. Ibn Taymiyya, drawing on his deep knowledge of both Aristotelian and Islamic logic, argues that this dismissal confuses the container with the content. The true source of probability or certainty lies in the material (al-madda) — the premises — not in the formal structure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of the critique
Ibn Taymiyya attacks later logicians who weaken analogical reasoning (qiyas al-tamthil). He notes they altered Aristotle's logic and their critique stems from observing jurists using analogy.
The target is the claim that analogical form is inherently weaker than categorical form.
2
Distinguish material vs. form in logical arguments
Ibn Taymiyya emphasizes the distinction: madda (material/content/premises) vs. sura (form/structure). He insists probability (zann) arises from the material, not the form.
Classical logicians often treat form as key to validity; Ibn Taymiyya shifts focus to epistemic value of premises.
3
Examine the jurists' use of analogy
Jurists frequently use analogy (qiyas) in legal reasoning with probable premises. The probability is due to the material, not the analogical structure.
If the same material were cast as a categorical syllogism, it would still be probable.
4
Apply the principle to a theological example
The example of proving the world's origination via composition: weak premises (composition implies contingency) cause weakness, not the analogical form. The same material in categorical form would be equally weak.
Ibn Taymiyya says critics weakened the argument due to its material, not because the form is probable.
5
Observe a reductio ad absurdum by al-Amidi
Al-Amidi's example 'the world exists, so it is eternal like the Creator' is clearly invalid because of a flawed premise (everything existent is eternal), not because of syllogistic form.
This shows that even categorical syllogisms can be absurd if the material is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما ذكروه من تضعيف قياس التمثيل هو من كلام متأخريهم. واعلم أنهم في المنطق الإلهي بل والطبيعي غيروا بعض ما ذكره أرسطو، لكن ما زادوه في الإلهي خير من كلام أرسطو، فإني قد رأيت الكلامين. وأرسطو وأتباعه في الإلهيات أجهل من اليهود والنصارى بكثير كثير. وأما في الطبيعيات فغالب كلامه جيد. وأما المنطق فكلامه فيه خير من كلامه في الإلهي.
وما ذكروه من تضعيف قياس التمثيل والاحتجاج عليه بما ذكروه، هو من كلام متأخريهم لما رأوا استعمال الفقهاء له غالباً. والفقهاء يستعملونه كثيراً في المواد الظنية. وهناك الظن حصل من المادة لا من صورة القياس، فلو صوروا تلك المادة بـ«قياس الشمول» لم يفد أيضاً إلا الظن. لكن هؤلاء ظنوا أن الضعف من جهة الصورة، فجعلوا صورة قياسهم يقينياً، وصورة قياس الفقهاء ظنياً.
ومثلوه بأمثلة كلامية ليقرروا أن قياس التمثيل ظني، كما مثلوه من الاحتجاج عليهم بأن الفلك مؤلف، فكان محدثاً قياساً على الإنسان وغيره من المولدات، ثم أخذوا يضعفون هذا القياس؛ لكن إنما ضعفوه بضعف مادته. فإن هذا الدليل الذي ذكره الجهمية والقدرية ومن وافقهم من الأشعرية وغيرهم على حدوث الأجسام أدلة ضعيفة لأجل مادتها، لا لكون صورتها ظنية. ولهذا لا فرق بين أن يصوروها بصورة التمثيل أو الشمول.
والآمدي ونحوه ممن يصنف في الفلسفة ويكره التمثيل بمثل هذا لما فيه من التشنيع على المتكلمين فيمثل بمثال متفق عليه كقوله: «العالم موجود، فكان قديماً كالباري» فإن أحداً من العقلاء لا يحتج على قدم العالم بكونه موجوداً، وإلا لزم قدم كل موجود. وهذا لا يقوله عاقل.
What they mentioned of weakening analogical reasoning is from the speech of their later ones. And know that they, in divine logic and even natural logic, altered some of what Aristotle mentioned, but what they added in divine logic is better than Aristotle's speech, for I have seen both speeches. And Aristotle and his followers in divine matters are much more ignorant than the Jews and Christians. And as for natural matters, most of his speech is good. And as for logic, his speech in it is better than his speech in divine matters.
And what they mentioned of weakening analogical reasoning and arguing against it with what they mentioned is from the speech of their later ones, when they saw the jurists using it often. And the jurists use it much in probable matters. And there, probability arises from the material, not from the form of the syllogism. For if they had formulated that material with categorical syllogism, it would also yield only probability. But these people thought that the weakness was due to the form, so they made the form of their syllogism certain and the form of the jurists' syllogism probable.
And they exemplified it with theological examples to establish that analogical reasoning is probable, such as their argument against them that the celestial sphere is composed, so it is originated, by analogy with humans and other generated things. Then they began to weaken this syllogism; but they only weakened it due to the weakness of its material. For this proof that the Jahmiyya, Qadariyya, and those who agreed with them among the Ash'aris and others mentioned for the origination of bodies are weak proofs due to their material, not because their form is probable. For this reason, there is no difference between formulating them in the form of analogy or categorical syllogism.
And al-Amidi and others like him who write on philosophy and dislike analogy with such examples due to the disrepute it brings to the theologians, so he gives an agreed-upon example, such as his saying: 'The world exists, so it is eternal like the Creator.' For no rational person argues for the eternity of the world because it exists, otherwise every existent would be eternal. And no rational person says this.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of the logicians' argument that analogical reasoning is inherently weak. Ibn Taymiyya first comments on the historical development: later logicians altered Aristotle's logic, and while Aristotle's divine logic is poor, his natural logic is good. The main point: logicians criticize analogical reasoning because they see jurists using it in probable matters. But Ibn Taymiyya clarifies that the probability comes from the material (the premises), not the form. If the same material were put into a categorical syllogism, it would also be probable. Thus, the weakness is not in the form of analogy but in the content. He gives an example: the theologians' argument for the origination of the universe based on composition is weak because the premise 'whatever is composed is originated' is not certain, not because it is analogical. Finally, he mentions al-Amidi's example of 'the world exists, so it is eternal' to show that even categorical syllogisms can be obviously fallacious if the material is flawed.

صورة

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya refines the critique of analogical reasoning: the certainty or probability of an argument depends on its material premises, not its formal structure.

Ibn Taymiyya's Dilemma on Exhaustive Division
Author refutes the logician's claim that exhaustive division cannot yield certainty in rational matters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's dilemma against the logicians and explain how it reveals their inconsistency.

Core Idea
Exhaustive division yields certainty – logicians use it, so they cannot deny it.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the logicians' claim that exhaustive division (al-taqsīm al-ḥāṣir) in rational matters cannot produce certainty. He presents a powerful dilemma: if division yields certainty, then their own disjunctive syllogism is undermined; if it does not, then their entire argument collapses. He then shows that logicians themselves accept the exhaustive division of existence as certain, exposing a fatal inconsistency. This lesson breaks down the logical structure of his critique.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
Find where the logicians deny that al-sabr wa al-taqsīm yields certainty. In the Arabic, it is at the end: 'إن السبر والتقسيم لا يفيد اليقين'.
2
Extract the dilemma's disjunction
Ibn Taymiyya sets up two possibilities: 'إما أن يكون التقسيم في العقليات قد يفيد اليقين، وإما أن لا يفيده بحال'. This is an exclusive disjunction covering all possibilities.
The disjunction is exhaustive, leading to a constructive dilemma.
3
Trace the first horn
If division yields certainty (first horn), then the logicians' use of the conditional disjunctive syllogism (al-sharṭī al-munfaṣil) as a demonstrative form is invalid, because that syllogism relies on exhaustive division which they are now denying.
This undermines their own logic.
4
Trace the second horn
If division does not yield certainty (second horn), then the logicians' own argument (the one being responded to) becomes invalid. So both horns lead to a problem for the logicians.
The second horn makes their current objection self-defeating.
5
Find the concrete counterexample
Ibn Taymiyya points out that the logicians themselves accept the exhaustive division of existence (al-wujūd) into necessary/possible, eternal/originated, etc., as certain. This is a clear case where division yields certainty, refuting their claim.
This is the 'highest science' (al-ʿilm al-aʿlā) in their tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وإن ثبت لخارج، فمن الجائز أن يكون لغير ما أبدى ، وإن لم يُطَّلع عليه مع البحث عنه، إلخ». فيقال : إما أن يكون التقسيم» في «العقليات» قد يفيد اليقين، وإما أن لا يفيده بحال. فإن كان الأول بطل جَعْلُهم «الشرطي المنفصل» من صور القياس البرهاني. وإن كان الثاني بطل كلامهم هذا. ومعلوم أن هذا أحقُ بالبطلان من ذاك، فإن القائل إذا قال: «الوجود» إما أن يكون واجباً، وإما أن يكون ممكناً؛ وإما أن يكون قديماً، وإما أن يكون حادثاً ؛ وإما أن يكون قائماً بنفسه، وإما أن يكون قائماً بغيره ؛ وإما أن يكون مخلوقاً، وإما أن يكون خالقاً؛ ونحو ذلك من التقسيم الحاصر لجنس الوجود»، كان هذا حصرا لكلي عقلي بل «الوجود (۲)» أعم / ۲۳۸ الكليات. وإذا أمكن العقل أن يحصر أقسامه فحصر أقسام بعض أنواعه أَوْلى. وهم يسلمون هذا كله وهذا هو العلم الأعلى عندهم . فكيف يقولون : إن «السبر والتقسيم لا يفيد اليقين؟
His statement: 'And if it is established for an external [attribute], it is possible that it is for something other than what was presented, even if it is not known upon investigation, etc.' So it is said: Either the division in rational matters yields certainty, or it does not at all. If the first, then their making the 'conditional disjunctive' a form of demonstrative syllogism is invalid. And if the second, then their statement here is invalid. And it is known that this is more deserving of invalidity than that, for if someone says: 'Existence is either necessary or possible; either eternal or originated; either self-subsisting or subsisting through another; either created or creator; and so on from the exhaustive division of the genus of existence' — this is a comprehensive division of a universal rational, nay existence is the most general of universals. And if the mind is able to encompass its divisions, then encompassing the divisions of some of its types is even more appropriate. And they concede all this, and this is the highest science with them. So how can they say that 'exploration and division (sabr wa taqsīm) does not yield certainty'?
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This section is a critical response to the logicians' claim that exhaustive division (taqsīm) in rational matters cannot produce certainty. The author presents a dilemma: either such division can yield certainty or it cannot. If it can, then the logicians' own use of the conditional disjunctive syllogism (sharṭī munfaṣil) as a demonstrative form would be invalid because that syllogism relies on exhaustive division. If it cannot, then their argument against the use of division fails. The author asserts the first horn is more absurd because we commonly use exhaustive division of existence (al-wujūd) into necessary/possible, eternal/originated, etc., which they accept as certain. This is the highest science (al-ʿilm al-aʿlā) in their own tradition. Thus, they contradict themselves by denying certainty to sabr wa taqsīm.

Key Terms
الشرطي المنفصل
التقسيم الحاصر
السبر والتقسيم
العقليات

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) He claims that the conditional disjunctive syllogism is invalid

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's dilemma exposes a logical inconsistency: the logicians cannot claim exhaustive division is uncertain while relying on it in their own demonstrative system.

Will Implies Knowledge: A Fortiori Argument and Response to Qiyas Objection
Ibn Taymiyya presents an argument that will implies knowledge and responds to an objection about qiyas in probable matters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's a fortiori argument for divine knowledge from will and evaluate his response to the objection about exhaustive divisions in qiyas.

Core Idea
Will implies knowledge; if humans know their actions, God knows more.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya uses a fortiori (awla) reasoning to argue that God's will necessarily entails His knowledge. He also addresses a logicians' objection about qiyas: that an exhaustive division of possible causes might be invalid if the cause is the totality or a part not realized in the branch. His response highlights that such cases occur in probable matters (zanniyyat) of fiqh, illustrating the validity of qiyas even in uncertain domains.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Find the central thesis: that will implies knowledge, and God is more worthy of knowledge than humans.
The Arabic uses 'أولى أن يعلم' indicating a fortiori reasoning.
2
Extract the a fortiori argument
Notice the structure: human agents have knowledge of their actions because they are willing. Since God is the Creator and more perfect, He must have knowledge even more so.
This is a form of qiyas al-awla (superior analogy).
3
Analyze the exhaustive division
Ibn Taymiyya divides the property that makes will imply knowledge: it is either specific to humans or common to God and humans. The specific is false (would imply imperfection in God), so the common is determined.
This is a disjunctive syllogism: either A or B; not A; therefore B.
4
Examine the objection
The logicians argue that when premises are exhaustive (qism hasir), the cause might be the totality or a part that does not apply to the branch, invalidating the analogy.
The objection targets the validity of qiyas when the 'illa is complex.
5
Evaluate the response
Ibn Taymiyya concedes this is possible in some forms, like probable matters in fiqh. This shows qiyas is still valid, as the possibility does not negate the general method.
The example from fiqh grounds the logical discussion in Islamic jurisprudence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثالث - تمثيل استلزام الإرادة العلم. إذا قيل: الفاعل منا مريد وهو متصوّر لما يفعله، فالخالق أَوْلى أن يعلم ما خَلَق. أو قيل: إذا كان الفعل الاختياري فينا مشروطاً بالعلم، فهو في حق الخالق أولى، لأن ما به استلزمت الإرادة العلم إما أن يختص بالعبد، أو يكون مشتركاً، والأول باطل، فتعيّن الثاني. لأن استلزام الإرادة العلم كمال للفاعل، لا نقص فيه، و«الواجب» أحق بالكمال الذي لا نقص فيه مِن الممكن المخلوق. فإذا كان العبد يعلم ما يفعل فالباري أولى أن يعلم ما يفعل. ... ثم قال: وأما قولهم: «إذا انحصرت الأقسام فمن الجائز أن يكون معللاً بالمجموع، أو بالبعض الذي لا تحقق له في الفرع»، فيقال: هذا ممكن في بعض الصور، كالمسائل الظنية من الفقه.
Third Station - Representation of the Implication of Will for Knowledge. If it is said: The agent from among us is willing and he has a concept of what he does, then the Creator is more worthy that He knows what He created. Or it is said: If voluntary action in us is conditioned by knowledge, then it is more worthy in the case of the Creator, because that by which will implies knowledge is either specific to the servant or common, and the first is false, so the second is determined. Because the implication of will for knowledge is a perfection for the agent, with no deficiency in it, and the Necessary (al-Wajib) is more entitled to perfection that has no deficiency than the contingent created being. So if the servant knows what he does, then the Creator is more worthy to know what he does. ... Then he said: As for their saying: 'If the divisions are exhaustive, it is possible that it be caused by the totality, or by some part that has no realization in the branch,' it is said: This is possible in some forms, like the probable matters of fiqh.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage is an exposition by Ibn Taymiyya on how logical reasoning (qiyas) can be used in theological matters, specifically to show that God's will implies His knowledge. The author presents two a fortiori arguments: (1) If human agents have knowledge of their actions because they are willing, then God, being the Creator, is more deserving of knowing what He creates. (2) If voluntary action in humans is conditioned by knowledge, then in God it is even more so, because the property that makes will imply knowledge is either particular to humans or common between God and humans. The particular cannot be, because that would make God less perfect. Since implying knowledge from will is a perfection, God must have it more. Then the author addresses an objection from logicians who claim that when premises are exhaustive, the cause ('illa) might be the totality or a part that does not apply to the 'branch' (far') in an analogy. Ibn Taymiyya responds that this is possible in some cases, such as probable matters (zanni) in fiqh, where the 'illa may be a combination or a part that is not fully realized. This illustrates that qiyas is valid even in such cases. The commentary avoids secular examples; the example here is from usul al-fiqh regarding probable matters.

Key Terms
استلزام الإرادة العلم
القسم الحاصر
المسائل الظنية

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Only the totality can be the cause.
D) The objection is invalid because divisions are never exhaustive.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya uses a fortiori and disjunctive reasoning to show God's will implies knowledge, and he defends qiyas by acknowledging its validity even when objections about exhaustive divisions arise in probable matters.

Refuting the Eternity of Celestial Spheres via Modus Tollens
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the argument that celestial spheres are eternal by pointing out the possibility of their non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct a modus tollens conditional argument in a kalām context, and distinguish between the impossibility of non-existence for the Necessary Being and for possible beings.

Core Idea
Possibility of non-existence disproves eternity of the spheres.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya uses a simple but powerful conditional argument to refute the claim that the celestial spheres are eternal. He shows that if the spheres were eternal, their non-existence would be impossible; but since proofs indicate that their non-existence is possible, they cannot be eternal. This is a textbook example of modus tollens. However, he also anticipates and refines the objection: that the impossibility of non-existence only applies to what is eternal by itself. Let's break down the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional claim
Find the antecedent (If P) and consequent (then Q) in the passage. Here: If the spheres were eternal (P), then their non-existence would be impossible (Q).
The Arabic phrase 'لو كانت الأفلاك قديمة لامتنع عدمها' expresses this conditional.
2
Identify the evidence against the consequent
Find the claim that denies the consequent (not Q). Ibn Taymiyya says 'but their non-existence is possible' (لكن عدمها ممكن) based on proofs. This is the denial of the consequent.
3
Apply modus tollens
Since the consequent is false (non-existence is possible, not impossible), the antecedent must be false: Therefore, the spheres are not eternal. This is the modus tollens inference.
Modus tollens: If P then Q; Not Q; Therefore not P.
4
Examine the refinement
Ibn Taymiyya anticipates an objection: the impossibility of non-existence only applies to what is eternal by itself (like God), not to what is an effect of another. He counters that even if the spheres are called eternal, they are still possible beings, so the argument holds.
This distinction is crucial: possibility of non-existence applies to all created/possible beings, whether 'eternal' as an effect or not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا قال القائل: لو كانت الأفلاك قديمة لامتنع عدمها، لكن عدمها ممكن بالأدلة الدالة عليه، فلا تكون قديمة، لم يجز أن يقال: بل القديم لا يجوز عدمه لعلة مختصة بالقديم بنفسه، دون ما كان معلولاً لغيره، فالأفلاك، وإن قيل هي قديمة، فهي ممكنة.
If one says: If the celestial spheres were eternal, their non-existence would be impossible, but their non-existence is possible by the proofs indicating it, so they are not eternal, it cannot be said: Rather, the eternal is not permissible to cease due to a cause specific to the eternal by itself, not to what is an effect of another. So the spheres, even if they are said to be eternal, are possible.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that the celestial spheres (al-aflāk) are eternal. Ibn Taymiyya presents a conditional argument (modus tollens): If the spheres were eternal, then their non-existence would be impossible. But their non-existence is possible, as shown by proofs. Therefore, they are not eternal. He then anticipates an objection: that the impossibility of cessation applies only to what is eternal by itself (like God), not to what is an effect of another. He counters that even if the spheres are claimed to be eternal (as effects), they are still possible (mumkina), meaning they could cease to exist. This undermines the argument for their eternity. The passage is a typical kalām argument against the eternity of the world (qidam al-ʿālam).

Key Terms
أفلاك
ممكن
ممتنع

D) Hypothetical syllogism

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the eternity of celestial spheres by applying modus tollens: if eternal, then non-existence impossible; but non-existence is possible; therefore not eternal.

Types of Negation in Sibr and Taqsim
Author explains the two types of negation when enumerating causes via sibr and taqsim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between case-specific negation and absolute negation in the enumeration of causes via sibr and taqsim, and explain why this distinction matters for the strength of causal inference in usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
In sibr and taqsim, not all negations are equal: case-specific negation requires a separate negator, while absolute negation is stronger.

In the method of sibr and taqsim (enumeration and elimination) used by jurists to identify the effective cause ('illa), one enumerates all possible causes and eliminates all but one through negation. But Ibn Taymiyya points out a crucial distinction: the negation may be limited to the original case or it may be absolute. Understanding this distinction is essential for evaluating the strength of causal reasoning in Islamic jurisprudence. This lesson unpacks that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the context of sibr and taqsim
Recall that sibr and taqsim is a method for identifying the 'illa by listing all possible causes and negating all but one. The passage discusses the nature of that negation.
In usul al-fiqh, this method is used to confirm a cause for analogical reasoning (qiyas).
2
Distinguish the two types of negation
The passage says negation can be either: (1) negating that the cause operates in the original case only, or (2) negating causation absolutely (i.e., it is not a cause at all). The first is limited, the second universal.
The original case is the case from which the ruling originates (e.g., wine for prohibition).
3
Analyze the logical consequence of each type
If negation is only for the original case, you still need a separate proof (negator) to show why the cause is not operative there. If negation is absolute, it automatically covers the original case and all others, so no separate negator is needed.
This affects the certainty of the conclusion: absolute negation yields stronger inference.
4
Apply to an example from fiqh
Suppose a jurist suggests that intoxication is the cause of prohibition in wine. If the negation of other causes is only for wine (original case), then it doesn't automatically rule out those causes for other substances like nabidh. The jurist would need additional proof. But if the negation is absolute, then those causes are eliminated universally.
This distinction helps in evaluating the validity of qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكلام فيما إذا حصرت أقسام العلة، ونُفي التعليل عن كل منها إلا واحد. والنفي قد يكون لنفي التعليل بها في الأصل، وقد يكون لنفي التعليل مطلقاً. فالأول يحتاج معه إلى النافي في تلك الصورة، وأما الثاني فهو يتناول النفي في تلك الصورة وغيرها.
And the discussion concerns when the divisions of the cause are enumerated, and the causation is negated from all except one. And the negation may be for negating the causation by it in the original [case], and it may be for negating the causation absolutely. So the first requires with it the negator in that particular instance, and as for the second, it encompasses negation in that instance and others.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage discusses the method of sibr and taqsim (complete enumeration and elimination) used in determining the effective cause ('illa) in juristic reasoning. The author clarifies that when one enumerates all possible causes and then negates all but one, the negation can be of two types: (1) Negating that the cause is operative in the original case only (i.e., it is not the cause for that specific instance), but it could still be a cause in other cases. (2) Negating causation absolutely, meaning that the alleged cause is not a cause in any case, including the original. The first type requires a separate negator (a proof that it is not the cause in that specific case), while the second type covers negation universally. This distinction is important because the strength of the conclusion depends on the type of negation: absolute negation yields a stronger inference than case-specific negation. For example, in juristic reasoning, if one negates that drunkenness is the cause of prohibition in wine (the original) because it is not present in nabidh, but then affirms it as the cause in nabidh through qiyas, the negation must be absolute to be valid.

Key Terms
السبر والتقسيم
العلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) No further proof is needed; the negation covers all cases.
B) A separate negator is needed to show the cause is not operative in the original case.
C) The cause must be absolutely negated for all cases.
D) The original ruling must be abrogated.

Key Takeaway
The strength of the causal inference in sibr and taqsim depends on whether the negation of alternative causes is case-specific or absolute; absolute negation yields a more certain conclusion.

Explain using the two types of negation.

The Certainty of Analogical Reasoning
Author defends analogical reasoning as certain and necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply Ibn Taymiyya's method of eliminating differences (ilgha' al-fariq) to construct a certain analogical argument and evaluate its epistemic strength.

Core Idea
Analogy achieves certainty when differences are eliminated and similarity is rationally known.

The author of this passage defends analogical reasoning against the charge that it is unnecessary. He argues that examples can be not only helpful but also necessary for establishing the cause (ta'leel) and can themselves yield certainty when based on the method of ilgha' al-fariq. In this lesson, we will examine how ilgha' al-fariq works and see its application in the examples of blackness and motion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's position
Locate the statement being refuted: 'إنه يستغني عن التمثيل' (it dispenses with the example). This is the claim that analogical examples are dispensable.
2
Extract the three benefits of tamthil
The author lists three benefits: (1) clarification and conceiving a parallel, (2) facilitating proof of the cause, and (3) potential for certainty. Focus on the third.
3
Understand ilgha' al-fariq
The key method for certain analogy is ilgha' al-fariq: eliminating the difference between the original and branch. This relies on the principle 'equal to equal is equal' (مساوي المساوي مساو). It is a rational judgment that two things are perfectly similar with respect to the ruling.
4
Analyze the blackness example
The author says: if one particular blackness is an accident needing a substrate, then all blacknesses are likewise, and by extension all colors are likewise. This is not just enumeration; it is based on the nature of accidenthood.
5
Recognize the role of reason and revelation
Knowledge of similarity may come from reason (e.g., blackness example) or from revelation. When the similarity is rationally known, the analogy is certain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله : إنه يستغني عن التمثيل»، فيقال: نعم، والتمثيل في مثل هذا يذكر الإيضاح، وليتصوّر للفرع نظير، لأن الكلي إنما وجوده كلي في الذهن لا في الخارج، فإذا عُرف تحققه في الخارج كان أيسر لوجود نظيره. ولأن المثال قد يكون ميسِّراً لإثبات التعليل بل قد لا يمكن بدونه وسائر ما تثبت به العلةُ من الدوران والمناسبة وغير ذلك إذا أخذ معه السبر والتقسيم أمكن كون «القياس» قطعياً. وأيضاً، فقد يكون قياس التمثيل» يقينياً في كذا، فإن الجمع بين الأصل والفرع كما يكون بإبداء الجامع يكون بإلغاء الفارق، وهو أن يُعلم أن هذا مثل هذا، لا يفترقان في مثل هذا الحكم، ومساوي المساوي مساو، والعلم بالمساواة والمماثلة مما قد يعلم بالعقل، كما يعلم بالسمع، فإذا عُلم حكم أحد المتماثلين علم أن الآخر مثله، لا سيما إذا كان الكلام فيما تجرَّد من المعقولات، مثل القول بأن شيئاً من السَّواد عَرَض مفتقر إلى المحل، فيقال : سائر أفراد السواد كذلك . بل ويقال : وسائر الألوان كذلك . وكذلك إذا قيل : إن حركة الكواكب تُحدث شيئاً بعد شيء. قيل: وسائر الحركات كذلك. وبالجملة، فقد بينا أن كل «قياس» لا بد فيه من قضية كلية إيجابية، وبينا أن تلك خلاصة القضية لا بد أن يُعلم صدق كونها كلية . وكل ما به يعلم ذلك [به] يعلم أن الحكم لازم لذلك الكلي المشترك. فيمكن جعل ذلك الكلّي المشترك هو الجامع بين الأصل والفرع فكل قياس شمول» يمكن جعله قياس تمثيل. فإذا أفاد اليقين لم يزده التمثيل» إلا قوة.
And as for his statement: 'It dispenses with the example', it is said: Yes, and the example is mentioned in such cases for clarification, and so that a parallel may be conceived for the branch. Because the universal exists merely as universal in the mind, not in the external world. So if its realization in the external is known, it is easier for its parallel to exist. And because the example may facilitate proving the cause; indeed it may not be possible without it. And the rest of the means by which the cause is established, such as correlation (dawaran) and suitability (munasaba), when taken along with enumeration and division (sibr wa taqsim), make it possible for the syllogism to be certain. Also, analogical reasoning may be certain in some cases. For connecting the original and the branch can be done not only by presenting a common attribute (jami') but also by eliminating the difference (ilgha' al-fariq), which is to know that this is like that, they do not differ in such a ruling. And equal to equal is equal. Knowledge of equality and similarity may be known by reason, just as it is known by revelation. So when the ruling of one of two similars is known, it is known that the other is like it, especially when the discussion concerns abstract rational matters, such as the statement that a particular instance of blackness is an accident in need of a substrate, then it is said: all other individuals of blackness are likewise. Rather, it is said: all other colors are likewise. And similarly when it is said: the motion of the planets produces one thing after another, it is said: all other motions are likewise. In summary, we have shown that every syllogism must have a universal affirmative proposition, and we have shown that that proposition must be known to be true as universal. And everything by which that is known [thereby] it is known that the ruling is necessary for that common universal. So it is possible to make that common universal the common attribute between the original and the branch. Thus every deductive syllogism (qiyas shumul) can be made into an analogical syllogism (qiyas tamthil). So if it yields certainty, the analogy only increases its strength.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage is a defense of analogical reasoning (tamthil) against the claim that it is dispensable. The author makes several points. First, examples are useful for clarification and for conceiving a parallel for the branch case. This is because universals exist only in the mind; seeing an example in the external world helps grasp the universal. Second, an example may be necessary for proving the cause; indeed, the standard methods of establishing the cause (like dawaran, munasaba, sibr wa taqsim) may require an example to achieve certainty. Third, analogical reasoning can itself be certain when the similarity is known through reason or revelation. The author gives two intellectual examples: (1) Once it is known that a particular blackness is an accident needing a substrate, all instances of blackness are likewise, and by induction, all colors (which are accidents) are likewise. (2) The motion of planets being temporally successive implies that all motions are temporally successive. This shows that analogical reasoning, when based on elimination of differences (ilgha' al-fariq) rather than mere common attribute, can yield certainty. Finally, the author summarizes that every deductive syllogism (qiyas shumul) requires a universal affirmative premise, and that universal premise can be the common attribute linking the original and branch. Hence, every deductive syllogism can be reformulated as an analogy, and vice versa. Analogical reasoning does not weaken certainty; it strengthens it when the universal premise is known with certainty.

Key Terms
قياس التمثيل
إلغاء الفارق
قياس الشمول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) By always converting it to a deductive syllogism.
D) By using a single example from the original case.

Key Takeaway
Analogical reasoning can be certain when the similarity between original and branch is grounded in rational knowledge of equality, achieved through ilgha' al-fariq.

The Relationship Between Deductive and Analogical Syllogisms
Author argues for the interconvertibility of deductive and analogical syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to transform a deductive syllogism (qiyas shumul) into an analogical argument (qiyas tamthil) and explain how the universal premise serves as the common attribute.

Core Idea
Every deductive syllogism can be recast as an analogy, and vice versa.

The author concludes his discussion by asserting that every deductive syllogism can be made into an analogical syllogism, and if the deduction yields certainty, the analogy only strengthens it. This is a profound claim that bridges the gap between logical deduction and juristic analogy. In this lesson, we will learn how to identify the common attribute (jami') in a syllogism and convert it into analogical form.

Guided Path
1
Recall the structure of a categorical syllogism
A deductive syllogism (qiyas shumul) comprises a major premise (universal), a minor premise (particular), and a conclusion. The middle term connects the two premises.
2
Identify the universal affirmative premise
The author says every syllogism must have a universal affirmative premise. This premise states that all members of a category have a certain property. For example: 'Every intoxicant is haram.'
3
Treat the subject of the universal premise as the original (asl)
In the universal premise, the subject is the category (e.g., intoxicants) and the predicate is the ruling (haram). This can be seen as a statement about the asl: 'The asl (intoxicant) has the ruling (haram).'
4
Treat the minor premise as stating that the branch (far') shares the same attribute (jami')
The minor premise (e.g., 'Nabidh is an intoxicant') shows that the far' shares the attribute of being an intoxicant. Thus the jami' is the middle term (intoxicant).
5
Reformulate as an analogy
The analogical form: 'The asl (intoxicant) is haram because it is an intoxicant; the far' (nabidh) is intoxicant; therefore nabidh is haram.' This is logically equivalent to the deductive syllogism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله : إنه يستغني عن التمثيل»، فيقال: نعم، والتمثيل في مثل هذا يذكر الإيضاح، وليتصوّر للفرع نظير، لأن الكلي إنما وجوده كلي في الذهن لا في الخارج، فإذا عُرف تحققه في الخارج كان أيسر لوجود نظيره. ولأن المثال قد يكون ميسِّراً لإثبات التعليل بل قد لا يمكن بدونه وسائر ما تثبت به العلةُ من الدوران والمناسبة وغير ذلك إذا أخذ معه السبر والتقسيم أمكن كون «القياس» قطعياً. وأيضاً، فقد يكون قياس التمثيل» يقينياً في كذا، فإن الجمع بين الأصل والفرع كما يكون بإبداء الجامع يكون بإلغاء الفارق، وهو أن يُعلم أن هذا مثل هذا، لا يفترقان في مثل هذا الحكم، ومساوي المساوي مساو، والعلم بالمساواة والمماثلة مما قد يعلم بالعقل، كما يعلم بالسمع، فإذا عُلم حكم أحد المتماثلين علم أن الآخر مثله، لا سيما إذا كان الكلام فيما تجرَّد من المعقولات، مثل القول بأن شيئاً من السَّواد عَرَض مفتقر إلى المحل، فيقال : سائر أفراد السواد كذلك . بل ويقال : وسائر الألوان كذلك . وكذلك إذا قيل : إن حركة الكواكب تُحدث شيئاً بعد شيء. قيل: وسائر الحركات كذلك. وبالجملة، فقد بينا أن كل «قياس» لا بد فيه من قضية كلية إيجابية، وبينا أن تلك خلاصة القضية لا بد أن يُعلم صدق كونها كلية . وكل ما به يعلم ذلك [به] يعلم أن الحكم لازم لذلك الكلي المشترك. فيمكن جعل ذلك الكلّي المشترك هو الجامع بين الأصل والفرع فكل قياس شمول» يمكن جعله قياس تمثيل. فإذا أفاد اليقين لم يزده التمثيل» إلا قوة.
And as for his statement: 'It dispenses with the example', it is said: Yes, and the example is mentioned in such cases for clarification, and so that a parallel may be conceived for the branch. Because the universal exists merely as universal in the mind, not in the external world. So if its realization in the external is known, it is easier for its parallel to exist. And because the example may facilitate proving the cause; indeed it may not be possible without it. And the rest of the means by which the cause is established, such as correlation (dawaran) and suitability (munasaba), when taken along with enumeration and division (sibr wa taqsim), make it possible for the syllogism to be certain. Also, analogical reasoning may be certain in some cases. For connecting the original and the branch can be done not only by presenting a common attribute (jami') but also by eliminating the difference (ilgha' al-fariq), which is to know that this is like that, they do not differ in such a ruling. And equal to equal is equal. Knowledge of equality and similarity may be known by reason, just as it is known by revelation. So when the ruling of one of two similars is known, it is known that the other is like it, especially when the discussion concerns abstract rational matters, such as the statement that a particular instance of blackness is an accident in need of a substrate, then it is said: all other individuals of blackness are likewise. Rather, it is said: all other colors are likewise. And similarly when it is said: the motion of the planets produces one thing after another, it is said: all other motions are likewise. In summary, we have shown that every syllogism must have a universal affirmative proposition, and we have shown that that proposition must be known to be true as universal. And everything by which that is known [thereby] it is known that the ruling is necessary for that common universal. So it is possible to make that common universal the common attribute between the original and the branch. Thus every deductive syllogism (qiyas shumul) can be made into an analogical syllogism (qiyas tamthil). So if it yields certainty, the analogy only increases its strength.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage is a defense of analogical reasoning (tamthil) against the claim that it is dispensable. The author makes several points. First, examples are useful for clarification and for conceiving a parallel for the branch case. This is because universals exist only in the mind; seeing an example in the external world helps grasp the universal. Second, an example may be necessary for proving the cause; indeed, the standard methods of establishing the cause (like dawaran, munasaba, sibr wa taqsim) may require an example to achieve certainty. Third, analogical reasoning can itself be certain when the similarity is known through reason or revelation. The author gives two intellectual examples: (1) Once it is known that a particular blackness is an accident needing a substrate, all instances of blackness are likewise, and by induction, all colors (which are accidents) are likewise. (2) The motion of planets being temporally successive implies that all motions are temporally successive. This shows that analogical reasoning, when based on elimination of differences (ilgha' al-fariq) rather than mere common attribute, can yield certainty. Finally, the author summarizes that every deductive syllogism (qiyas shumul) requires a universal affirmative premise, and that universal premise can be the common attribute linking the original and branch. Hence, every deductive syllogism can be reformulated as an analogy, and vice versa. Analogical reasoning does not weaken certainty; it strengthens it when the universal premise is known with certainty.

Key Terms

قياس الشمول
الجامع

Choosing Between Deductive Syllogism and Analogy
Author specifies condition for preferring deductive syllogism over analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to decide when to use deductive syllogism versus analogical reasoning in Islamic legal argumentation based on the availability of infallible textual premises.

Core Idea
Revealed universal premises call for deductive syllogism; otherwise analogy may serve better.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya provides a practical rule for choosing between two types of reasoning: deductive syllogism (qiyas shumul) and analogical reasoning (qiyas tamthil). He argues that the decision depends on whether one of the premises—especially the major premise—is known from an infallible text (nass ma'sum) or consensus. This lesson will help you apply his criterion to real juristic cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two reasoning types
Distinguish deductive syllogism (qiyas shumul), which uses a universal premise and a particular premise to reach a conclusion, from analogical reasoning (qiyas tamthil), which extends a ruling from an original case (asl) to a new case (far') based on a shared effective cause ('illah).
Deductive syllogism is also called syllogism of inclusion.
2
Locate the condition for preferring deduction
Notice that the author states: 'When one of the two premises is known from an infallible text, then using the deductive syllogism is more appropriate.' Identify the conditional structure: if a premise is infallibly known, deduction is better.
The infallible text includes the Qur'an, Sunnah, and consensus of the community.
3
Understand why analogy may be easier
The author explains that analogical reasoning can be turned into a deductive syllogism, but proving the universal premise (especially the major) may be difficult. When the universal is not revealed, establishing the original case (asl) is often easier, making analogy more helpful for proof.
Analogy starts from a specific known case, which may be more accessible than a universal statement.
4
Apply the condition to a scenario
Imagine you have a revealed universal such as 'All intoxicants are forbidden.' If you encounter a new substance, you can directly use deductive syllogism: major premise from revelation, minor premise that the substance is an intoxicant. If no such universal is revealed, analogy to a known intoxicant (e.g., wine) becomes more appropriate.
The passage stresses that the resulting proof remains religious (shar'i), not purely rational.
5
Recognize the nature of the proof
Note that even when using deductive syllogism with a revealed premise, the argument is still shar'i because it relies on revelation or consensus, not pure reason. This aligns with Ibn Taymiyya's broader critique of logicians who restrict certainty to rational premises.
This step connects to the rejection of logicians' claim that only rational demonstration yields certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا علمت إحدى المقدمتين بالنص المعصوم فاستعمال «الشمول» أولى. وقياس التمثيل يمكن جعله قياس شمول»، لكن قد يكون بيان صحته محتاجاً إلى بيان إحدى مقدمتيه، لا سيما الكبرى، فإنها هي في الغالب التي تحتاج إلى البيان . وإذا كان كذلك «الأصل» المقيس عليه أولاً أسهل في البيان فـ «قياس التمثيل» أعونُ على البيان إلا إذا كانت القضية الكلية معلومة بنص المعصوم فهنا يكون الاستدلال بها أَوْلى من الاستدلال بـ «قياس التمثيل». لكن الدليل هنا يكون شرعياً لم تعلم إحدى مقدمتيه إلا بالنص المعصوم أو الإجماع المعصوم لم تعلم بمجرد العقل.
When one of the two premises is known from an infallible text, then using the deductive syllogism (shumul) is more appropriate. And analogical reasoning can be turned into a deductive syllogism, but sometimes proving its correctness requires proving one of its two premises, especially the major premise, for it is usually the one that needs proof. And if that is the case, the original (asl) analogized from is easier to prove, so analogical reasoning is more helpful for proof, except when the universal proposition is known from an infallible text. In that case, reasoning by that premise is more appropriate than reasoning by analogical reasoning. However, the evidence here is religious (shar'i); one of its premises is not known except by infallible text or infallible consensus (ijma'), not known by pure reason.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage provides a practical guideline for choosing between deductive syllogism (qiyas shumul) and analogical reasoning (qiyas tamthil) in juristic contexts. The author states that if one of the premises (especially the major premise) is known from an infallible source (masum), such as the Qur'an or Sunnah, then it is better to use the deductive syllogism directly, citing the revealed premise. However, if that premise is not known from revelation, analogical reasoning may be more useful because it starts from the original case (asl) which is easier to establish, and then proceeds to the branch. But the author emphasizes that when a universal premise is revealed, it should be used directly rather than going through analogy. Nonetheless, the resulting argument is still a religious (shar'i) proof, not a purely rational one, because one of its premises depends on revelation or consensus. This distinction is key in Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the logicians who restrict demonstration (burhan) to purely rational premises.

The Syllogism

Interchangeability of Categorical and Analogical Syllogisms
Ibn Taymiyya explains the interchangeability of categorical and analogical syllogisms and when to prefer the categorical if a premise is revealed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert a categorical syllogism into an analogical one and vice versa, and determine when to prefer the categorical form when a premise is from an infallible text.

Core Idea
Categorical and analogical syllogisms are interchangeable; revelation-tested universals prefer the categorical form.

Ibn Taymiyya argues that every categorical syllogism (qiyas al-shumul) can be transformed into an analogical syllogism (qiyas al-tamthil) and vice versa. The choice between them depends on whether a universal premise is directly known from an infallible text or needs to be established through analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the universal major premise
In a categorical syllogism, locate the universal affirmative proposition that serves as the major premise. For example: 'Every intoxicant is haram' is a universal.
2
Recognize the universal as the common factor
Ibn Taymiyya states that this universal can serve as the 'jami' (common factor) between the original and branch in analogy. So the universal is the link.
3
Determine the source of the universal premise
Check if the universal is known directly from an infallible text (nass ma'sum). If so, the categorical form is preferred. If not, analogy may be used to establish the universal from a specific case.
The passage says: 'if one premise is from infallible text, using shumul is more appropriate'.
4
Practice conversion
Take a categorical syllogism and express it as an analogy: identify the original (asl) and branch (far') that share the universal. Then convert back.
Example: categorical: 'Every intoxicant is haram, khamr is intoxicant → khamr is haram'. Analogical: 'Khamr is intoxicant and haram; nabidh is intoxicant → nabidh is haram'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثالث - متى يستحسن استعمال «قياس الشمول»؟ مفتقر إلى المحل ، فيقال : سائر أفراد السواد كذلك . بل ويقال : وسائر الألوان كذلك . وكذلك إذا قيل : إن حركة الكواكب تحدث شيئاً بعد شيء. قيل: وسائر الحركات كذلك . وبالجملة، فقد بينا أن كل «قياس» لا بد فيه من قضية كلية إيجابية، وبينا أن تلك خلاصة القضية لا بد أن يُعلم صدق كونها كلية . وكل ما به يعلم ذلك [به] (١) يعلم أن الحكم لازم لذلك الكلي المشترك. فيمكن جعل ذلك الكلّي المشترك هو الجامع بين الأصل والفرع فكل قياس شمول» يمكن جعله قياس تمثيل. فإذا أفاد اليقين لم يزده التمثيل» إلا قوة . إذا علمت إحدى المقدمتين بالنص المعصوم فاستعمال «الشمول» أولى و قياس التمثيل يمكن جعله قياس شمول»، لكن قد يكون بيان صحته محتاجاً إلى بيان إحدى مقدمتيه، لا سيما الكبرى، فإنها هي في الغالب التي تحتاج إلى البيان . وإذا كان كذلك «الأصل» المقيس عليه أولاً أسهل في البيان فـ «قياس التمثيل» أعونُ على البيان إلا إذا كانت القضية الكلية معلومة بنص المعصوم فهنا يكون الاستدلال بها أَوْلى من الاستدلال بـ «قياس التمثيل». لكن الدليل هنا يكون شرعياً لم تعلم إحدى مقدمتيه إلا بالنص المعصوم أو الإجماع المعصوم لم تعلم بمجرد العقل. وهم والكلام مع من يجعل موادَّ «البرهان» القضايا الكلية المعلومة بدون قول المعصوم بل قد يسمون القياس المستدل على إحدى مقدماته بقول المعصوم من «الخطابي» و «الجدلي»، لأنهم يجعلون تلك القضايا من «المسلمات» و «المقبولات»، لا من البرهانيات اليقينيات. هذا البحث وهذا أيضاً من ضلالهم. فإن القضية اليقينية : ما عُلم أنها حق علماً يقينيا». فإذا علم بدليل قطعي أن المعصوم لا يقول إلا حقا، وعُلم بالضرورة أنه قضى بهذه القضية النبوية غير الكلية، كما قضى بـ أن الله بكل شيء عليم ، و أن الله خالق كل شيء»، و «أنه لا نبي بعده، ونحو ذلك من القضايا الخبرية التي عُلم بالضرورة أن المعصوم أخبر بها عامة كلية، كان هذا من أحسن الطرق في حصول اليقين. وهذا الطريق لا يدخل في قياسهم البرهاني، ولكن هذا لما كان مبنيا على مقدمات سمعية لم نقررها في هذا الموضع لم نحتج به عليهم بل احتججنا عليهم بما يسلمونه . وما بيناه بمجرد العقل من أن قولهم العلم الكسبة لا تحصل إلا بقياسهم البرهاني قول باطل، بل هو من أبطل الأباطيل . هذا في جانب النفي
The third station: When is it good to use the syllogism of inclusion? It is dependent on the subject, so it is said: 'All individuals of blackness are like that.' Rather it is said: 'And all colors are like that.' And likewise if it is said: 'The motion of the planets produces things one after another.' It is said: 'And all motions are like that.' In summary, we have shown that every syllogism necessarily requires a universal affirmative proposition, and we have shown that the essence of that proposition must be known in its truth as being universal. And everything by which that is known [by it] it is known that the judgment is necessary for that common universal. So it is possible to make that common universal the common factor between the original and the branch. So every syllogism of inclusion can be made into a syllogism of analogy. And if it yields certainty, analogy does not increase it except in strength. If one of the two premises is known from an infallible text, then using 'al-shumul' is more appropriate. And the syllogism of analogy can be made into a syllogism of inclusion, but verifying its correctness may require establishing one of its two premises, especially the major premise, for it is usually the one that needs explanation. And if that is the case, the 'original' that is the basis for analogy is easier to explain, so the 'syllogism of analogy' is more helpful for explanation, except when the universal proposition is known from an infallible text; then making an inference by it is more appropriate than inference by 'syllogism of analogy'. But the proof here would be scriptural, one of its two premises being known only by the infallible text or the infallible consensus, not known by mere reason. A delusion: And the discussion with those who make the materials of 'burhan' the universal propositions known without the saying of the infallible; rather they may call the syllogism whose one premise is inferred from the saying of the infallible as belonging to 'rhetorical' and 'dialectical' [syllogisms], because they make those propositions from the 'accepted' and 'acknowledged', not from the certain demonstrative ones. This discussion. And this is also from their misguidance. For the certain proposition is what is known to be true with certain knowledge. So if it is known by a definite proof that the infallible says only the truth, and it is known necessarily that he has judged by this prophetic proposition, not as a universal, as he judged that 'God is Knower of everything', and 'God is Creator of everything', and 'there is no prophet after him', and similar informative propositions which are known necessarily that the infallible informed of them as general and universal, this would be one of the best ways to obtain certainty. And this way is not included in their demonstrative syllogism, but because this is based on scriptural premises which we have not established in this place, we have not argued against them using it, rather we have argued against them using what they accept. And what we have explained by mere reason: that their statement that 'acquired sciences are only obtained through their demonstrative syllogism' is a false statement; rather it is among the most false of falsehoods. This is on the negative side.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage from Ibn Taymiyya's 'Radd al-Mantiqiyyin' constitutes the third station (maqam) of his critique, where he investigates when it is appropriate to use the 'syllogism of inclusion' (qiyas al-shumul) — the categorical syllogism with a universal major premise. He begins by establishing that every categorical syllogism necessarily requires a universal affirmative proposition whose universal truth must be known. This universal can serve as the common factor (jami') between the original (asl) and the branch (far') in analogical reasoning, proving that all categorical syllogisms can be transformed into analogical ones (qiyas al-tamthil). If the categorical yields certainty, analogy does not add to it except in strength. Then he introduces a condition: if one premise is known from an infallible text (nass ma'sum), the categorical form is preferable because it directly applies the known universal. However, analogical reasoning may be more helpful for establishing the universal premise, especially the major premise, since it starts from an easier original case. But if the universal is itself directly known from revelation, then inference by that universal is better. The proof then becomes scriptural (shar'i), based on the infallible text or consensus, not on pure reason. Ibn Taymiyya then criticizes the logicians who exclude such scriptural premises from their demonstrative syllogism (burhan), labeling them as rhetorical or dialectical. He calls this a misguidance. He argues that if it is known with certainty that the infallible (the Prophet only speaks truth, and that he delivered universal propositions like 'God is Knower of everything' etc., these are certain and should be considered as such. Although this path (revelation) is not part of their burhan because it relies on scriptural premises, Ibn Taymiyya notes that he only argued against them using premises they accept. He concludes that their claim that all acquired knowledge must come through their demonstrative syllogism is false, indeed the most false of falsehoods. This ends the negative aspect of his critique.

Key Terms
قياس الشمول
قياس التمثيل
النص المعصوم

Key Takeaway
Categorical and analogical syllogisms are interchangeable; the preferred form depends on whether the universal premise is directly given by revelation or needs to be established from a particular case.

The Syllogism

Ibn Taymiyya's Defense of Revealed Premises as Certain
Ibn Taymiyya argues that revealed universal propositions are certain and belong to demonstrative syllogism, contrary to the logicians' classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that universal propositions from infallible texts qualify as certain (yaqini) and thus belong in demonstrative syllogism, refuting the logicians' exclusion.

Core Idea
Revealed universal propositions, if known with certainty, are valid premises for demonstrative syllogism.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya attacks the logicians who dismiss scriptural premises as merely rhetorical or dialectical. He argues that if we have definitive proof that the infallible speaker (the Prophet) only speaks truth, and he has delivered universal propositions, those propositions are certain and should be accepted in demonstrative reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' position
The logicians claim that premises derived from infallible texts belong to rhetorical or dialectical syllogisms, not to burhan (demonstration), because they are not known by pure reason.
2
State Ibn Taymiyya's definition of certain proposition
He defines a certain proposition as what is known to be true with certain knowledge, regardless of its source. The key is the epistemic certainty, not the source.
‘القضية اليقينية : ما عُلم أنها حق علماً يقينيا’.
3
Trace the epistemic chain for revealed universals
If we know with a definitive proof that the infallible (the Prophet) only speaks truth, and we know necessarily that he has delivered a universal proposition (like ‘God is Knower of everything’), then that proposition is certain.
Ibn Taymiyya provides examples: ‘أن الله بكل شيء عليم’, ‘أن الله خالق كل شيء’, ‘أنه لا نبي بعده’.
4
Evaluate the logicians' exclusion as misguided
Ibn Taymiyya concludes that the logicians' exclusion of such premises from burhan is a misguidance (dalal). Certainty can be obtained through revelation, and their claim that all acquired knowledge must come through their burhan is false.

المقام الثالث - متى يستحسن استعمال «قياس الشمول»؟ مفتقر إلى المحل ، فيقال : سائر أفراد السواد كذلك . بل ويقال : وسائر الألوان كذلك . وكذلك إذا قيل : إن حركة الكواكب تحدث شيئاً بعد شيء. قيل: وسائر الحركات كذلك . وبالجملة، فقد بينا أن كل «قياس» لا بد فيه من قضية كلية إيجابية، وبينا أن تلك خلاصة القضية لا بد أن يُعلم صدق كونها كلية . وكل ما به يعلم ذلك [به] (١) يعلم أن الحكم لازم لذلك الكلي المشترك. فيمكن جعل ذلك الكلّي المشترك هو الجامع بين الأصل والفرع فكل قياس شمول» يمكن جعله قياس تمثيل. فإذا أفاد اليقين لم يزده التمثيل» إلا قوة . إذا علمت إحدى المقدمتين بالنص المعصوم فاستعمال «الشمول» أولى و قياس التمثيل يمكن جعله قياس شمول»، لكن قد يكون بيان صحته محتاجاً إلى بيان إحدى مقدمتيه، لا سيما الكبرى، فإنها هي في الغالب التي تحتاج إلى البيان . وإذا كان كذلك «الأصل» المقيس عليه أولاً أسهل في البيان فـ «قياس التمثيل» أعونُ على البيان إلا إذا كانت القضية الكلية معلومة بنص المعصوم فهنا يكون الاستدلال بها أَوْلى من الاستدلال بـ «قياس التمثيل». لكن الدليل هنا يكون شرعياً لم تعلم إحدى مقدمتيه إلا بالنص المعصوم أو الإجماع المعصوم لم تعلم بمجرد العقل. وهم والكلام مع من يجعل موادَّ «البرهان» القضايا الكلية المعلومة بدون قول المعصوم بل قد يسمون القياس المستدل على إحدى مقدماته بقول المعصوم من «الخطابي» و «الجدلي»، لأنهم يجعلون تلك القضايا من «المسلمات» و «المقبولات»، لا من البرهانيات اليقينيات. هذا البحث وهذا أيضاً من ضلالهم. فإن القضية اليقينية : ما عُلم أنها حق علماً يقينيا». فإذا علم بدليل قطعي أن المعصوم لا يقول إلا حقا، وعُلم بالضرورة أنه قضى بهذه القضية النبوية غير الكلية، كما قضى بـ أن الله بكل شيء عليم ، و أن الله خالق كل شيء»، و «أنه لا نبي بعده، ونحو ذلك من القضايا الخبرية التي عُلم بالضرورة أن المعصوم أخبر بها عامة كلية، كان هذا من أحسن الطرق في حصول اليقين. وهذا الطريق لا يدخل في قياسهم البرهاني، ولكن هذا لما كان مبنيا على مقدمات سمعية لم نقررها في هذا الموضع لم نحتج به عليهم بل احتججنا عليهم بما يسلمونه . وما بيناه بمجرد العقل من أن قولهم العلم الكسبة لا تحصل إلا بقياسهم البرهاني قول باطل، بل هو من أبطل الأباطيل . هذا في جانب النفي
The third station: When is it good to use the syllogism of inclusion? It is dependent on the subject, so it is said: 'All individuals of blackness are like that.' Rather it is said: 'And all colors are like that.' And likewise if it is said: 'The motion of the planets produces things one after another.' It is said: 'And all motions are like that.' In summary, we have shown that every syllogism necessarily requires a universal affirmative proposition, and we have shown that the essence of that proposition must be known in its truth as being universal. And everything by which that is known [by it] it is known that the judgment is necessary for that common universal. So it is possible to make that common universal the common factor between the original and the branch. So every syllogism of inclusion can be made into a syllogism of analogy. And if it yields certainty, analogy does not increase it except in strength. If one of the two premises is known from an infallible text, then using 'al-shumul' is more appropriate. And the syllogism of analogy can be made into a syllogism of inclusion, but verifying its correctness may require establishing one of its two premises, especially the major premise, for it is usually the one that needs explanation. And if that is the case, the 'original' that is the basis for analogy is easier to explain, so the 'syllogism of analogy' is more helpful for explanation, except when the universal proposition is known from an infallible text; then making an inference by it is more appropriate than inference by 'syllogism of analogy'. But the proof here would be scriptural, one of its two premises being known only by the infallible text or the infallible consensus, not known by mere reason. A delusion: And the discussion with those who make the materials of 'burhan' the universal propositions known without the saying of the infallible; rather they may call the syllogism whose one premise is inferred from the saying of the infallible as belonging to 'rhetorical' and 'dialectical' [syllogisms], because they make those propositions from the 'accepted' and 'acknowledged', not from the certain demonstrative ones. This discussion. And this is also from their misguidance. For the certain proposition is what is known to be true with certain knowledge. So if it is known by a definite proof that the infallible says only the truth, and it is known necessarily that he has judged by this prophetic proposition, not as a universal, as he judged that 'God is Knower of everything', and 'God is Creator of everything', and 'there is no prophet after him', and similar informative propositions which are known necessarily that the infallible informed of them as general and universal, this would be one of the best ways to obtain certainty. And this way is not included in their demonstrative syllogism, but because this is based on scriptural premises which we have not established in this place, we have not argued against them using it, rather we have argued against them using what they accept. And what we have explained by mere reason: that their statement that 'acquired sciences are only obtained through their demonstrative syllogism' is a false statement; rather it is among the most false of falsehoods. This is on the negative side.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage from Ibn Taymiyya's 'Radd al-Mantiqiyyin' constitutes the third station (maqam) of his critique, where he investigates when it is appropriate to use the 'syllogism of inclusion' (qiyas al-shumul) — the categorical syllogism with a universal major premise. He begins by establishing that every categorical syllogism necessarily requires a universal affirmative proposition whose universal truth must be known. This universal can serve as the common factor (jami') between the original (asl) and the branch (far') in analogical reasoning, proving that all categorical syllogisms can be transformed into analogical ones (qiyas al-tamthil). If the categorical yields certainty, analogy does not add to it except in strength. Then he introduces a condition: if one premise is known from an infallible text (nass ma'sum), the categorical form is preferable because it directly applies the known universal. However, analogical reasoning may be more helpful for establishing the universal premise, especially the major premise, since it starts from an easier original case. But if the universal is itself directly known from revelation, then inference by that universal is better. The proof then becomes scriptural (shar'i), based on the infallible text or consensus, not on pure reason. Ibn Taymiyya then criticizes the logicians who exclude such scriptural premises from their demonstrative syllogism (burhan), labeling them as rhetorical or dialectical. He calls this a misguidance. He argues that if it is known with certainty that the infallible (the Prophet only speaks truth, and that he delivered universal propositions like 'God is Knower of everything' etc., these are certain and should be considered as such. Although this path (revelation) is not part of their burhan because it relies on scriptural premises, Ibn Taymiyya notes that he only argued against them using premises they accept. He concludes that their claim that all acquired knowledge must come through their demonstrative syllogism is false, indeed the most false of falsehoods. This ends the negative aspect of his critique.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what is the defining characteristic of a certain proposition (al-qadiyya al-yaqiniyya)?
A) It is known by pure reason without any external source.
B) It is known to be true with certain knowledge, regardless of its source.
C) It must be a universal affirmative proposition.
D) It must be agreed upon by all logicians.

Key Takeaway
Certainty in premises is determined by epistemic assurance, not by source; revelation can provide certain universals suitable for demonstrative syllogism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using Ibn Taymiyya's reasoning, explain why the universal proposition 'Allah is Creator of everything' (from the Qur'an) qualifies as a certain premise for a demonstrative syllogism. Identify the epistemic steps: definitive proof of infallibility, necessary knowledge of the universal, and the conclusion of certainty.
?
Construct a short argument that refutes the logicians' claim that acquired sciences only come through their demonstrative syllogism, using the example of revelation as a source of certain universals.
